Tears and Triumph
The Red Page
The brilliance, the sparkling wit, and the
passionate, though apparently paradoxical,
sincerity of Bernard Shaw hall-marks Tears
and Triumph, by Dowell O’Reilly (printed
by Beatty, Richardson. Paling’s Buildings.
Sydney, publisher and price not stated), as
the most striking Australian publication of
the year. The intrusion of the name of
Shaw, necessary for the purpose of a world
standard, since we have nothing like , this
O’Reillism in Australia, may convey the
wrong impression that this fantasy in prose
is but an imitation or a parody of Bernard.
In Tears and Triumph there is wit equal to
Shaw's and audacity excelling Shaw’s; but
this work is unique in construction and in
style, its essence predominantly O’Reillian.
The plot is slight, the texture tough.
Outwardly it is but a romantic encounter
one blistering summer day between a man
and the eternal woman. The romance, it
is necessary to state, is entirely on his side.
For while at the sight of her he mentally
saw himself “matching his brains against
her coquetry—the black ink of his deliberate
falsehood against her gorgeous indifference
to truth,” yet his face seemed to interest
her. “not as a human document, but rather
as a draper’s advertisement or a butcher’s
bill—her gaze suggested arithmetic.”
Jt
The hero had come to look for “Elysium.”
This, according to a land-agent s deceptive
“litho” was a “waterside garden suburb, only
10 minutes from Bankstown station.” It
was a day of heat, and as the hero goes
along the bush track he treads indifferently
on innumerable red ants, and mutters mad
jingles chiming with his brutal footfall, such
as: “The poor little beggars must keep on
the run or they’ll blister the soles of their
feet. I am kinder than God, so I send ’em
to hell, where they’ll never complain of the
heat.”
He did not discover “Elysium,” but he dis-
covered the eternal woman in the scrub.
“With expressionless face, his laughter
bled inwardly*—a common experience with
the intellectual man when strutting his part
in the comedy of passion.” And it is this
internally bleeding laughter that gives the
book its high distinction. For his italicised
reflections, a running commentary on the
charmingly told woman’s pathetic romance,
range over the whole evolution of woman
and project themselves far into her future,'
and his philosophy explains and excuses, in-
deed, almost canonises, the suffragette. It
is the finest appeal on behalf of Woman that
this critic has hitherto met.
Here we catch a glimpse into the profound
and ever-widening gulf that sunders man
from woman. In passion she finds herself;
he loses himself. She rushes forward to
fulfil her purpose ; he turns back from his.
So passion is to her highest self a sacra-
ment: to his highest self a sacrilege.....
Woman must know that, from time imme-
morial, the one universal theme for man’s
laughter has been the one theme that, in her
very nature, she must ever hold sacred. Why
is this so? Because man knows that, as a
male, he has sunk beneath contempt—his
mind and soul alone count. And the eternal
woman knows that it is her glorified sex
that counts —her Motherhood, that is Life
itself.
And Woman! “What unspeakable eter-
nities must her sex have weltered through, to
emerge at last, so strangely different from
man !” And then we have the brilliant de-
scription of the evolution of woman, from
that far s,way time when she first began to
fidget. And between the acts of this great
drama we catch the trivial romance of the
woman v chatting of her own so much
greater’tragedy, as if she were Sitting in the
stalls, while behind the curtain you heard
the setting of a new scene. Thus she tells
her own story :
We had our first row when he tried to
kiss me—l slapped him—l was that inno-
cent. He said, “What’s that for?” I said,
“Never you mind!” He said. “Well, don’t
you do it again.” I said, “Just you try.”
He said. “That’s the limit—l’m off!” I said,
“Good riddance of bad rubbish” —I wasn't
going to let on I liked him, but, my word,
didn’t 1 cry that night”
d*
Then the curtain is rung up and the play
goes on.
So man and woman travelled together—
within the germinating “idea” of life—right
up through chaos—electrons —matter. All
that time they never had a difference; but,
of course, she wasn’t a woman then....
Then came the first wonder of organic bliss
—a united and perfectly contented Amoeba
—at least his half was—and life seemed one
long honeymoon. They broke off their chil-
dren as they wanted them, and, with the in-
creasing corpulency of years, found this a
most admirable provision, for they actually
retained their good figure, while raking in
baby-bonuses. But she wouldn’t let well
alone. She said that they had been keeping
company quite long enough—it didn’t matter
while out on the track, but now, approach-
ing settled districts, they had better get
“married.”... .At last she took up her bur-
den of motherhood, and set out on her jour-
ney ....
She first stood up—the child on her
breast. It was she who first saw the Stars.
“My dear,” she said, “I have only just
realised what a long way we still have to
go, and if we —that is, if baby is ever to get
there, I must give up a lot more for him.”
“Please yourself, darling.” He was now
an experienced married gorilla, and knew
how to avoid an argument.
“Of course,” she said with a brave little
smile, “I'll try to keep up with you, but baby
is so heavy, and the children drag at one’s
fur so—but I'll try.”....
Yet. all through those ages, she was doing
—in secret—a most awful thing. She was
unconsciously dislocating and distorting her
athletic body, that her child might have
ampler room for growth and nobler sources
of nourishment. She lengthened her trunk
and shortened her legs, and forced her lungs
to breathe upward. Her head ceased to ex-
pand. Her hands and feet —once powerful
as her mate’s—withered through eternities
of helpless brooding and nursing....
“Haven’t I a pretty little foot?” she said
one day to her mate, lie kissed it—but not
with tears, for he did not understand....
Then we learn how by sweeping out the
cave,
It took her exactly one minute and a half
to lay the foundations of civilisation. In
00 seconds she established the fact that en-
vironment —that shapes all lesser life—must
no longer shape her destiny. In fact, she
wasn’t going to have a destiny—so there!
....She had originated the idea of “home.”
More than that: she had established a
“midden” —say rubbish-box—for all univer-
sal laws, and other dirt and refuse that
might, in ages to come, offend her nose, or
revolt her womanhood.
So the story, or the tragedy, of woman
runs on. “Think! For an eternity I have
just been saying ‘Goo !’ to my eternal baby!”
And at last she tells man to go straight on
to God —“and tell Him I sent you.. ..1 shall
go on—my own way—with baby.” And
thus we come “from History-in-the-crucible
to Hysteria-in-Chrystabel,” and hence to the
stupendous scene, brilliantly and humor-
ously and dramatically pictured, where
Woman presents ' her ultimatum to the
Powers. Into the secret conclave of
diplomats came the Woman, “flung on
their table a scented ultimatum —
flounced into a vacant ,arm-chair and mys-
teriously silenced her crying infant.” And
the ultimatum, which, with the already sel-
fish abuse of quotation, we have to refrain
from printing here, should be emblazoned in
letters of gold over every Speaker’s chair,
and flaunted in banners at the head of every
army in the world. It begins : “To the Hor-
rid, Selfish. Savage, Silly Old Powers; I’m
sick of the whole pack of you !” and is signed
“Civilised Motherhood, R. 1.” But one final
quotation demands space here;—
If an eternity of sex-specialisation has re-
duced woman to what she is, what —in God’s
Name —will another eternity of ; sex. and
nothing but sex, reduce her to? A horror of
sex-incarnate —brainless, soulless, helpless—
something gross and leprous-white for ever
lying still- —an abomination disguised in silks
and flowers and perfumes—'mowing like an
idiot —a momentary delight to the depraved
male —a lasting insult to his mind and soul
—a pollution from which her little son must
be snatched —
Do we blame her cry of terror —her
blanched cheek—‘the passionate fury of her
refusal to follow any longer the old path?
J*
It has taken a writer in Australia, where
the suffragette was long ago vaccinated
with freedom, to write the finest exposition
of the soul of this remarkable movement, and
to write it with an astonishing wit, a genial
humor, rarely to be found flourishing on a
scientific basis of biology.
The Man Who Sees Fairies
His name is Maurice Hewlett, and he is
one of the few foremost novelists in Eng-
land. In Lore of Proserpine (Macmillan)
he gives us what one may call, as Hewlett
himself suggests, the Natural History of the
Preternatural. The fairies he sees are real
fairies, not jim-jams, which are' apparently
denizens only of the Commonwealth. If
Hewlett came to Australia we would in all
probability have an equally valuable book on
“The Anatomy and the Habits of the
Fantod.”
At the outset Hewlett remarks: —
I hope nobody will ask me whether the
things in this book are true, for it will then
be my humiliating duty to reply that I don’t
know. They seem to be so to me writing
them; they seemed to be so when they oc-
curred, and one of them occurred only two or
three years ago.
But that is exactly what any reader of
this book is entitled to ask. Is it true, or
merely the delightful fiction of a master of
fiction? And though Hewlett says he does
not know, he contrives to leave the impres-
sion—indeed, he strives very persistently to
leave the impression—that he does know.
The only alternatives he allows the reader
are, either that he possesses the power of self-
deception, or that he really saw a London
telegraph messenger who was a Greek god,
and saw a Cockney’s wife of no particular
morals floating through the air and dancing
with other fairies in a London park.
Hewlett’s first fairy he saw when he was
12 years old.
I must have been looking at him for some
time before I made out that he was there. ..
I presently and gradually did realise...a
shape, a creature, a thing of form and pres-
sure—-not a wraith, not, I am quite sure, a
trick of the senses.
Hewlett describes the figure with meticu-
lous detail. He was absent-mindedly choking
a rabbit; but, being a fairy, of course, there
was nothing shocking in that. Fairies, we
are informed, are without shame, being non-
human, and therefore not to be judged by
our moral standards.
The next was a Dryad. It is curious to
note that all the fairies Hewlett saw be-
longed to the Greek mythology. Either Eng-
land hasn’t any indigenous fairies, or all
fairies are Dagos. lie never met a Hebrew
spook or a Wesleyan jinnee. This Dryad
was preening herself in the sunlight—"a
radiant young female thing, fiercely favored,
smiling with a fierce joy, with a gleam of
fierce light in her narrowed eyes. .. .Her gar-
ment was thin and grey, and it clung to her
like a bark, seemed to grow upon her as a
creeping stone-weed grows.”
The next was Mrs. Ventris, of the London
slums, who was. also a prostitute. Hewlett
saw her often, leaning out of her window —
“a pale-faced, curling-papered, half-bodiced,
unwashed drab of a girl, who would have
had shame written across her for any-
one to read if she had not seemed of all
women I have ever seen the least shame-
faced.” Then, one night, Hewlett saw her at
her window:
She! Could this be she? It was so, in-
deed, but she was transfigured... She rayed
forth light. The moon shone full upon her,
and revealed her pure form from head to foot
swathed in filmy blue. Translucent web-
bery, whatever it was, it showed her beneath
it as bare as Venus was when she fared
forth unblemished from the sea. .. .Breath-
less I watched her. the drab of my daily ob-
servation, radiant now; then as I watched
she stretched out her arms and.... without
falter or fury launched herself into the air,
and dropt slowly down out of my sight.
Like a bird, Hewlett suggests. Mrs. Ven-
tris was, you see, a fairy. And he saw her
again one night in a London park, playing
games with a crowd of other fairies. “A
mad business under the cloudy moon. It
had a dreamlike element of riot and wild
triumph. I suppose I must have been there
for two or three hours, during all which
time their swift play was never altogether
stopped.” Then they melted away.
The case of the fairy telegraph boy, Quid-
nunc, is circumstantially related —too circum-
stantially for conviction. For here Hewlett
is not concerned solely with his own sight,
which may be defective, but with the evi-
dence of hundreds of other people.
Quidnunc was a disappearing telegraph
boy, who, in answer to your appeal—his
office hours were after midnight in a London
park—brought you, in the form of a telegram,
the news you desired ; and yet no one of the
immense number of people who had seen him
ever thought of identifying him by means of
their telegrams at the telegraph office.
Finally, a member of the aristocracy, a young
girl, disappeared with him. That is circum-
stantial enough, as Hewlett tells it. But
the fiction writer gives way to his artistic
desire to round the story off. So we have
the quite unnecessary final chapter. “A
curate of my acquaintance” is dragged in to
state that he found the vanished girl at
Pherae, in Greece, some years later, wearing
the costume of the peasants, and possessing
two or three young children, all fair and
golden-haired, while she was dark. She told
the curate that her husband was away on a
journey. And Hewlett adds the further un-
necessary touch. “It is odd, and perhaps it
is more than odd, that there was a famous
temple of Hermes in Pherae in former times.”
You see, the fiction-writer had to have his
artistic ending. Hermes—a modern tele-
graph boy—how aptly everything fits!
Jt
No objection can be taken to the story of a
certain Mr. Beckwith, who found and brought
home a fairy (with a gash in her throat),
since here Hewlett is not responsible for any-
thing but the telling. Beckwith, he states,
left a statement of this happening. Hew-
lett’s share is merely that of the sympathetic
historian. Beckwith kept his fairy—-un-
known to his wife T—in the dog kennel; the
dogs recognised her, and so did Beckwith’s
child. And though Beckwith was able to
keep the fairy a prisoner, owing to his dis-
covery that fairies, like slugs, will not cross
zinc, ultimately she, the dog and the child
disappeared. So, the story of the Fairy
Wife is related second-hand; though in both
cases one would like to verify Hewlett’s
rather ostentatiously paraded' references.
However, he returns to his own experiences
when he relates how he saw a male and a
female Oread at their fairy love-making, and
—strange coincidence —subsequently he saw
the female Oread with her child. And what are
we to make out of this remark, in reference
to the story of the wife “who suffered a fairy
union and b#re a fairy child’.'”
The fairy father was, so far as I can
make out, the indwelling spirit of a rose, and
the story is too painful and the detail in my
possession too exact for me to put it down
here. I was myself actually present, and in
the house, when the child was born. I wit-
nessed the anguish of the unfortunate hus-
band, who is now dead.
And, delightfully descending, in a foot-
note, to statistics, Hewlett gravely states
that “The number of fairy wives in England
alone is very considerable —over a quarter of
a million, I am told.”
An enchanting book, written by a master
of the whimsical. Looked upon as fiction,
the thing is gallantly done; as fact it is fact
expressed through a sensuous and poetic tem-
perament. Frankly we believe that Maurice
Hewlett, of whose impressionable youth he
gives us a convincing picture, believed that
he saw fairies; but, in the instances which
we have noticed his evidence is open to grave
doubt. He believes he saw fairies, but he
fails to make us believe that other people Saw
them. So any poetical drunk believes he
sees fantods, but unless we are similarly
poetical and similarly delirious, we cannot
give corroborative evidence. But it must be
delightful to be poetically drunk enough to
see fairies.
Mrs. Boult, the author of Joan of Arc, in
four acts, seven scenes and a tableau or two,
demands a huge stage and a Shakespearian
freedom. The drama, published by D. S.
Fprd, George-street, Sydney, depicts a dra-
matic subject without dramatic conciseness.
The speeches fiow on in narrative rather
tnan in dramatic form; and the drama is
more in the subject she has chosen than in
its presentment. Yet there> is good work,
and considerable cleverness in her adaptation
of a tremendous story for the stage. How-
ever, she has not adapted it for the modern
stage, since she has quite obviously taken
the technique, and the technique alone, of
the Elizabethan dramatists. Thus in the
scene, “Before the Principal Gates of Or-
leans,” she introduces the English Generals,
and immediately on their retirement, brings
on the attendants of Joan, and Joan herself
on a black charger—long before the English
Generals could have got out of sight. Then,
straight upon her departure, scouts of the
English army appear on the same small sec-
tion of ground, while “from off stage comes
all the tumult of a bloody war.” So close is
the bloody war that the scouts are able to de-
scribe to each other every petty detail of
the conflict. These conventions would have
passed unnoticed in a play of Shakespeare’s,
but for the modern drama they strain the
intelligence of the audience overmuch. As
a pageant-play, with much blue-pencilling,
Joan of Arc would have its place, not ex-
cluding the tableau of The Coronation of
Rheims, and of The Beatification, with its
vision of Joan looking down upon the scene,
haloed and carrying a white lily and a silver
crucifix. But in the modern drama Mrs.
Boult is a strange anachronism. She should
have been the literary hostess of the Mer-
maid Tavern.
Frankfort Sommerville contrives to say,
not something new, but something known
expressed in a new way, in The Spirit of
Paris (Black, per Dymock’s Library). The
illustrations in color by French artists alone
are attractive; but the style of the author
has the daintiness, the charming artifice, the
delicate precision, and the whimsicality of
these illustrations. He chatters of the
Parisienne, the theatre, the “Prismatic Pre-
cincts” of fashion, cafCs, amusements, and
artists —all the stock subjects, but pleasantly
and delicately treated. He takes his reader
to out-of-the-way theatres and cafes, intro-
ducing him to one of the latter, where all the
waiters are gentlemen who have lost
their fortunes and where there is a specially
named dinner for every kind of client. Thus,
the list suited to blokes who bring ladies
includes: “The ‘Petite Femme,’ for certain
circumstances ; the ‘Honeymoon’ dinner, con-
sisting merely of friandises and absurdities;
the ‘Delicate,’ a stage higher in tone; the
‘Consomme,’ where the lady cannot touch
anything but a spoonful of soup and a
shrimp or two, and has to be tempted; the
‘Rose-Marie,’ where the lady has a little
more healthy appetite ; the ‘Ylfinagfere,’ for a
man and his wife ; and so on.” And there is
for men the dinner “Perdrix”: “an excellent
little dinner meant to satisfy and interest,
but not too much to distract the attention.
Suited to persons who wish to talk —and
not merely of the things they are eating.”
And the “Bon Ton”: “Especially suited to
a gentleman who invites a friend and wishes
to give him an excellent meal only it does
not admit of so much conversation —it re-
quires thought.”
The latest shilling issues from Hodder and
Stoughton include The Dis-Honorable and
The Bush Track, two Australian stories by
David the author of the winning
entry in this firm’s recent prize novel com-
petition.

Sore Throat and Influenza.
THE COMMONEST WINTER COMPLAINTS:
THEIR PREVENTION AND CURE.
When the days are cold and wet in winter, disease
germs have a much greater power over man than
they have in summer. The reason is that chill and
damp lower the vitality and render the body less
liable to withstand the attack of the germs and to
destroy them. #
Although Nature has made a wonderful provision
for meeting these germ attacks by delegating to
certain panicles of the blood the mission to eat up
these intruders, the warmth and moisture in the
back of the mouth and throat enable the germs to
develop with extraordinary rapidity. Ivach germ
divides into two in twenty minutes, so that, in the
course of a few hours, a single germ will have pro-
duced hundreds of thousands.
If the body is in a good state of health, the germs
are destroyed very soon after being inhaled, so that
they produce no ill-effects.
When, however, the system is below par or
weakened by any cause, the germs easily overcome
the defending forces in the blood, and we get the
particular disease they produce.
It is in this way that sore throat,
influenza, and bad colds arise.
Obviously the only means of cure
and prevention is to destroy the
germs in the mouth and throat.
Of all the methods hitherto de-
vised by Science for producing this
result, nothing can compare with
Wulfing’s Formamint. This is
unanimously admitted bythe medical
profession.
Many thousands of doctors, in-
cluding several to crowned heads of
Europe, have written to the pro-
prietors stating that they invariably
use Wulfing’s Formamint for treat-
ing sore throat. The reason is that
nothing destroys the germs so rapidly
and completely.
This was first proved by a famous
scientist, who removed some virulent
germs of diphtheria from the throat
of a patient seriously ill with that
disease, and mixed them with a
solution of Formamint. Within
ten minutes they were all killed. In spite of
this, Formamint is not offered as a cure for
diphtheria, although it will undoubtedly prevent
that dread complaint and other serious germ-
diseases.
The Prevention of Diphtheria.
This fact is vouched for by many sanitary
inspectors. The sanitary inspector of High
Wycombe, England, writes : “ I know of no other
preparation so effectual in preventing infectious
disease as Wulfing’s Formamint. During an out-
break of diphtheria I have frequently given away
Formamint to those who have been in contact with
the disease, and no other cases have been removed
to hospital from the same household. Whenever
our ambulance is ordered out for the removal of an
infectious case, Formamint always accompanies
us.”
Many articles have appeared in the medical
papers pointing out Formamint’s supreme excel-
lence in the cure of sore throat. In The Prac-
titioner the Chief Medical Officer of one of the
largest Infectious Diseases’ Hospitals in the world
writes: “ I have never had sore throat myself since
I began to use Wulfing’s Formamint, although I
suffered periodically before.”
A writer in the Lancet states : “ I have had the
best results from the use of Wulfing’s Formamint
Tablets.”

Experiment* made at the Physiological Institute, Berlin, by Dr. Piorkowski.
Fi*. 1 shows the germs allowed to grow freely on
a culture medium comparable to the favourable
breeding giound of our throats. Fig. 2 shows the
remarkable decrease in the number of germs when
the breeding ground had been treated with the
saliva of a person who had sucked three Pormnnnirt
Tablets. Fig. 3 shows the entire destruction of the
germs when the bleeding ground had been tr. ated
with the saliva of a person who had sucked five
Formamint Tablets five minutes previously. ThM*
the Slicking of Wulhng’s Formamint prevents germ-
diseases by destroying the germs wh.eh oause them.
It should be noted, however, that only Wulfing’s
Formamint produces such results. None of the
many imitations now on the market will do so.
How valueless they are is shown by a physician,
who writes that he “ has tried six substitutes, and
has not found one of them efficacious.”
Remarka le Concensus of Opinion.
The social testimony to Formamint’s value is as
remarkable as the medical. No preparation, with
the single exception of Sanatogen, which is made
by the same firm, has received such distinguished
testimonials.
The Right Hon. Viscount Massereene and Ferrard
writes: “ I have found Wulfing’s Formamint most
effective for the cure of sore throat, which quickly
disappears after using a few tablets.”
The Right Hon. Lord Glantawe writes : “ I have
been using Wulfing’s Formamint for sore throat
with most satisfactory results.”
The Right Hon. Lord Kingsale writes: “I and
my family use quite a number of Wulfing’s
Formamint Tablets, and find them all you say they
are. I always carry a goodly number in my pocket,
and have recommended them to many friends.”
As to their value in influenza, Sir George Alex-
ander writes: “ Formamint Tablets have been my
constant companions during the winter, and I have
found them a dangerous enemy to the influenza
fiend.”
Among other distinguished users
of Wulfing’s Formamint may be
mentioned the Right Hon. Arthur
J. Balfour, M.P., Sir Gilbert Parker,
M.P., and scores of other M.P.’s; the
Hon. Mrs. Alfred Lyttelton ; Madame
Adelina Patti; and Lord Justice
Buckley, in addition to many leading
barristers, clergymen, actors , and
singers, to all of whom the preserva-
tion of the voice and the avoidance
of sore throat is of the utmost im-
portance.
Wulfing’s Formamint may be ob-
tained of all Chemists, price 2s. 6d.
per bottle. A Free Sample will be
sent to every reader who has not
tried it before who writes to Messrs.
A. Wulfing & Co., 17, Bond Street,
Sydney, mentioning the Bulletin ,
and enclosing two penny stamps
towards postage. With it will be
sent an interesting booklet on sore
throat.
Business, Robbery, etc.
“Machete”: The clerks in the various Port Moresby
(Papua) business houses turned as one worm the
other day and threatened to strike unless their hours
were shortened. After a futile kick by the bosses,
it was agreed that work should begin in future at 9
a.m. instead of .8 a.m. Having tasted blood, the
victors are contemplating further “exactions” ; and
Heaven knows what poor old harassed Capital will
do about it. After Papua, there are not many islands
left for it to tty to.
* * * *
“Ricardo”: The outlook for the resurrected beet-
sugar industry at Maffra (Vic.) is very gloomy.
Growers have tried the crop for two years now, and
there apparently isn’t one of them who hasn t found
the business a complete failure. Further, the grow-
ers believe that, with successive croppings, the
vield will fall off, so that things will get worse in-
stead of better; and a third of them have conse-
quently quitted. Five tons is their average; and, at
present prices, 10 tons, they say, is the smallest crop
that will pay. About the one man who hasn’t lost
heart is Dyer, the imported expert; and his opti-
mism isn’t counting for much among the men who
are losing the money. He's on salary ; they're not.
* * * *
“Gerald Barker”: Apropos Sydney’s North Shore
bridge. The height of the new German leviathan's
masts above water level is 250 ft., whereas the high-
est point of the main snan of the proposed bridge,
recommended by the Ylorks Committee, .is 1 /6ft.
T coming of the leviathans to Australia is not
yet, but I notice that there will be an 18,000-
tonner in Sydney Harbor by September next—the
White Star liner Ceramic. Everybody knows that
l he swinging out of the big boats moored at Circu-
lar Quay is dangerous, and their departure from
the Quay is not far off. Where are they to go, if not
higher up the harbor and “under the bridge”? We
don’t want to build this bridge to accommodate
merely the vessels of to-day. It must be good enough
for a hundred years. And if Australia grows and
shipping grows as both have been growing lately,
what chance has a 176 ft. bridge of satisfying the
needs of the year 1960 ?
The dexterous butter faker has been a busy artist
this year, according to the Commonwealth Export
Department. From January 1 to May 31, 162 con-
signments examined for export had to be held back
because the pump had encroached unduly on the
work of the cow. Sixteen per cent, of moisture is
allowed by both English and Australian laws, but
the insatiable faker poured in considerably more.
There were only 84 consignments stopped for a like
reason last year, so evidently the prestidigitator is
getting his hand in practice. In addition, a lot of
boxes were “short weight,” and more were “bare
weight,” i.e., contained less than sufficient alleged
butter to make good on the scales on arrival in Eng-
land. Seven consignments contained over 0.5 of
boric acid, and they had to be stopped. The Export
Department rounded off its report as follows: “If
those concerned in the butter trade exercise the same
energy in producing first-class butter that some of
them do in complaining about British buyers, and
in fighting against any assistance from the Govern-
ment here in the matter of grading, etc., the ques-
tion of marketing would soon be settled satisfac-
torily.” And the faker deserved the acid post-
script.
* * * *
Some time ago, when the chaos on Sydney’s
wharves was a public scandal, and the wail of the
carter, looking for his cases under a heap higher than
the Kutab Minar was loud in the land, the Harbor
Trust Commissioners tired of trying to make anti-
Sosh cart its goods away in decent time. So they
purchased two motor-lorries, and announced that the
Trust would shift the goods of any consignor who
asked it to do so. Sydney’s merchant princes, im-
porting kings and middlemen generally, are anti-
Sosh to a man, yet they rushed the Socialistic motor-
cart to such an extent that there is now quite a small
fleet running to cope with the requests from anti-
Sosh; and to-day, when an oversea liner arrives to
disgorge its cargo, it doesn’t proceed to dump it on
top of the remains of the previous three or four
shiploads. This has so annoyed Private
Enterprise in the carrying trade that an
irate deputation lately waited on the Trust,
to protest against State competition. _ The
Chief Commissioner blandly disclaimed any idea of
competition, but announced that, as long as the
trading community asked the State to shift its
stuff, the State would go on shifting. The deputa-
tion fumed. It despaired, evidently, of inducing its
old ant’-Sosh customers to come back to the good
anti-Sosh fold, and it went away proclaiming its
intention of “having the law” of the abominable
tiger, which has once more turned out to be a reli-
able working bullock.
* * * *
“Kailoa”: Signing the necessary form for a Syd-
ney Ferries season ticket is becoming, year by year,
more and more like signing your own death warrant;
but the latest one, which follows directly on top of
very much advanced rates, would make the ordinary
death-warrant tear out its hair and go green with
envy. It leads off with an earnest prayer to the
manager that he will be gracious enough to permit
you to help the Ferries Co. pay its dividends by issu-
ing to you a season ticket, and as a further induce-
ment, you will agree to abide by certain conditions,
including this: —
“The co. shall not be liable for loss of life or in-
jury suffered by any passenger however caused, nor
loss of or damage to passengers’ luggage or other
property .... whether such loss or damage arises from
accident or from theft or pilferage, or from any
act, default, neglect or error of judgment of masters,
officers or crew or any other servant or agent of the
co. .. .by storms, stress of weather, riots, strikes,
lockouts or combination of... .any persons or labor-
ers whatsoever connected with the. .. .obtaining of
coal or other material used in connection with the
running of a ferry service.... or any other persons
employed, directly or indirectly, in the business or
industry of running a ferry service.”
So when, through “neglect or error of judgment” of
an .underpaid ferry captain, your wife and family
suddenly cease to take any further interest in ferry
companies ; or when your new Marianne reports that
a S.F. employee stole her luggage, and she can t
arrive; or wlien one of the deck-hands pokes your
wife’s eye out with a broom, or trips you up with
a rope and breaks your leg, and you go with fire in
your eyes to the Ferry Co., it will merely produce
your writing authorising them to do such things
free of charge. Then you will rise up and damn
the co., and damn everyone who voted “No” on
the Referendum, and damn anyone who advised any-
one else not to vote “Yes.”
* * * *
“B.”: Australian wines will, it is said, be largely
represented at the next London wine show. But
that isn’t much good to the Australian consumer.
Whatever opinion London passes on choice samples
will leave the Australian the victim he mostly is
to-day. If proprietors looked ahead they would see
that the Australian consumption is the backbone of
their trade, and that the export is merely an extra.
Therefore they would study the Australian’s pocket
and his head, too. It is ridiculous to roll the stuff
in casks or barrels into any shop that can pay the
small license fee, and trust to the shopman or shop-
woman to give it a name and a price. Some firms
label “our own bottling,” but that is only in respect
of the higher-priced. Even then the buyer has to
risk a lot. But without this small guarantee it is
all risk. Wine growers or proprietaries that wish to
treat the consuming public fairly must follow their
casks into the market, and see that the public gets
the best goods possible at the price paid. Till then
Australian wine-drinking will be under a cloud.
* * * *
From a correspondent in Perak (Federated Malay
States), showing, among other things, the kind of
odd jobs which State Socialism is expected to do
nowadays:—“During the last three months the rub-
ber planters of Malaya have been much harassed by
the raids of wild elephants. Several important plant-
ing centres have been visited, especially in the exten-
sive district of - Sungei Siput, Perak, where
are situated the estates of several well-known cos. —
for instance, Kamuniiig, Changkat Salak, Heawood,
and Perak —as well pe many others, privately owned.
In this district alone, since the beginning of the year,
there have been no less than six raids, and several
thousands pounds’ worth of damage has been done.
Only trees of about 10 months’ old and under have
been attacked. The elephants have not contented
themselves with simply pulling up the trees, but in
many cases have eaten the roots, thus making re-
planting an impossibility. The planters have for
some time past been agitating for immediate public
action, and, at the time of writing, the Government
is organising a large ‘drive.’ It is hoped to catch
the two herds that are doing all the damage. The
planters intend keeping up their agitation, for, under
the special circumstances, it is absolutely essential
that the herds of wild elephants throughout the
Federated Malay States should be kept completely
under control. If this is not done, it is felt that,
with the fall in the price of rubber, the matter will
be very serious.”

“Yarriambiak”: There is a youthful genius in Mel-
bourne who will probably end up as a J. D. Rocke-
feller if he can keep out of the clutches of the police
long enough to give his talents a fair chance._ I first
saw him about a week ago, when he was piloting an
eminently respectable but distinctly blithered old
lady across t’linders-street. ITe propped her up
against a post and informed her that any tram would
take her to Spencer-street. The elderly female was
profuse in her expressions of gratitude, but the boy
expected more. lie burst into voluminous tears and
sobbed out his pitiful story. He was an orphan,
whose cruel father had turned his mother and him-
self from the door; he was suffering from sleeping
sickness; he had not had anything to. eat for seven
years, and could the kind lady give him a trine for
food. The kind lady could, and did. A couple of
hours later the inventor of this new trade —or is it
a profession?—discovered an ancient gentleman who
had been putting down the cursed drink, and was
busy making figure eights outside the railway sta-
tion. He took the old chap in charge, tucked his
arm lovingly under his own, and threaded his way
through the traffic. Passing the John Hop on point
duty, he remarked in a loud whisper, “Hold up,
father,” and father dutifully held up. Arrived at
the other side, “father” was placed in a vertical
position against a lamp-post, and the information
was conveyed to him that any car except those
marked St. Kilda would take him to Prahran. The
old gentleman weighed out a shilling without being
asked, and the new street pilot darted away to look
for more customers. I am expecting to hear any
day that he has formed himself into a limited lia-
bility company, and is training a corps of assistants
with special " aptitude for finding the wandering
drunk. After that there’s bound to be a union and
a wages board for the industry, and large questions
of dirt money and such like.
* * * *
“Bloggs”: The number of trivial ways in which
a casual, and more or less trusting, citizen may be
taken down in Sydney is astonishing. Did you ever
notice, for instance, the way half-pennies have of
appearing in your pockets seemingly from nowhere?
They are among the minor curses of my life. Who
wants a half-penny? What conceivable article of
value can be bought with it? The coin is, to all
intents and purposes, useless; and it takes up room
and is generally dirty, and in other respects unde-
sirable. And yet, since it is money, one is reluctant
to throw it away. As a gift, it is spurned even by
the smallest of children. In brief, it is a nuisance.
Half-pennies mostly come to me, I find, from tram-
conductors. The conductor dashes a handful of change
into my hand, consisting of an assortment of pennies,
three-penny-bits and the half-penny. I glance casually
at the money. It seems to count up all right. I
pocket it. The conductor is up a half-penny—that is,
if I don’t detect the swindle. If I do, the dishonest
hound apologises blandly, pays up, and tries the
same game on another. It is the safest swindle I
know, and guaranteed, in a hurried community like this,
to come off triumphantly four times out of five. The
other day I halted before a pleasant-looking paper-
seller who had arrived at the age of discretion, and
in every respect appeared reliable and honest. I
took a paper; gave him a shilling; and stood
brooding, as is frequently my wont. After a while
he said he couldn’t change a shilling and asked for
sixpence. I handed him sixpence. After a further
examination of his garments, he said, with a rather
taking air of mock despair, that he hadn’t even change
of sixpence, but would get it at the adjacent pub.
He returned, and handed me five-pence. I said:
“What about the shilling?” A look of anguish dis-
torted his face: “The shilling!” he wailed. “I give
it back ter yer.” Dull ns I am in business matters,
I knew well that he hadn’t. And he knew that he
hadn’t; and knew, moreover, that I knew he knew
he hadn’t. But what was there to be done? Fight
him? Give him in charge? Indulge in a public
wrangle with him? None of them seemed worth
while. With a sickly grin I left him to his ill-
gotten wealth. A couple of nights later I was de-
feated in an even simpler fashion. Again lusting
for a news-sheet, I tendered a boy paper-vendor a
half-sovereign; the only coin, barring one of the
tram-conductor’s sovereigns aforementioned, that I
had about me. He went for change to the usual pub,
while I stood reading. After I had digested the
whole of the cables and had got half-way through a
lurid murder-report, I bethought me of my change.
Simultaneously I observed that the pub was a corner
one with an" entrance (likewise an exit) in each
street. I debited 9s. lid. to my under-suspicious
disposition, and went home. I could cite a dozen
other ways in which I have been had during the last
six months or so, but fear to be accused of imbecility
or something like that. Yet why should I mind? The
accusation would come from people who had had
precisely similar experiences themselves.
UNREASONABLE GENEROSITY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We are devoting the next day or two to giving
away Miss Macdougall’s large stock of underclothing.
—Startling announcement in Sydney Sun.
At such a time as this, when past
Our ears a blizzard’s blowing ;
When all the skies are overcast;
And in the hills it’s snowing ;
1 say it neither just nor right is
To give away a lady’s nighties.
Charity tons of sinning shrives,
So claim the worst of Grundies ;
Yet surely not, when it deprives
A maiden of her undies;
No man of Soul e’en one small knicker
Would alienate —unless in liquor.
What right have people to dispose
In this high-handed fashion
Of Miss Maedougall’s underclothes?
(Forgive the note of passion;
Even the semi-altogether
Provokes a shudder in such weather.)
And who is She who idly stands
And makes no lamentations,
While folks deal out, with lavish hands,
Her winter combinations —
Garments which plainly are essential
To those whose life’s not penitential ?
It may be said that others gain
By all this reckless giving;
Those, as a case in point, who strain
Beneath the Cost of Living.
My answer rings like • some fierce bugle:
“Quite so. But what of Miss Macdougall?”
N.S.W.
D. B. J.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
An Idyll of Winter.
The languid stf« forgot to shine.
A grey light lay on land and river.
For one soft hand that dropped in mine
My hot kiss thanked the gracious giver.
A chill wind blew across the world
And brought a black cloud rolling after,
But one small hat-brim deftly curled
Made a red roof above our laughter.
Moved by that sound a small, brown bird
Glanced forth upon our gladness spying,
Amazed at that clear sound that stirred
Through the wide winter’s leafy sighing.
All careless we of clouded light,
Our warm blood beat to that old measure
That pulsed amid the scented night
When some brown god invented pleasure.
ffidl i ; n a valley moony pale,
Where sang the spray of one white foun-
tain.
An oread heard the whispered tale
Breathed by the shepherd of the mountain.
. k
And, stooping from the pine-fringed hill,
A kindly deity made mention
That that delightful moment’s thrill
Should be henceforth a world convention.
Stirred by the sweetness of a glance
Young love to beauty kissed devotion’,
And that which came by lightest chance
Became a recognised emotion.
The small bird perked his plumes and fanned
The chill leaves with a flirting feather,
As if he failed to understand
Our laughter in the bleak, grey weather.
But I, with Mabel’s hand in mine,
Felt summer in my full veins glowing,
With poppy flame and seas of wine
And hot December’s mirth-horns blowing.
A ruddier vintage seemed to start
From the swift burning of her blushes,
Than that which, from the red sun’s heart,
Between twin worlds the evening crushes.
So everything was really well,
We loved in simple, serious fashion,
Until the first large raindrops fell
And fotind two kindred hats to splash on.
Two hats above a single thought!
In case the angry skies should melt her,
Our single soul in haste we caught
And wrapped her up and ran for shelter.
N.S.W.
CURSE O’ MOSES.
Karl Von Hoffman.
Hard by the Northern Road lives Karl Von
Hoffman —
His porchway red with many a climbing
rose
Twice twenty blue and gold Australian
summers
And thirty German summertides he knows.
His river-lands are rich in purple lucerne —
How rich his red, fat-sided cattle tell;
His clustering grapes, bee-visited, bird-
haunted. ...
Upon the hillsides to their ripening swell.
Long commerce with the soil has made him
wealthy, , .
Much trade with nature brought him gra-
cious gain;
Year in, year out, he bartered them and
muscle
For wine and honey, cream and golden
grain.
At times beneath that porch of climbing
Her Cheeks rose-mantled and her mouth
She sits and sings—his fair Australian
daughter, , , . , .
What time Von Hoffman smokes his big-
bowled pipe.
She sings him back again to morning splen-
To boisterous youthhood by the dancing
Half the night was given to gold-haired
Gretchen, •
And half to high carousal, jest and wine.
Once more beneath the moon in piny wood-
He walks’ with Love, an exile from the
Or booSv-mated in black-raftered taverns
To clinking flagons shouts a rousing song.
He qpes himself a booted, bearded Uhlan
New come to battle, splashed and stained
TTio lift-pd sabre to the hilt-hold crimson,
H rS-fetlocked with the foeman's
blood.
Once more he sees that after-battle group-
The road-side inn set round with walnut
And wine tr S S elbow. seated three tall fellows
With trailing swords and helmets on their
knees.
Thev rise, they shout, their long-gapped
swords are lifted* ~ i
Their eyes, their blades with pride and
France.
Thus, hour by hour, with golden stars above
moons new risen, red and round and
bowled pipe.
N.S.W.

The Bulletin

Leod, of Musgrave and McLeod Streets,

Wales, Australia.

TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.
(In Advance, Only.)
Per Annum . . .. ..£l6O
Half-yearly .. .. .. 0 13 6
Quarterly . . . . . . .. 0 7 0
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty -
Six Shillings Sterling you can have The Bulletin
posted regularly to you every week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., which should be crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to The
Manager.
How to Address Eetters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some
care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,-” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the form
of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short
Tales or Sketches (those dealing with, Australian sub-
jects, and not exceeding two columns in length, or,
say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable). Paragraphs
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of
MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea,
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.
To Travellers Abroad.
Copies of The Bulletin can be obtained in Lon-
don, after the arrival of each mail, from Messrs.

THURSDAY, JULY 17, 1913.
THE POSTPONED BRIDGE.
The Sydney-North Shore bridge has no great
luck. Perhaps it has killed a black cat or
slain a Chinaman. It has been advocated
since the roots of Original Sin. It has been
discussed by more commissions and com-
mittees than the Oldest Inhabitant cares to
remember, because the recollection of them
makes him feel too old. In fact, the recol-
lection makes him feel buried. The members
of the very first commission walked on the
shore in the twilight holding each other’s
tails, and discussed cantilevers and such-like
engineering devices while carrying a cocoa-
nut in the hand that wasn’t full of tail. It
was decided then to postpone the great work
until Adam should be born, in the hope that
he would be able to see Abraham, the first
Jew, and speak intelligibly to him concern-
ing a loan. The Oldest Inhabitant reckons
that that unsatisfactory sort of business has
been going on ever since. The evidence taken
at the various inquiries, if it were piled up in
the middle of the harbor, would make a very
good support for the bridge. The work has
been passed, and endorsed, and recommended
again and again. But through much post-
ponement an idea seems to have arisen that,
in some way or another, Sydney is a wholly
unusual kind of city; that North Sydney is
an unprecedented kind of suburb; and that
what is required is a unique bridge, built
amid unparalleled circumstances, with a
hitherto unknown variety of water flowing
beneath it, at a pace not previously heard
of. Everybody concerned seems to be
afflicted by the idea that it isn’t a mere ques-
tion of building a plain old bridge, just like
any other plain old bridge of the same size,
but that the community is up against a pro-
blem. The people concerned feel as if they
were Columbuses, tittering on the verge of
a sea of mechanical investigation. So the
Government staggers each time a report
comes to hand, and passes its trembling hand
over its matted brow, and decides that it
won’t rush into the wildernesses of the Un-
known just yet. It will wait for further de-
velopments, and find out if bridges really
exist in other parts of the world, and if they
are a success, and if Providence doesn’t
object to them.
* * * *
The. usual experience has happened again.
There was a notable investigation, and almost
everybody gave evidence so long as his voice
held out, and when he couldn’t speak any
more he made signals with his hands. The
construction of the bridge was duly recom-
mended. This was about the one-hundredth
committee of investigation, and it ascertained
nothing that the previous ninety-nine inves-
tigations weren’t equally acquainted with. It
simply found out that there was land on each
side, and water in between, and that there
was a recognised method of dealing with such
cases. Then the matter was hung up on
the same old basis. Minister Gkifeith pro-
fesses to have faith that the postponement is
only temporary, but faith has been on the
premises so long that the community is tired
of seeing the animal, and hates to pay the
dog-tax on it any longer. Temporariness
in the matter of the Sydney-North Shore
bridge seems a trifle more permanent than
eternity. One reason assigned for the stop-
page is that the country objects to the ex-
penditure of so much money on the bloated
city of Sydney. It is quite probable that it
does object to the expenditure of money on
Sydney. But the country also objects, in
many places, to the building, largely at the
country’s expense, of unnecessarily long rail-
ways to carry the country’s produce to Syd-
ney, when there are much more convenient
ports to which the country, if it wasn’t for
the hungry monopoly of Sydney, could send
its exports at less than half the cost. And
the country objects not a little to costly rail-
way duplications, which wouldn’t be re-
quired for many years save that the policy
of dragging everything to Sydney at the coun-
try’s expense has hopelessly clogged the ex-
isting lines. Further, the country can’t be
expected to feel any better over the expendi-
ture of public money on Sydney’s under-
ground railways than on a city bridge. Yet
the congestion policy, and the centralisation
policy, and the policy of shutting up the out-
lying ports, and the costly railway duplica-
tions these policies make necessary,
all go on. And the city underground rail-
ways are to be started, according to Minister
Griffith, almost immediately, regardless of
the cost and irrespective of the country’s feel-
ings. These railways, it is alleged, are
matters of necessity, while the Sydney-North
Shore bridge is merely an affair of conveni-
ence. The difference between necessity and
convenience in' this matter is not easy to
define.
* * * *
The Sydney, tube railways are certainly
a matter of urgency, for N.S.W.’s capital is
probably the only big civilised city on earth
which to' convey all its traffic on the
surface, by /tram-car, motor-car, cab, taxi-
cab, buggy, cart, dray, perambulator, wheel-
barrow, ambulance, horse, human hoof, drunk
carried by a policeman, and such-like simple
conveyance, and to do this amid a maze of
narrow, winding streets. But the first essen-
tial reform is to abolish the present Hump-
and-Dead-End station, and to push the fairly-
cheap surface railway right through the city
and across the harbor. Even in a place as
large as Sydney a surface railway is inex-
pensive as compared with a subterranean
one. Probably the surface railway and the
bridge would cost as little as the subter-
ranean system without the bridge, and, by
practically abolishing the tramway traffic to
and from the present railway station, the
surface railway might give more immediate
relief. But the subterranean or tube rail-
way, or underground tramway, represents the
line of least political resistance. It will only
compete against the State-owned tramways,
and they , have no infuriated host of scrip-
holders to protest on behalf of their vested
interests. On the other hand, an extension
of the surface railway would make a bridge
practically inevitable. . And a bridge would
compete against a very rich monopoly, re-
presented by a very rich and angry and in-
fluential body of shareholders. It may be
that one Government after another has sized
up the influence and hostility of these share-
holders, and has become afraid. It may be
that it is the influence of these shareholders
that has always kept Sydney’s railway sta-
tion in an absurd spot on the outskirts of
the city. If once that railway moves into the
city, then the building of the bridge in order
to connect with North Sydney and establish
an alternative route to the North becomes
inevitable. And the building of the bridge
will be disastrous to the ferry monopoly. And
the railway hasn't moved worth a cent.
Almost every other city on earth —from Mel-
bourne to mouldering old Damascus —has run
its railway right through, but Sydney stands
by itself on the theory that a continuous line
through a big centre of population is impos-
sible. Exactly how much the voting power
of persons interested in the immensely rich
ferry monopoly has to do with this strange
meptal attitude it is impossible to say. There
is, so far as this paper is aware, no other
ferry monopoly of its wealth and size in the
world. Certainly there was nothing resem-
bling it on the Hawkesburv Biver. a few
miles north of Sydney, and the building of a
bridge there was found quite easy.
* » * *
There have been many proposals that the
State should interfere, by the construction
of bridges or tunnels, available for the use
of trains, tram-cars, private vehicles, horses,
dogs and foot-passengers, with the proud
Private Enterprise institution, which prac-
tically owns the harbor, yet reserves the
right to refuse to sell tickets to people who
want to cross the harbor if it happens to
object to these people. Each proposal has
bumped up against Something. Each one
has come into fatal concussion with Cir-
cumstances Unknown. Even when money
was being borrowed at a rate never before
heard of, and gaols and crockery were being
acquired out of loans, it always happened
that money couldn’t be borrowed for this
especial work. When the surplus was said
to be larger than any previous surplus it
somehow chanced that it wasn’t the kind
of surplus that could come in just here. The
right kind of surplus (probably a red sur-
plus with green stripes) and the right kind
of loan (possibly a pale blue loan with pas-
sementerie on it) which would fill the par-
ticular bill never eventuated. And all the
time the ferry monopoly grows larger and
more influential, and acquires more respect
among politicians. Also, it may be, more
politicians’ mothers-in-law acquire shares in
THE FOUR INDESCRIBABLES AND
THE UNSPEAKABLE.
When the complete history of the wild-cat
war in the Balkan Peninsula is written it
should make a remarkable narrative. The
dead haven’t been counted yet, but there is
a suspicion that in some of the combats a
lone-standing record has been broken. In
ISO 9, when Charles of Austria wiped the
floor with Napoleon, and taught him for the
first time that he might be beaten at his own
game, it is recorded that the victors of the
battle of Aspern and Essling lost one-third
of their number, and the defeated crowd
lost one-half or a little more. These figures
have held good in Europe for 104 years, and
if the blood-blot in some of the recent Bal-
kan annoyances has been bigger in propor-
tion than that of Aspern and Essling it will
be a strenuous event. When four of the
five little Balkan States —for lioumania
stood out —combined to make war upon the
Unspeakable Turk the odds against them
looked impossible. They mustered about
10,000,000 people under four separate com-
mands, while the Unspeakable had 24,000,000
people, on a rough estimate, to draw upon,
and his armies were under one chief, and
directed by one intelligence. Also he had
behind him a great pres.tige and a fair
amount of money—or debased coinage. Like-
wise he had tons of badly-printed notes. The
Confederates were divided by three great
hatreds, for the Bulgarian loathes the Greek
and is loathed in return ; and the Bulgarian
also hates the Servian and Montenegrin
(these two hold pretty well together), and
is hated in return; and the Servian and
Montenegrin have no use for the Greek, who,
by way of retaliation, has no use for them.
The one binding force of the Alliance
was the common objection to the Unspeak-
able Turk, and this common virtue held the
discordant elements together while the first
war lasted. The Confederates mapped out
the territory that was to be conquered, and
they carefully divided the skin of the bear
before the bear was dead.
* * * *
The bear died to a very considerable ex-
tent, amid the applause of everybody who
knew him, for there is no better way of
spending a pleasant Sunday afternoon in
south-eastern Europe than in attending a
Turk’s funeral —unless, indeed, one can bury
two Turks and leave a dog on the grave. It
was a great effort made against heavy odds.
The Greek seemed to have recovered the
punch which he lost at Chaeronea, 2250 years
ago; and the Servian was getting square
for the downfall of Lazarus, the Czar, at
Kossova in 1389; and the Bulgarian had
enough grievances to avenge to fill a whole
Book of Lamentations. Also the multitu-
dinous Turk didn’t fight up to his reputa-
tion. His two best generals were murdered
by their own people, and the much-adver-
tised Enver Bey fled for his life and left
his troops behind him. Unfortunately, when
the Unspeakable was defeated, the four In-
describables fell out among themselves. The
skin of the bear was ready for division
according to previous arrangement, but out-
side Powers interfered. When Austria and
its allies declared that Skutari must go to
enrich a new kingdom of Albania, Monte-
negro found that its one big nrize had van-
ished, and that it had been fighting for prac-
tically nothing. A bit of Servia’s share of
the plunder also went to Albania. Greece
found some of its portion of the skin cut off.
So these three States put it to Bulgaria,
which retained its portion of the captured
territory intact —put it as one infuriated
Christian to another—that there should be
a new division of what captured territory
was left. And Bulgaria, suffering from the
last possible magnificence in the way of
swelled head, and inflated by the fact that
it had done the hardest fighting and in-
curred the biggest losses, told its three late
allies to go to that place which isn’t men-
tioned in polite society. Incidentally it
began to think about robbing its allies of
the little bit of profit thac remained
to them. Then commenced the new
war of three Indescribables against
one, while the defeated Unspeakable put
raw steak on his damaged eye in the back-
ground, and gave the scrap his moral sup-
port, and said it was good. A less decayed
person than the Turk would have joined in
and goj: something of his own back again.
The Greek took up the challenge of Bul-
garia with joy in his heart and a light shin-
ing in his countenance. He sets it down-
largely to the attacks of the Bulgarians in
the far past that his empire was so weak-
ened as to make successful resistance to the
Turk impossible. For the loss of Constan-
tinople and Adrianople and Smyrna and
Aleppo and Damascus, and all his vast pos-
sessions in Europe and Asia, and for his 400
years or so of servitude to the Turk, he
holds his recent ally in a great degree re-
snonsible. He would like a dead Bulgarian
for a birthday present quite as much as a
seltzogene. Also the Servians remember that
the Bulgarians licked them badlv in the
80’s of last century; and they hold that, if
Bulgaria had stood fast by the Serb empire
in the time of Stephen Dushan. the Turk
would never have taken root in Europe at
all and centuries of woe would have* been
averted. So the recent allies gathered up
some five centuries’ accumulation of malig-
nity and went to war. And, in the back-
ground, the defeated Unspeakable silently
applied raw steak to his eye.
* * * *
Information from the seat of war has
been wild and mad and gory and confused
but it soon appeared that the Bulgarian
person had taken on too large a con-
tract. He represents a State with
only million people. He sent his
last man and his last coin to the front in
the conflict with the Turk, and he bought
victory at tremendous expense. He started
the new war with his second best army, for
his best army was buried. For want
of men to till the farms there is practically
no crop in view, which is a serious business
for a mostly-agricultural community. The
Bulgarian had to keep some kind of an army
on his southern border to watch the Turk,
who still has a population of nearly
20,000,000 to draw upon if he feels like
renewing the war. He was doing a fierce
scramble in a westerly direction with
Greece (2,800,000 people) ; Servia
(3,000,000) ; and Montenegro (250,000).
On the north lies the old, semi-Latin king-
dom of Roumania (population 6,000,000),
which demands payment at Bulgaria’s ex-
pense for its neutrality during the recent
disagreement, and suddenly sent 500,000
troops to collect the bill. So busi-
ness was strenuous, and Ferdinand,
the elderly botanical king with the
countenance of a Mephistopheles, had
much on his hands. Savoff, the Bulgarian
commander who did such good service
against the Turk, was recently sacked on
a charge of “precipitating” the present an-
noyance. This was a case of changing
horses in the middle of the stream. Of his
successor it was said proudly that he had,
by a fine military proceeding, “driven a
wedge” between the Servians and the
Greeks, which is a dubious expression and
capable of two meanings. If the commander
who does that sort of thing wins it is said
that he broke the enemy’s line. If he loses,
it is said that the enemy attacked
him on both flanks and wiped him out. The
Bulgarians seem to have done a brilliant
manoeuvre and fallen in. In war almost
any tactics are the right tactics provided
the right man is behind the tactics, even
supposing they are wrong. Thus Napoleon
I. made his successes by a policy of swift
concentration directed against antiquated
generals, who scattered their forces. Then
he struck irreparable disaster when he col-
lided with a Russian commander who spread
an inferior army along a line several hun-
dred miles from one end to the other,
and proved that the elusive, scattered kind
of warfare was the game that he really
knew. Bulgaria appears to have struck the
prize mishap of its recent history when it
broke the enemy’s line. Perhaps it was the
wrong kind of general who broke the line,
or he broke it at the wrong place, or he
broke the wrong man’s line.
So Bulgaria appears to have fallen almost
in a day from the pinnacle of glory to the
cellar or basement of misfortune. Mean-
while the Unspeakable Turk hangs
round in the background and puts raw steak
on his eye. And whatever troubles may be
created by the rather squalid “Christians”
of the Balkans-—however Greek and Bul-
garian and Serb may howl and bite at one
another —the Turk’s discomfiture makes
Europe a good deal cleaner than it was.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Concerning a Stamp.
The proposal of Postmaster-General Agar
Wynne to put the Post Office under the
control of Commissioners may lead to one
good result. It may prevent maddened per-
sons like Agar Wynne altering the coun-
try’s postage stamp whenever a new Govern-
ment comes in with only the Speaker’s cast-
ing vote and the forbearance of its oppo-
nents to ■ save it from destruction. The
stamp introduced by the Fisher Adminis-
tration had its drawbacks, certainly. It was
not so artistic as it might have been. But
at least it was Australian. The simple,
plain old kangaroo was its leading feature,
and Australia owns; the kangaroo. That
quaint and cheerfiißibjteast is this country’s
monopoly and ad%ra£<ppaent. Australia may
be said to ;ClateSffts' from the time
when PelsArt A; the Diifch navigator, took
reports and" drawings and par-
ticulars and plans and specifications of the
kangaroo back with him to ’Holland. It
might he possible to limn a better kangaroo
than the one op the fTsnfcß Government’s
stamp, but it would certainly be easy to do
a better King than t lifer one wlio is to take
the creature’s place. ’ Agar Wynne pro-
poses rush in where emus fear to tread,
with a concern that looks like a marine store
or a museum. In the middle there is the
head of the King. Above that appears the
word “Australia.” Then happens a top
line consisting of five designs—-a cross with
four spots on it; a sort of Maltese cross; a
crown; a lobster ; and a hurricane or blot or
something. Down the left side is a swan,
some foliage, a very meaty kangaroo, and a
large statement which says “Id.” At the
bottom the stamp says “One Penny.” On
the right-hand side going upwards is again
the wild, unsupported statement “Id.” It
isn’t even backed by an affidavit made be-
fore a Justice of the Peace.V Next —still
going upwards—the, infuriated spectator
arrives at a hen or emu ,or ostrich with a
stack of hay for a tail. sort of burst of
foliage separates this., wild egg-layer from
an alleged lion wi(h an impossible tail in
three curves. Round the Kina: appears the
statement “Australia” (above) “Postage”
(below). Itv is necessary to mention that
it is “postage” in order to prevent the public
imagining that it is an insurrection or a
wheelbarrow. The King, who occupies the
middle of the spectacle, wears a moustache,
a beard, some hair, a smile, a uniform, a
badge, two epaulettes, four stars, and a few
sundries.. there is a crinkled
border like a grandmother’s cap. All good
citizens are hereby advised to lay in £5
worth of the old kangaroo stamps to keep
them going until Agar Wynne and the
<. o°. K Government and the new postage
foolishness have all disappeared.
The “S. M. Herald” Discovers Some
More Facts about the Beef Trust.
o T l S r a <^eei ‘i n g s is n of the times that the
S. M. Herald should be palpably getting
ready, even at this early hour, to drop on to
the pro-Referenda side of the political fence.
It discovered officially, last month, for the
first time, that the Beef Trust was in Aus-
tralia a matter that had been common
knowledge to most intelligent folks outside


party politics for about a year. Also, it
mentioned that the Trust’s object in coming
to Australia could only be to corner the
British meat trade — a proposition which was
first put forward by The Bulletin nearly
three years ago. On Friday last, the cau-
tious old sheet went a step or two further,
and published some details which this paper
has been harping on ever since the Referenda
campaign started. The following, for ex-
ample, is the sort of thing that The Bulle-
tin has said repeatedly, and that the ordin-
ary common or hedge or platitude-mongering
Liberal has as often condemned loftily for so
many reckless attempts to raise the “Trust
bogey”: —
A matter that is of the utmost consequence both
to this country and to Great Britain is the American
Beef Trust, which controls the supply in North
America, and which is said to be acquiring control
of that in the Argentine. That is to say, the Trust
is practically supreme in two of the three sources
from which comes the bulk of the beef consumed in
Britain. The existence of a competitor in the shape
of Australia still keeps the Trust from dictating the
prices in London, but there is not the least doubt
that could the Trust once include Australia among
its conquests it would be> in a position to impose
what terms it liked upon the consumer.... As long as
we are able to export in competition with the Ameri-
can Trust the British public will be able to eat beef
at a reasonable expenditure. Once we fall under its
influences, the price of beef in Britain will be what
the Trust thinks fit to demand, and that will be as
much as can be screwed out of the pockets of a
public to whom beef is almost a necessity of life.
No wonder that Britain is anxious, and we in Aus-
tralia should not be less concerned to keep the Trust
from getting a foothold here. If it gets any sort of
control this will affect not only the price of exported
meat, but also of meat for local consumption. Should
the Trust acquire any large interests in any of the
States where cattle-raising is carried on extensively,
everyone would feel the pinch. For our own sakes, no
less than for the sake of Great Britain, we must, by
every means, endeavor to keep such a development
from our doors.
By way of justifying the very different tone
of voice in which it talked last month, when
the electors of N. S. Wales needed to have
their legs drastically pulled in the interests
of Liberalism, the Hekald finished up as
follows: —
Our attitude to the Referendum on tuis point was
that, in the first place, there is no evidence that such
Trusts exist, and in the second place, that we have
adequate legal machinery to deal with them if and
when they do. We still believe that we are correct
in each of these propositions. But we are fully alive
to the danger which must constantly threaten the
economic organisation of every country nowadays, and
we believe that in the case of the Beef Trust this
danger may sooner or later threaten Australia.
If (1) the American Beef Trust is
not now in Queensland (and on the 26th
of last month the Herald itself admitted
that it was) ; or if (2) it is there, but
is impotent to do any damage, owing
to our 1910 Anti-Trust Act being a far
more efficacious monopoly-prophylactic than
America’s futile Sherman Act, then the
Herald is wantonly wasting its leader space
in discussing the Trust menace at all. In
either of the above events Australia is per-
fectly safe, and so is Great Britain. If the
Beef Trust (which, by the way, does not
confine its operations to; beef, as the Herald
evidently imagines; but also deals in mutton
and every other sort of meat, and :n all the
by-products of the meat trade, as well as m
a host of other foodstuffs which have no re-
lation to the dead animal business), if this
mammoth curse is not here, or can be ejected
as soon as it starts to be objectionable, there
is no more need for the sounding of warn-
ings on the subject than there is for excited
public mention of the fact that the inhabi-
tants of Mars might be dangerous if they
invaded the Sandwich Islands. From which
premises it may readily be deduced that the
Herald in its heart to-day believes that the
dangers which it scoffed at as recently as last
month are the genuine goods. The doctrine
of State rights has, in a manner of speaking,
rendered the Sherman Anti-Trust Act in-
effective in the United States; and has al-
lowed the Beef Trust to grow there, lhe
same pernicious doctrine is going to have the
same effect here ; inasmuch as (1) the Austra-
lian Parliament has, under existing circum-
stances, no power to touch a Trust which is
registered in any ond State of the I edera-
tion, and (2) the experience of decades goes
to show that no individual State can be
relied on to show sufficient altruism to oust
a rich monopoly which is making .its profits
at the expense of the neighbors of those who
are harboring it, while designedly sparing
its hosts. The only possible cure is an
amendment of the Trade and Gommerce
clause in the Constitution, that will give
the Australian Parliament full control or
both inter-State and intra-State trade and
commerce. Judging by the form of the past
few weeks, this paper confidently expects to
see the S. M. Herald advocating the
Fisher Government’s king-pin amendment
within —say —a year from to-day.

When a Tory newspaper starts screaming
at the iniquities of State Socialism and the
martyrdom of Private Enterprise, even th<
staunchest Tory is apt to rub his eyes ana
wonder whether he reads and hears arign .
For a Tory Granny in her wrath will say
anything. She rails dementedly. Just now
the Melbourne Argus has lost her reason
(or her “block”) as the result of the Wati

bearing country to Private Enterprise,
unhappy ancient beats her flat breast,
bangs her head against the stone wall ana

As soon as the State commenced operations at
Wonthaggi it became evident that Privat

Private Enterprise has been forced to pocket
