The Imperishable Independent.
C, G Wade, one-time State Premier of
N S W., is either a very simple-minded
man or else he is a stupendous bluffer. Con-
sider the following from the column-long
protest, which he lodged the other day,
against the formation of a Country party .
Within the Parliamentary party there is ; a distinct
section known as the Country party. There is a
sub-committee specially appointed to act on behal
of the country—to watch all matters effecting coun
trv interests, and to consult with the Liberal leader.
t anv coterie of members, calling themselves _ a
vure Country party, secure better results or exercise
more influence that does the country section of the
present Liberal party?.
The obvious answer is a resonant xes.
Absurd though it s«.Vnds;htheT^orraparlia-
liamentary party, like the Labor 1
mentary party, is out. primarily, not hai
£ much to advantage the State or any par-
ticular section thereof, as it is to seize office
and hold it. With that end in view would-
be Liberal candidates are forced to sign the
most binding pledges imaginable before they
are allowed to pursue a Liberal nomination ,
V iberal nominees have to sign more pledges ,
and Liberal members who betray a shadow
of restiveness under the party whip or, to
rmHt otherwise, who display the possession
put it ojnei > regard to any con-
ot minds oE theirown in g heartily
‘‘mugwumps” and “traitors.”
and if they Persist are ousted from the party
U Lip? (due of the main tragedies of
the° g archaic political system coder wh.ch
Australia staggers so painfully to and from
the pawnshop, lies in the fact that a long
spell in office is rarely the reward which a
party gets for doing the big and urgent
things. The Federal Labor party, for ex-
ample, was responsible for the imposition
of the Land Tax, and for the starting of a
local army and navy; it inaugurated the
Interstate Commission; and got a move on
with one transcontinental railway—neces-
saries which no previous party had had the
nerve to tackle. As one result the Cook
party, whose entire policy consists appar-
ently in changing the appearance of a stamp
and abolishing signed journalism, is in re-
ceipt of the wages and perquisites of office.
One of the first rules of the Party Govern-
ment game is: “Never alienate support.”
Another is: “Soothe the enemy, if possible,
while conceding him nothing of any value.”
A third is: “Remain inoffensively inert un-
less absolutely kicked into action.” The
above rules apply more particularly to Lib-
eral Administrations, which depend for so
much of their support, financial and other-
wise, on those who are interested in main-
taining the status quo. All of which cir-
cumstances make the institution of a separ-
ate Country party a first essential to decen-
tralisation or any other move which theatens
the material interests of Sydney and the
Sydney press. A country section of the
Liberal Parliamentary party is an extremely
useful thing in its way—useful, that is, to
get Liberalism into power and keep it there.
The fact that it is in existence acts as a
sort of balm, or emollient lotion on mad- ;
dened backblockers who are wooden-headed
enough not to see the audacious way in
which the Vested Interests of Sydney use
whichever party happens to be in power to
their own advantage. All hands are en-
couraged to blither about decentralisation
for all they are worth. As C. G. Wade
put it last week: —
Was it not the Liberal party who first initiated
decentralisation as a fixed principle of policy more
than three years ago? In pursuance of that prin-
ciple my Government appointed a Royal Commission
to determine upon the best routes for connecting up
the interior with coastal ports.
There is no vestige of trouble or protest
from anywhere until some misguided mem-
ber of the country section of the Parliamen-
tary party, exasperated by soothing but pro-
fitless chat, and the appointment of Royal
Commissions which lead nowhere, attempts
to make a definite move in the interests of
the country dwellers. Then such an uproar
arises from the Sydney papers and the Lib-
eral leaders, on the subject of discipline and
the need for the due observance thereof that
the wretched member either caves in, or
turns Independent and prepares for a swift
return to private life. The formation of a
Country party is as vital an essential to the
achievement of decentralisation in N. S.
Wales to-day as the formation of a
Nationalist party was to the achieve-
ment of Home Rule for Ireland in
the Mid-Victorian epoch. It is as
necessary that there should be inde-
pendent country representatives In the N. S.
Wales Legislative Assembly, if the country
is to make any headway against those whose
one aim in life is to bloat Sydney, as repre-
sentation by a lawyer or two would be in the
case of a citizen who was battling to estab-
lish his right to portion of a rich estate.
This is, no doubt, a regrettable, not to say
scandalous, state of affairs. Under a
rational system of government the whole
Parliament would be concerned, solely to do
its best for the State at large; and not be
driven to discriminate injuriously against
any one section as a condition of keeping
in office. But till Party Government is
abolished or human nature completely
changed, the formation of Independent par-
ties, with a collective motto of “Support in
return for Concession,” must continue to be
a feature of the political landscape.
The Policy of Preference to Rottenness.
To a deputation from the Dorrigo (N-S.W.)
district Minister-for-Works Gkiffith spoke
as follows last week :
In regard to Coif’s Harbor, he had spent a lot of
money in anticipation by ordering a plant for the
construction of a harbor there, and he was certain
Parliament would sanction his action. He intended
to make Coff’s Ilarhor a well-sheltered spot on the
north and Kembla a similar harbor on the south, so
that farmers should not send everything on the con-
gested lines to the city.
If a man owned a first-class motor-car, that
was in good running order, and, in prefer-
ence to travelling in it, had a ruined wheel-
barrow patched up, and hired someone to
push him about the city in that,. we should
say that he was eccentric. And if he had a
comfortable house, and yet insisted on inhab-
iting an inverted dust-box, or a damp, sea-
side cave, we should cast severe reflections
on his intellect. But hardly anyone has a
curse to fling at the N. S. Wales and Vic-
torian Ministers, who, for fear of prejudicing
the vested interests of Sydney or Melbourne,
as the case may be, blew millions of bor-
rowed money on harbors that are practically
useless to man or beast, while firmly ignor-
ing the very best things in this line that
adorn the coasts of Australia. Port Ste-
phens and Jervis Bay and Twofold Bay
have so often been given the glassy eye of
dislike and distrust, by successive N.S.W.
Ministries, Liberal and Labor, that the gen-
eral public has acquired a vague impression,
seemingly, that there is wrong
with them. That vague impression is, of
course, a false one. Jervis Bay is
probably the finest harbor N. S. Wales
possesses. Its natural advantages are so
many that it would cost an absurdly small
sum to so open it up that it would absorb
the whole of that trade between \V yalong
and the sea which now meanders expensively
to its destination -via Port Jackson. Port
Stephens is nearly as good. The bringing
of a railway to its shores, and the building of
a few wharves and similar trade necessaries
would mean that a hhge volume of IS or them
commerce would find an outlet there,
to the detriment of Sydney, possibly, but to
the vast advantage of the producer, and of
the State at large. These places are boy-
cotted by Sydney-bossed Ministries, not be-
cause they are in any way bad, but because
thev are too good. Their strong points——
their depth of water, their security in rough
weather, their vastness, and so on —are then-
weak points. Their virtues are their vices.
Thev are abhorrent to Sydney for the same
reason that the capable but, at the same
time disquietihgly beautiful housemaid is
anathema to the elderly and no longer lovelv
housewife. They are too dangerous. The result
is that the State’s (or rather the foreign
pawnbroker’s) money is wasted ceaselessly
on repairing and constructing and patching
up alleged ports, whose merit Consists
solely in the fact that if the wealth of all
the Rothschilds was expended on them
every year, they could never hope to rival
Sydney. Coif’s Harbor and Port Kembla
both fit into this category. They are so
insignificant that the latest available edition
of Harmsworth’s immense Atlas and
Gazetteer makes no mention of them. They
are the merest indentations on the face of a
rugged coast. Providence never intended
either of them to shelter so much as a sea-
gull. But because no matter what is done to
them, they can never be made sufficiently
safe or convenient to encourage the building
of an opposition city to Sydney in their
neighborhood, State Ministers feel that it
is safe to subsidise them; and because half
a loaf, or, for that matter, a fragment of
State crust, is better than no bread when
starvation looms, the Northerners and
Southerners go on clamoring for the expendi-
ture of more and more money on the futile
places. Another example of the same in-
sane tendency is afforded by the way cash
is poured out on the SiSYPHUS-like job of
keeping the State’s bar harbors in order.
Have you ever frequented a bar harbor,
gentle reader? The entrance to a bar har-
bor resembles a surfing beach, with a record-
sized line of breakers, the first of which is
about a mile from the shore. Between the
breakers the tide runs out (and likewise in)
along a narrow channel, after the fashion of
a mill-race. If the weather is perfectly calm ;
and the channel along which the tide careers
happens not to be silted up ; and the skipper
or pilot of your craft is absolutely sober, you
mnv get safely inside the bar harbor. Other-
wise you must stay outside till the sea mod-
erates ; or the channel is dredged clear ; or
the navigator sobers up. Once inside the
bar harbor you are liable to stick fast any-
where at any time, since the channel, which
is the harbor, is continually altering its for-
mation, depth and so on. Thousands are
spent yearly on these ridiculous and perilous
resorts. In fact, with the exception of Syd-
ney, the only harbors on which the otate
spends anything are inlets such as Narooma,
which can only be entered when the voy-
ager’s luck is well in, and dents or small
holes in the coast like Kembla, which were
no more intended by nature to be ports than
jaguars were intended to be black bream.
It is arguable whether it wouldn’t be a finan-
cial blessing to Australia if someone de-
stroyed Sydney utterly. For one thing, two
Sydneys would presently arise—one at Jer-
vis Bay and one at Port Stephens—in addi-
tion to the city that would begin tb re-form
at Port Jackson. Under the existing system
of preference to rottenness, as regards har-
bors, there can never be more than one
Sydney, and a collection of trade-starved
coastal villages.
Sydney is beginning to develop a shudder,v
feeling over what might have happened had
it been struck by virulent instead of modi-
fied small-pox. An enormous proportion or
the people had never been vaccinated at all,
for the Ma State doesn’t make vaccination
compulsory. Some 30 years ago the city
had a small-pox scare and went into raving
lunacy. Patients were rushed down to the
hospital shin Faraway, and shoved into any
odd corner of the vast hulk. There was no
proper system of registration or inspection.
It was merely a wild throwing on board.
When the scare was over and the Faraway
was cleaned up. dead bodies were found in
all sorts of odd corners. These were the
patients who had been heaved on to the
floating premises in the small hours of the
morning, and whom the doctors and nurses
had never heard of. Small-pox is an im-
partial disease, and has been known to at-
tack children before they were born. There
is no record of it having attacked anyone
after death, but' there it no proof that it
wouldn’t if it got the chance. Like nK)S f
other jim-jams it runs in a variety of shapes.
In confluent small-pox the eruption, instead
of coming out in small, single pimples, takes
the form of a huge patch, in which the face
seems to literally run out of shape. The dis-
figurement left by this brand is repulsive,
and somewhere about 50 per cent, of those
attacked die. One hundred per cent, of those
who get through it wish they had died, for
people cross the street rather than meet
them. Another form is the haemorrhagic, in
which the pocks and all the surfaces bleed,
and the patient is worn out by loss of blood,
and perishes of sheer weakness. But the
worst form is the malignant or toxic. Per-
haps one patient in 100 recovers, and his
face for ever afterwards is no more like a
face than it is like a pyramid or a tree. A
wooden god is human by comparison. In
the Glasgow epidemic of 1900-1901 the mor-
tality among the un-vaccinated was 516 per
1000. aDd 104 per 1000 among the vacci-
nated. The vaccinated ones had probably
neglected to be re-vaccinated when the
scourge came, but still derived considerable
benefit from the treatment. However, it is
cheering to know that vaccinated babies are
absolutely immune from any form of the
hideous complaint, and the moral is obvious.
Many of the objections to vaccination have
disappeared owing to the abolition of human
transmission. Virus from one child used to
infect the other with more than cow-pox.
and sometimes produce a crop of troubles. But
in these days pure calf-lymph only is used,
and bab.v can be scratched with impunity and
a light heart.

a log. Employees must in future pay their

ordinary fallers, £2 15s. 6d.; shootmen, as-
sistant ropemen and assistant drivers, £2
145.; and men conveying logs from water
depot to mill. £2 11s. per week of 48 hours.”

The Loans.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
See the merry little Loans—
Infant Loans!
What a world of usury their littleness con-
dones !
How they captivated members,
In the ancient, dead Decembers —worthy
members, such as Juggins, Smith, and
Robinson, and Jones !
Planted nicely in the sraveyard, under their
respective stones!
How those Ministers departed—once so jolly
and light-hearted—
In a gladsome moment started with those
friendly, little Loans—
Saying: “ Borrow — borrow — borrow! We
can pay 'em off to-morrow; and this
world is full of sorrow, so we'll raise
some little Loans!"
Note the well-developed Loans —
Grown-up Loans!
Lusty, hairy-breasted fellows, who are any-
thing but drones!
How they throng about the Nation, dazed
’twixt Drought and Condemnation, like a
Second Dispensation— not in Sinai’s
thunder-tones !
How each adult Loan discloses, tables certi-
fied by Moses — interest-tables, one sup-
poses* where the Gentile State atones!
High ambassadors of Cohen, ever growin’,
growin’, growin’—
When they’ll stop there seems no knowin’ —
even Robinson and Jones,
Who are now quite ancient bones.
Cannot send a spook to tell us if they’re
never-dying Loans,
Haunting Young Australia’s portal, or if,
after all, they’re mortal—
They look good for many laps, do those
hairy-breasted chaps—Oh, a Century,
perhaps!—
These same well-developed Loans.
Mark the legion of the Loans —
Monster Loans!
What a mighty horde of them that everyone
disowns!
How they haunt us day and night, never
absent from our sight—ranked on cen-
tre, left, and right —Hear their groans!
They are groaning to be fed—
Not a single Loan is dead, though some sixty
years are sped—just like Jones !
Smith and Robinson and Brown, who once
ruled the blessed town, years ago were
fastened down,
But their Loans —
Still inhabit this fair region ; they are grown
a mighty Legion, who are constantly
besiegin’ —New South Wales !
All the merry infant Loans — .
All the middle-agbd Loans, lusty, hairy-
breasted Loans —
All the new-made, monster Loans —
Are on deck!
There are ancient, withered Loans, like old
greybeards and bent crones, who appeal
in piping tones to be nursed
There are stalwart, whiskered Loans, who
declare they’ll grind our bones for cash
lent on telephones—things accursed!
There are also Loans galore, just recruited
by the score, who are yelling out for
more —they’re the worst! _
While the Nation mutters “Time! m a sort
of looney rhyme, with audacity sublime,
more loans rise,
Bearing interest at Four and a Half-yea,
even more —soon the thing we all abhor
(just like Jones!)
Will be here; that is to say, we shall soon
be ashed to pay! And will Cohen
heed our moans?... .
Not a heed! Kings on their thrones have
been routed by the Loans by the mel-
ancholy folly of the Loans—Loans—
Loans (19 times).
N.s.w.
O. C. CABOT.


SAVED HUNDREDS OF CHILDREN.
“My sons, Charles and John, are subject to colds,”
says Mrs. C. Carter, 33 Market Street, South Mel-
bourne (Vic.). “Some time back I would have lost
both of them with croup but for the timely advice
of Mrs. Snowden, who recommended me to give them
Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy. I tried it, and saw
the change for the better in them right away, and
they were both soon well again. 1 have seen the
lives of hundreds of children saved by Chamberlain’s
Cough Remedy.”*

ploughman worth 50s. —which is what 40s.
and “found” amounts to? Of course timber-
cutting is rather more of a manufacturing
than a rural industry. Though necessarily
carried on in the backblocks it is scarcely a
“primary” industry—the mills are run on
much the same lines as a city factory, as
far as the employees are concerned —and the
semi-manufacturing and “secondary” em-
ployer is always expected to —and does, as
a rule —pay decent wages. The primary pro-

garded as a poor, spineless being who must
always be assisted in some way or another by
State charity and must be allowed to sweat
his employees till further orders. That, at
any rate, is the reputation his press bar-
rackers are getting for him.
The suffragette captains are said to have
made all the necessary arrangements
for setting fire to London in sundry
places at a given time, but, after
getting all the phosphorus and com-
bustibles in readiness for a lovely burst of
conflagration, they spoilt the scheme by in-
viting French aviators to fly along to the
scene of action and drop bombs into the
burning. The astonished foreigners warned
the British Ambassador in Paris, and the
police seized the stock of fire-kindling mater-
ials. If this story is as true as it sounds,
the Pankiiurst Government should retire
from business. There is no reasonableness
in women who could imagine the pluckiest
of aviators willing to take a share in a
murderous job in a friendly country, or being
ready to sympathise with its organisers.
The Frenchmen were sure to give the game
away. Any half-witted man could have
told the women they were imagining a vain
thing. If the suffragettes want any bomb-
dropping from aeroplanes to be done on be-
half of their cause they will have to do it
themselves. And why not? A Pank-
hurst who winged her flight with a packet
of nitro-glycerine and dynamite drops
aboard, and let them fall from such a giddy
height that they exploded in mid-air, and
blew her into smithereens, would be a splen-
did martyr to her convictions, as well as a
useful experimentaliste. Thus far, the
effect of dropping explosives from the clouds
has not been properly tested.

Soa;e few weeks back the N.S.W. Post Office
bosses received a letter from a Sydney resi-
dent, offering, for a consideration, to show
where the Department had lost £6OOO. The
bosses having required particulars, the cor-
respondent gave dates, names, and the
amount due, which he said was £5933. Later
on, he received a communication that a spe-
cial audit by experts had been made of the
accounts of the firm mentioned, and the audi-
tors were satisfied that the informer was
barking up the wrong vegetable. Where-
upon he remarked that the G.P.O. had been
paying refunds of duty on imported postal
material where it wasn’t due. He also
sooled more dates and other information at
the Department, which, in desperation, called
up its whole reserve of investigators. They
found that the G.P.O. was out to the tune
of £5983, and the money, being applied for,
was promptly paid. The lynx-eyed citizen
who directed the postal tortoise to the moun-
tain of shortage was awarded £4O0 —a very
cheap fee in the circumstances.

It is time that Charles Gregory Wade,
ex-Premier of N.S.W., was saved from his
friends. Thus James Alexander Hogue,
ex-MiDister. about the wonderful fact that
Wade didn’t appoint himself to the job of
Chief Justice of N.S.W.
“We offered it to him,” said Mr. Hogue. “We
urged; him to take it, again and again. I have
good Reason for saying that his intimate friends and
relatives wanted him to take it. The legal profes-
sion was favorable, so was the press, and the Oppo-
sition desired it. We all wished him to take the
position, although conscious of the great loss the
party would sustaim But he refused it, for con-
scientious reasons, ft w*s a sacrifice unexampled in
the political history of the country. How many
members of the Labor party would have refused a
position worth £3500 a year, with a substantial
pension on retirement?”
Wheii a disastrous friend like Hogue drags
up unpleasant memories like that, it is up
to someone to speak to the disastrous
friend. The legal profession wasn’t favor-
able to Wade’s proposed appointment. It
regarded Wade as nothing approaching the
right size of man for the job. The
press was largely hostile. Sydney Tele-
graph, for example, was absolutely insult-
ing on the subject. The friends and rela-
tives didn’t count, being biassed parties.
And (the sacrifice wasn’t “unexampled.”
Every’ previous Attorney-General of
N.S.W. has abstained from appointing him-
self to the Bench. Wade was the only man
of them all who seemed to come near to
departing from an honorable precedent in
this respect. His possible appointment
hovered for months before he definitely and
publicly tore himself away from the tempta-
tion which men like Wise and Want, of his
own State, and Bryan O’Loghlen, of Vic-
toria, didn’t consider for a second. As
regards the gibe at the Labor party, no Labor
political lawyer has yet rushed the Bench. Cer-
tainly Griffith, in Queensland, and Bar-
ton, Isaacs and Higgins in Federal
affairs, went from politics to the Bench ; but
they weren’t Laborites.
Hardly had Cook taken his seat as Prime
Minister at the opening of Parliament than
he got a foretaste of his doom. Bruce
Smith was absent. When his name was
called for swearing-in, Bruce was still miss-
ing, and the Whip, muttering a prayer, fled
out of the hall to find him. In a few min-
utes he returned, followed by the missing
majority, which he carefully deposited in its
seat. But the chances are that, when an
important division is on, this majority will
not be so easily discovered, and Cook will
realise what sort of a mistake he made
when he neglected to hold down and anchor
Bruce Smith to the floor of the House with
a portfolio. The most urgent need of the
Cook Ministry just now is the discovery of
some means to glue Bruce Smith to his job
and keep him glued.

“Theatre-goer,” on the One form of Show
that Australia has not yet seen: —
Australian managements have chased the elusive
oof-bird with almost every conceivable class of show,
of late years, from beliow-drama to “Twelfth Night,”
and from light comedy to screaming farce; but the
one sure money-maker they have, for some reason
or other, left unexploited. There is a fortune for
the first management which puts on a Review in
this country. Reviews (or R&vues, as they are called
in their land of- origin) have been steadily ousting
the legitimate, and a good many other forms of
drama all over the world for the past five years.
The only two that have not done sensationally well
have been “J’adore Ca” and “Come Over Here” in
London; and they boomed conspicuously in Paris
and New York, which were their respective native
cities. The Review type of show, which is approved-
of abroad, would be wildly popular here, for a whole
herd of obvious reasons. Australians are extremely
self-centred. Year by year, as the national spirit
grows, they are taking less and less interest in out-
side affairs, and more and more in their own. To
the average Australian, the local trainees are far
more absorbing than the entire British army; and
Speaker Willis is a more interesting figure to them
than Poincare or the Kaiser. Incidentally, they are
the greatest newspaper readers and politicians on
earth. Not a local gag, social or political, would
miss them. Not a local character would go unrecog-
nised. A Review is really a sort of sublimated
gossip ; and a big section of Australians love gossip
more than they love drink, as many a maddened
Governor, and so forth, has had cause to know.
Australians, in their passion for broad humor and
bitter satire; in their national self-consciousness;
and in their love of seeing their own institutions
burlesqued, bear a close resemblance to the Greeks,
who first discovered the usages of the Review.
The musical comedy vogue is pasing all over the
world. More especially it is passing here. A produc-
tion with special features such as “The Waltz
Dream,” or with unusually charming music, such as
“The Merry Widow” will still make good, of course.
But anything short of exceptional—not to say, stag,
gering—merit is bound to do more and more indiffer-
ently even in a country where theatre-going is, to
many people, a form of religious rite that simply
must be performed at short intervals. Musical
comedy continues to hold its head up to a certain
extent among us, partly because it is excellently done
by the J. O. Williamson management, as regards the
acting and staging ; partly because it has no rival in
its own line. But the subject-matter has long
ceased to have any local interest whatever. And
the absence of plot, which is an advantage in cities
like London and New York, where people drop into
a show at all hours, is a source of constant irrita-
tion to Sydneyites and Melbourneites, who mostly
arrive at the theatre in a state of coldly critical
sobriety at 8 pm., and remain doggedly till 11.
The successful Australian Review will have no
fantasy about it. (Did you ever notice how abhor-
rent fantasy is to Australia? “Peter Pan,” “The
Blue Bird,” and both productions of “A Midsummer
Night's Dream” were all failures, more or less. And
now word comes that the incomparable Genee is
doing none too well in Melbourne.) It will not
portray distant places like the Court of Ilistria or
the London Embankment. It yvill cut out the fairies
and animated dolls that are featured in so many
Paris revues. And it will not contain any jests about
Winston Churchill, or Lloyd-George’s Insurance Act.
On the other hand, it will have something in the
nature of a coherent plot. The humor and the
language will be such as Australians are familiar
with. It will tshow places that everybody knows,
and faces that *9O per cent, of the audience will
delightedly recognise—the chief reasons these why
“On Our Selection” is the sufecess it is. And as I
remarked before, there will ,be the fortune of a
lifetime in it—.and other fortunes, in other shows
of the sort, even after the first tremendous success
of curiosity has achieved.

A public event which calls for considera-
tion is the meeting of the Good Roads Asso-
ciation in the vestibule of Sydney Town Hall
at four o’clofck on July *24 —Thursday, a
week hence. The roads of N.S.W. are a
disgrace. They are starved by mean rate-
payers and cheap aldermen and hungry
mayors, who think the short cut to popu-
larity is to keep the rates down to Is. in the
£, and have bogs, precipices, horrors, ruts
and nightmares, when the rate ought to be
2s. and the highways like billiard tables.
The starvation scheme doesn’t pay, for
the community loses more in bumps,
accidents, delay, wear and tear of
vehicles and ruin of horses than it
saves in rates, and it has the disgrace
thrown in. The Good Roads Association
has a word to say about these matters, and
the public is cordially invited. That public,
if it only knew what good roads are,
wouldn’t require to be invited. It would
have invited itself long ago. Incidentally,
if the mayor didn’t mend his ways, a more
intelligent set of people would unanimously
elect him ex-mayor next year.
J. C. Williamson’s death makes practically
no difference to the management of the firm
in which, for the past two years, he was little
more than a figure-head. When the Combine
was formed, J.C.W. disposed of two-thirds
of his interest in the co. to his right-hand
man, George Tallis. Since then, and even
before then, Tallis was the controlling
force, although his old boss* drew a large
salary as “governing director.” The vete-
ran’s health had been his main concern for
some time past, and, when he went travel-
ling in company with the chief “managing
director,” it was the latter who selected
such big shows as “Within the Law” and
Gen6e. George Tallis, Clyde Meynell and
Hugh Ward will run the business exactly
as before, but will gradually broaden the
lines, no doubt. Tallis owns more than half
of it, and is wealthy in investments outside
of his theatrical interests. Moreover, he
is still quite young, as self-mhde capitalists
go. He entered Williamson’s employ as
clerk, about 25 years ago, and he brought a
head for figures and a talent for organisation
with him. His name first figured on the
bill as treasurer for Williamson and Mus-
grove, at Melb. Princess’s. Then he became
business manager at the same house, and
from the time when Williamson dissolved
partnership with Musgrove, there was
rapidly-increasing scope for Tallis’s mana-
gerial ability. By the death of his old boss
and partner, he is put in the position of
recognised head of the Combine.

The Sydney Criterion will remain un-
populated till Saturday, when “Miss Hook
of Holland,” in which title it is suspected
there is concealed a vague kind of geo-
graphical joke, will re-appear.
The last week of “Bobby Burnit” at the
Little Theatre (Sydney V is. announced. On
Monday Arnold Bennett’s comedy, “The
Great Adventure,” now running in London,
will be presented. This is the story of a
great artist who, in the pretended person of
his dead valet, finds the happiness and the
woman that, as a great artist, he failed to
discover.

There will be no more “Autumn
Manoeuvres” at Her Majesty’s, Sydney,
after next week. Miss Florence Young and
her delightful voice; Miss Arundale and
her cheery wit; Miss Dorothy Brunton and
her charm and youthful beauty; likewise
Talleur Andrews and the comedian Smith,
and Roberts and the rest of the warriors
are all getting ready to load up the ,baggage-
taxies, see to the powder-supplies, and strike
camp. A hideous rumor has been permeat-
ing the lines, and percolating to the en-
trenchments, that Miss Mollie Bearde, the
splendid show-lady who spurned the dia-
monds the other day, has packed up her
impedimenta of war and left the scene of
action already. It is all very sad. As some
slight compensation, the incomparable Gende
is due to arrive at Sydney’s biggest play-
house the night after the military show
effects its retreat.

Carl Prinz’s monkeys are back at the
National Amphitheatre (Sydney) helping
Tomato, the comic donkey, to rouse the mul-
titude to hilarity. Now, if only Cook could
be induced to bring on the Socialistic tiger
what a programme it would be! Unfor-
tunately, Joseph has to keep the beast on a
hair-trigger, so to speak, in the Federal Par-
liament lobbies, Melbourne, in case the caged
Majority attempts to escape.
At the Palace (Sydney), William Ander-
son’s American burlesque co; is still doing
big business with “The Grafters.” The
show is now in its fifth week, and appears
likely to run to the completion of the season,
a month hence.

The Bulletin nurses its sore arm and
howls above the groans of the multitude
that on Friday night “Man to Man,” hither-
to at Sydney Adelphi, will quit the quaran-
tined town. “No Mother to Guide Her”
(the Her in the case being Ethel Buckley)
is due on Saturday night.
If Sydney Tivoli-ites had developed a
habit of going regularly to Clara Butt con-
certs and such-like, Lola Krasavina, the
Russian operatic singer, wouldn’t get a very
enthusiastic reception. But,’ as most of
them don’t inhabit the Town Hall on such
occasions, she gets the reception on a big
scale, and The Bulletin isn’t going to
deny her any of it. The rest of the pro-
gramme is much the same as it was last
week, but a special word might be given to
the Banvards (last nights), Billie Reeves
the drunk, and the wire-walking Havilans.
After a record run of about eight weeks,
“Quo Vadis” at Sydney Lyceum will “snatch
its time” on Friday night. On Saturday,
Spencer’s will incite Young Australia to
become patriotic by screening “Australia
Calls,” a picture which includes various
military manoeuvres, aeroplane flights by
Hart, and the burning of Sydney. The
yarn is by J. Barr and C. A. Jeffries, whom
The Bulletin knows to be good craftsmen
and individually and collectively capable of
perpetrating the best picture idea that ever
was or will be. Edison’s new invention, the
Kinetophone, which provides the dialogue
and sounds incidental to a picture, will be
introduced the same evening.
Some of England’s clanking, iron-clad
history is now being beautifully illustrated
by Lewis Waller and co., at the re-decorated
Sydney Royal. The Battle of Agincourt
tableaux, the departure scene on Southamp-
ton Quay, the Harfleur setting, and the
night view of the English lines, with the
wavering lines of glowing watch fires on the
bleak hills, are alone worth the entrance
money. Then it is worth the price of
another stall to hear Madge Titheradge,
classically garbed and throned as Chorus,
deliver the speech before the curtain.
Finally, there is Lewis Waller—for nothing,
so to speak. The distinguished London
actor looks astonishingly youthful as the
mailed Henry, and his fine performance is
practically effortless. Not till the wooing
of Katherine in the last scene does he get
a chance to descend from the heroic key:
and then he is so unaffected and smiles so
sweetly that the women in the audience go
away sighing that Shakspeare’s clashing
battle-play has not got enough love-making
in it! Miss Titheradge, charmingly youthful
and winsomely intelligent, promises 'to heat)
more local laurels on a distinguished and
well-loved name. Her speeches as Chorus
are delivered with perfect elocution and tell-
ing effect. As the shy Princess Katherine,
in the last scene, she plays up prettily to the
bluff and boisterous Harry, who really seems
glad to throw off his heroic collar and get
down to a human, everyday level. The per-
fect love-making of the two finished artists
is just glimpsed for a few brief minutes in
the sentimental ending to all the steel-clad
battle spectacles. If Shakspeare were writ-
ing to-day he would have to hurry his lead-
ing lady into sight of the audience before
the final scene. Halliwell Hobbes, the re-
strained and careful actor who was last
here with Ethel Irving, supplies two good
old-men studies as the Archbishop of Can-
terbury and the frail and doddering King of
France. George Titheradge, a proud father
in mail, as Duke of Exeter, arrests the
three traitors on the Quay in a dignified
manner, and describes the finding of the
battle-scarred bodies in a heart-breaking
speech never excelled by mortal policeman.
Evelyn Beerbohm (Pistol), a swaggering
scarecrow in a red cloak and an electrically
disturbed wig and moustache, reminiscent of
Will Whitburn, gets the most out of his
picturesque role. Alec F. Thompson, a
little soldierly figure with earden produce in
his helm, is pleasantly effervescent as the
talkative Fluellen. Charles Ouartermaine
(the Dauphin). Edmund Waller (Nvm).
and Harry Halley (Bardolnh) are others
who demand honorable mention.

At Adelaide Royal the comic opera season
finished with “Florodora,” in which Blanche
Browne elected to be Dolores, and Palotta
scored once more in “Tact.” Now it is “Kis-
met,” with the heavy mounting skilfully
adapted to the small stage. Adelaide grum-
bles at the raised prices, but cannot stay
away. The booking was even stronger at
the end of the run than at the beginning.
Incidentally a Kismet Ball at the Exhibi-
tion provided a lot of amusement, with good
money for the Children’s Hospital. The
closing of the Royal for one night gave visit-
ing players a chance to wander, and Madge
Temple, at the Tivoli, calling on “married
men who love their wives” to stand up and
sing, had the pleasant surprise of a solo from
Jack Cannot in the stalls. O Hana San is
still at the Tivoli, and the powerful Bell-
clairs have just come in. At the King’s is
Paul Gorden, on a return visit, tipsily wire-
walking ; also Armstrong and Howarth, and
Vernon and Mack. Wondergraph remains at
the Town Hall, its new Hindley-street palace
being well advanced, but not ready, and relies
largely on local football matches ; but West’s
peg away solidly with “Quo Vadis,” of which
fine drama the public can seemingly stand
unlimited weeks.
** * *
The dance and song festivities at Her
Majesty’s (Melb.) were varied and further
enlarged last Saturday, although “Coppelia”
remained in the centre of the bill as before.
In the first place “The Daring of Diane”
was substituted for the mild, melodious story
of Suzanne and her cigarette habit. “Diane,”
which is a mere trifle, lasting only 20 min-
utes, introduced Miss Hilda Vining in the
name part. If the audience had thought
more of the operetta the merit of Miss
Vining would have been more generally
noticed. Then, after “Coppelia” had added
one more laurel to Genre’s local crown, that
indefatigable little lady figured as fairy-in-
chief of “Les Sylphides,” a muslin-skirted
ballet that moves to the music of Chopin.
The star’s chief support—actually as well
as figuratively speaking—is Alex. Volinin
(stated to be “the vision of Chopin”). whilst
Halina Schmolz and little Miss Novotna
are occasional soloists who supply ripping
tripping items. For instance, Miss Schmolz
gets her daintiest effect by seeming to be
blown backwards on her toes. Gen6e, how-
ever, gets all the pirouetting and posing that
need young man Volinin’s assistance. In
her poetic picture work, Gen6e is as dis-
tinctively light-hearted as in her humorous
pantomime, and each succeeding Saturday
she seems an even more ethereal body than
she was a week previously. But the fine art
of the principals goes to lessen the illusion
of the ballet as a whole. It is too chaste
for the surroundings. The rank and file
girls twitter not ungracefully on their toes,
yet they lack sylphideousness. They are
darlings of this world rather than denizens
of the sylvan groves of fancy. There is
nothing elusive about them. One never
thinks of them as being likely to fly up to
Heaven or melt into space. Friday is the
last night of “Coppelia.” which will have to
make room for an Oriental ballet on Satur-
day. The Divertissements are unchanged
and presumably the Swan dance of Halina
Schmolz. and lier delightful pas de flirtation
with Volinin, will be encored for the rest of
the season.

the Law,” in its ninth week at Melbourne

“On Our Selection” continues to fill Mel-
bourne King’s with mirth and money, lhe
programme refrains from classifying this
lucrative show. It is not described as drama,
melo-drama, comedy or play, but as a
Quaint Story of a Settler’s Life, and under
any other definition it would get there just
the same. The reason for the success is
shared by sundry causes, whereof the ab-
surdity of Dave’s nose is not more im-
nortant than the battered respectability ot
Dad’s belltopper. Public interest is stimu-
lated by Dad’s crowning glory. People go
away Yvondering how he ever contrived to get
into Parliament with a hat like that.

* * * *
“The Girl Who Loved a Soldier,” at Mel-
bourne Princess’s, is an infuriated play of
Sydney life, aggravated by strong resem-
blances to almost every other stage howl
that the writer knows. Despite its harbor
scenery and its bits of atmosphere from
Chowder Bay, Darling Point, and other
spots within the quarantine area, many Eng-
lish' effusions would have no hesitation in
calling it sister and giving it a fraternal
hug. Still it must be said to the credit of
the show that it has a strain of hyperbolic
exaggeration which is all its own, and there
is one scene wherein the author shows a
genius for imaginative sacrilege by dragging
in Sydney’s haughtiest Anglican cathedral
by the scruff of the belfry, so to speak. One
Toby Trackem, a boy scout, is the play’s
most prevalent institution, and he is always
on hand to save the hero from becoming a
heap of remains on the moist floor of Sydney
Harbor. Toby’s stunts in the matter
of hero-salvage might earn his creator a
fine of 7d. for larceny from current dramas;
but he is a cheerful lunatic whom one
Crossley shows to be in great need of a
voice jube and some dramatic culture.
George Cross, as th 6 heroic artillery private
with a habit of inventing submarines, has to
keep his usual loveliness in reserve, and
Lloyd is the wicked stockbroker with a fond-
ness for large and baffling misdemeanors —
like, say, cheeking a policeman or pinching
a canary. The only other people who call
for comment are Miss Agnes Keogh, who
plays Olga Lazaroff with engaging uphol-
stery, and C. J. Ennis, who unfortunately
brings to the portrayal of the heavy dad
the physique of a lead pencil. The others
are as flat as last month’s beer or this
month’s Booligal.
“The Modern Prodigal” leaped on to the
screen at Melbourne Majestic on Saturday,
and, after the manner of his kind, suddenly
tore away home to find that his mother’s
cupboard was bare and her bank account
emnty. Of course, the welcome stranger
succors the author of his being. With the
coin that used to be spent in riot and gam-
ing at the Hog’s Husk Club he puts the
commissariat and the bank account on their
feet and becomes a whale for piety and early
bed. Taken altogether, the film is a fine
pictorial tract.

Lucy Gillet has arrived at Melbourne
Opera House with her packet of juggling
tricks and her tendency to somersault on
the slightest provocation. The. young lady
won’t make Cinquevalli feel envious, but her
large, comfortable self-confidence and her
taking manner of using her understandings
go a long way to make her a pleasing en-
tertainment. Miss Irma Caron, the other
fresh face, fiddles the time-worn Barcarolle
creditably enough, but isn t sumptuously
equipped in a vocal sense. Other turns,
human, animal and feathered, are very
passable or even more so, the only drawback
being A. G. Spry’s poor, wheezy cross-talk
with an accomplice in the audience.
AT POVERTY POINT.
Private Boxer”: One begins to believe the Lon-
don managers’ assertion that everything connected
with the stage has become extravagantly dear of
late. The damages in breach-of-promise cases
brought by stage beauties, for instance.. Only the
other clay a Miss Daisy Markham (the name seems
to suit, somehow) wrested £50,000 from a Marquis
who preferred to part with his money rather than
marry his actress. A few years back Daisy’s lacer-
ated heart would, perforce, have had to be healed
with about half of 50,000 pence. Even the rather
famous Miss Fortescue, when she brought her action
for breach—a cause celebre of the period-—against
Lord Garmoyle (“Gumboil” to the irreverent) was
awarded only £lO,OOO.
Nellie Bramley, who helped to make “The Monk
and the Woman” boom for Marlow, is a very sick
girl nowadays, having been operated on four times
during the last few weeks. Although she has not
been on the boards for long (she is only 23), the
young Australian has done some good work, and has
got near the top of the tree in local melodrama.
“S.T.S.”: The one speech in “Milestones” which
seems to ring absolutely untrue is that of the poli-
tician, that his intended marriage may “kill the
Labor party.” Consider it as a personal matter.
At the age of 50, you are impelled irresistibly to
marry your old sweetheart. You being a Labor
leader, and she the daughter of a Conservative
baronet and widow of a peer, it is not an ideal alli-
ance, and, as soon as you have fallen into each
other's arms, misgivings set in. “This will kill
father,” she sighs; and you, as in duty bound, re-
assure her: “The old gentleman is pretty tough.”
And then you add (incredibly): “It may kill the
Labor party!” No man would admit, or even
realise, that his domestic affairs could kill his party.
Probably even Parnell, of whom it was very nearly
true, would have shied at that way of phrasing it.
It is the remark of a cynical person in the opposite
camj>—not of the successful lover and leader most
concerned.
“U.S.”: J.C.W.’s death recalls the parallel between
his Dutch “Struck Oil” and Jefferson’s Dutch “Rip
Van Winkle.” Williamson bought “The Deed,” ajid had
it turned round into “Struck Oil.” Its success in San
Francisco justified his coming to Australia. Coppin,
Stewart, Hennings and Greville refused him a mod-
est salary. Australia opened London to him. Jeff-
erson, 12 years earlier, had re-shaped a crude “Rip
Van Winkle” drama. He made a success with it in
San Francisco, and so was emboldened to try Aus-
tralia. Manager Tolano, at Sydney Vic., re-
fused him a modest salary. And he did so well on
his own that he was also able shortly to steer for
London. Here he had the piece re-written again by
Boucicault. It ran 150 nights. “Struck Oil” ran
100.
“The Second Mrs. Tanqueray,” being exactly 20
years old in May, was revived on Derby Day by
George Alexander and Mrs. Pat Campbell, the origi-
nal Tanqueray and Paula.
Beerbohm Tree, at Her Majesty’s, London, is
going to produce “Joseph and His Brethren” —-the
first Biblical play duly licensed by the Lord Cham-
berlain. This recalls that a play on the same sub-
ject by George Rignold and Rev. George Walters,
successfully performed in Sydney and Melbourne,
was refused a license when Rignold took it to Lon-
don, on the ground that there was a “long-estab-
lished rule of the Lord Chamberlain’s Department
not to license any drama avowedly drawn from
Scriptural sources.” Evidently somebody has in-
spected the rule and found that the moths have
made holes in it large enough fo let a knight
through, where a mere untagged actor and a parson
pushed and scrambled in vain.
Arthur Tranton, a popular though not widely ad-
vertised member of the Asche-Brayton co., died
other day, while on the way back to the Cold Country.
Tranton, who was Oscar’s understudy, appeared for a
time in the Melbourne production of “Kismet,” but
soon had to go into a private hospital, where he was
a prisoner for months. Then he made an effort to
reach England and his wife, but did not get as far as
Port Said. Cancer was the trouble. Asche behaved
in a most generous manner from start to finish of the
sad business—without taking his press agent into his
confidence either.
“Harrison O.”: London has had another “moral-
ity” play on the lines of “Everywoman.” “Every-
wife” is the name of the thing, and it was hatched
in Yankeeland. Everyhusband, the hero, falls under
the evil influence of the villain, Rhyme. Thereupon
he disregards the copy-book maxims of Reason, who
appears to fill the shoes of the gloomy Nobody,
whom Eardley Turner used to represent as a sort of
peripatetic Dante. Everyhubbv leaves Everymissus
at the bidding of a fast cat, Jealousy; and Happi-
ness, the Everycouple’s serving-maid, promptly gives
notice. Later, the breach between man and missus
is healed, and Everywife, presumably, makes tracks
for the nearest registry office in search of Happiness.
There’s a ring of truth about the theatrical story
that Volinin, the Russian dancing man, received a
note from a Melbourne Sassiety woman asking him to
write her a long letter and “tell her many th'ngs.”
Volinin, the story goes, did write and tell her
many things—in Russian —which she probably would
be sorry to get translated into English. When a
stage-struck matron (these “bold hussies” are mostly
married) implores a strange young man to tell her
many things, it would have to be a very unintelli-
gent foreigner who didn’t know which thing was the
subject nearest to her heart.
“0.K.”: “The Trial Before Herod,” which Herbert
Grimwood staged at Melbourne Auditorium the other
afternoon, is a crude trifle of a “Mystery play” as
compared with the “Everyman” that had an im-
pressive one performance at the Town Hall some
years ago. Yet it might have seemed worth while if
the introductory lecture by Professor Wallace had
been dramatically forceful or historically interesting.
As it was, the meagre audience felt bored to death
before the mystery play (why “mystery,” where the
story is so simple and straightforward?) started its
30-minutes’ course. The University professor deliv-
ered in dry monotone, like a lad reading from a
school book, a dreary string of facts which he had
committed to paper. Comments and quotations came
all the same from this self-satisfied teacher of the
young. With his eyes fixed on his notes, he uttered
his bald statements. The matter a"d of bis
discourse were equally dismal. He had no points to
make, nor could his voice have made cnem it diey had
been there. The prof.’s lecture, considered as food
for the intellect and stimulant for the imagination,
amounted to bread and water. Then some mock
monks marched down the hall, chanting Latin, and
placed themselves upon their marrowbones on either
side of the proscenium. Grimwood, in the character
of Herod, made a laudable endeavor to shake things
up. Always a difficult actor to understand when he
is declaiming his loudest, Grimwood, in the thinly-
populated auditorium, was the profoundest mystery
in the play. His enunciation, though doubtless the
same as usual, seemed to out-Herod Herbert. One
could only guess that he was asserting his kingly
majesty and protesting against Roman authority in
Jerusalem. His courtiers and a couple of Roman
soldiers helned to make his meaning clear, but, after
all, the only impressive feature of the proceedings
was the speechless Christ whom the soldiers set before
the King. It was not so much that actor Hignett
was picturesquely made up for the part, and mildly
dignified in his deportment. The strength of his
impersonation was in his silence. Herod ranted and
all the other people talked, or jeered, or did some-
thing in their turns, but the Figure merely stood still
and held His tongue. He was the embodiment of
masterly inactivity. There is nothing really Great
in being one still, silent Figure amidst an excited
gathering, but it seems immense. The effect of
hidden force and essential superiority is attained.
If Hignett had raved and gesticulated whilst Grim-
wood struck an attitude of cold indifference, the posi-
tions of majesty and impotence would have been
reversed in their stage presentment. One could sym-
pathise with the natural exasperation of Herod, but
there was no denying his belittlement. When one
King of the Jews was shouting, the Rival Claimant
to the title reigned supreme. He wore the crown of
muteness and the robe of impassivity, and He didn’t
need the white light on His face to make him Lord
of all. The moral was a convincing replv to the con-
troversial question in Melb. Age : “Do We Talk Too
Much ?”
This paper hates to spread jam on its own
bread-and-butter; but this goes, more in
defence of Bulletin readers than of Bul-
letin producers :
Allow me, as a clergyman, who has, after 40 years
work in different States, just retired from active
pastoral service, to enter an emphatic protest against
the Rev. Godfrey H. Smith, of Sale, and his testi-
mony against The Bulletin, from his experience in
North Queensland.
In issue July 3, the Rev. Smith states: “My ex-
perience with many of its close readers, among the
scattered population of North Queensland, forced upon
me the evidence of a cynical, pessimistic and morbid
spirit produced in them by its continued perusal.”
The present scribe has wandered into shearing-sheds
and stations in North Queensland, in Burketown, Clon-
curry, Croydon and Camoweal; and everywhere, in
station, shearing-shed, pub and parsonage, he has
found The Bulletin in welcome circulation. Fur-
ther, The Bulletin is the only print that finds a
way into several homes in the Never Never Land,
and I have never found a brighter, kinder, more
charitable, and more truly Christian folk than the
aforesaid readers of this so-called “print of Barab-
bas.” I found no trace of the “morbid spirit”
mentioned, but rather a genial humor and a light-
heartedness which, I am sure, was due to a great
extent, to the humor, genial criticisms and sarcastic
squibs and gibes at hypocrisy, tomfoolery and black
bigotry that appeared weekly in the pages of The
Bulletin. If the “morbid spirit” were generated
by pessimistic newspapers, I should stake my repu-
tation the account should go down to prints like
the Church Times, of Sale, and not to a paper
brightened weekly by Australia’s best humor and
brightest sketches, and salted by common sense and
a thorough national spirit that should rouse up the
most “morbid spirit” to enthuse over the joy of
living and the glory of being a nation-buijder. Let
The Bulletin still fleece hypocrisy, expose shams,
crush bigotry, continue the supply of humor, and it
will do good in bringing sunshine, common sense,
practical charity, and the true national spirit into
the lives of many Christians in North Queensland,
and maybe into the homes of a few atheists in Sale.
STANZAS TO A SILENT MUSICIAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
By her dancing, Genee is able to tell a story, and
to suggest many emotions. Her feet seem to talk.
—Melbourne paper.
Speak to me only with thy feet,
And bliss will then be mine,
Beyond that won from Helen’s kiss,
Or Circe’s golden wine;
For rich and rare beyond Compare
Is converse such as thine.
Oh, skip a chanson through for me
In soundless numbers sweet,
Or trip a silent lullaby,
My pleasure to complete ;
For tongues are numb and lips are dumb
Beside your fluent feet.
Dance out your elfin melodies,
That ring through Beauty’s bower.
Oh, talk to me in cadence soft;
While hour by tuneful hour
Unnoticed flies, with greedy eyes,
Your discourse I’ll devour!
VICTORIA.
HARRISON OWEN.
THE PARIAH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My bearing is not brave and high ;
My progress through the crowded city
Is noticeably skulking. I
Imagine strangers filled with pity.
I feel that each is my detractor;
All fancy me a malefactor.
I penetrate my fav’rite club
It strikes me that my friends avoid me;
The shifty look, the furtive snub
Are handed me. I grow annoyed ; see
Contempt in mere exuberances,
Hate in the friendliest of glances.
Last night I dined at Paris House
And asked the chef for mayonnaise: blame
An erring waiter for the douse
My spirits got when Sauce Bearnaise came.
Once I’d have cursed—-I frankly state it:
Last night I took the stuff, and ate it.
A humbler, sadder outcast you
Shall not discover than the writer ;
He battles vainly with the view .
That all regard him as a blighter.
The cause need but be adumbrated;
He has not yet been vaccinated.
N.s.w.
D.B.J.

SOCIETY
In the Sydney Telegraph of Monday the
poor old Pope got prodded Here is
the report:—
HOW CAN THEY ESCAPE HELL?
The Roman Catholic people should be no-license
advocates to a man. Either that or the infallibility
of the Pope is a fiction. So Rev. John Paterson
argues. At the Protestant Hall yesterday,- he said
that the last Pope was a prohibitionist, and when
he was elected to that position the first thing he did
was to forbid absolutely the sale of intoxicating
liquors in the Vatican. In the only part of the world
where he had full temporal powers the Pope enforced
absolute prohibition. What the speaker wanted to
know was how, if the Pope was infallible, and he
was a prohibitionist, all the Roman Catholics were
not going to Hell for not being prohibitionists also ?
“But they tell me I don’t understand the doctrine of
infallibility,” he added.
After all. the doctrine of infallibility is
very simple, even if it may seem rather
illogical. Thus it is claimed that David
was infallible while he was writing the
Psalms, all Scripture being inspired; but
that he was not infallible while he was steal-
ing Uriah’s wife. And Solomon was infal-
lible in the period of inspiration during
which he supplied his learned contribution
to Holy Writ, but not when he fell away
into idolatry. And when the patriarchs, the
judges, the prophets, and the apostles had
all had their turn at being God’s representa-
tives on earth the Pope of Rome and the
Patriarch of Constantinople took on the
contract. The last apostle was elected to
fill a vacancy, and the process of election
has gone on, with more or less success, ever
since. It was the choice of Matthias by ballot
which started the effort to continue the line of
inspiration, and the effort continues to this
day. The infallibility problem remains just
where it was in the time of. the second
Israelitish king. When the Religious Poten-
ttate at Rome- decides on matters of doctrine
lie is supposed to be inspired. When he de-
cides to be, in his own personal capacity, a
vegetarian or a teetotaller, he is presumably
looking after his own private affairs. He
doesn’t demand that all Roman Catholics
should be teetotallers aged 60 because he is
a teetotaller aged 60. Inspiration and in-
fallibility are vast and difficult subjects, and
the two men who complicated them worst
of all were Father David and Son Solomon.
“Catoptric” on some things the imported
expert can’t be expected to know :
The imported expert recommended a dado of un-
watched lights around our coast. These lights jig
along for quite a while, and are inspected every few
years —before, say, election time. South Aus. has a
couple of the let-’em-rip outfits, and last week an
inquisitive official, ambling round the littoral, missed
one of them. After a hunt he found it underneath
a colony of shags who had selected the beacon as a
residence, owing no doubt to the rent-raising habit
of the present-day landlord. Anyway, in the place
where the light used to be was a crowd of old shags,
young shags and middle-aged shags, shags of all sects
and shags of all sexes, living in tenement houses,
flats and villa residences, the whole constructed so
as to obscure the beacon. The irritated officer de-
molished the suburb and then left —to give the birds
a chance to restore their happy homes. It seems to
me that the expert under-rated our Australian- fauna.
I remember once, going down to the South Head
light, I found it obscured by about 135,000,000
beetles; and then there are such things as the Aus-
tralian Bight sea-serpent and the highly intelligent
iatUigator that wouldn’t let Johnson, M.P., sit on it.
'N® imported person can be expected to make allow-
ances: for what these and other strange local reptiles
are ’liable to do.
“Jeames” : The London Mail, which has
just been called upon to pay Sir Arthur
Vicars £5OOO for suggesting that he had
connived at the theft of ’the Dublin Crown
jewels, and was more friendly than circum-
stances warranted with Lady Haddo, is a
kind of Modern Society elevated to the
Nth degree. Modern Society was warned
off Smith’s book-stalls, all over the United
Kingdom, in the later Victorian epoch, for
mild little chroniques scandaleuses that would
be blue-pencilled by A. Moreton Mandeville,
the editor of the Mail, as too dull and home
circlish altogether. The Mail specialises
in this sort of paragraph
We Want to Know: —
If the tall son of the peer —that frequenter of the
Divorce Court —is not a chip of the old block ; and
what the mother of the pretty girl from the “Revue
thinks of his attentions to her daughter; and
whether she prefers “Irish whisky” to “blue blood.
Whether Mrs. Asquith enjoyed herself at the
Palace the other evening ; and which she thinks the
more fascinating, Pavlova or Maud Allan ? ...
If the young w.fe of the well-known Admiral
found out that her husband was visiting the night
club with the pretty lady from the- Empire Revue,
and if she persuaded a junior lieutenant .to take her
there, and what happened at the meeting; and if
the lieutenant now feels sorry for himself —and also
the Admiral.
Stuff like the above, where it isn’t meaning-
less —in the sense of having no relation What-
ever to fact or even to actual people —is as
financially perilous as dynamite. The first
two pars would be comprehended fully by
every Society gossip in the three kingdoms ;
and they are as unpleasant as regards their
inner meanings as the mind of man could
conceive. The third may be apocryphal -as
indeed about half of these efforts are. Sir
Arthur Vicars, the latest to be accused
of stealing, or assisting to steal, the Dublin
Crown jewels (practically everyone of any
prominence who was about the Castle in
1907 has now been charged with the theft of
these gauds), was Ulster King at Arms. The
Mail’s story was that his mistress had got
jealous of Lady Haddo and given him away.
Lady Haddo was an attractive widow before
her marriage to the 27-year-old son of
Lord Aberdeen. She gave evidence that she
had only spoken to Vicars once in her life;
and the Mail caved suddenly in, and ad-
mitted that its informant was a liar —an ab-
solute habit, I have noticed, in the social
informant Tine of business.
A case that happened in South Melbourne
the other day threw a dun-colored light on
the value of Victorian police evidence. Two
constables had seen a man who gave the
name of Patrick Mullins leaving the Sham-
rock Hotel on a Sunday, so they took out
a summons charging him with being illegally
on the premises, and then went to the resi-
dence of one Andrew Murphy and handed
that citizen the brief. Heaven knows why
Murphy accepted it, but he did, and came to
court in due course. There the two con-
stables swore that the man in the dock was
the man they had seen leaving the beer foun-
dry at an illegal hour. The man in the dock
denied this—he had been at home in bed at
that moment, he said, and his name wasn’t
Mullins. The Bench scrutinised Murphy
thoughtfully; but he was quite sober, and
quite positive, so the case was adjourned for
further inquiry. When it came up again
the police admitted they had “made a mis-
■ take.” Murphy’s tale was quite correct, and
thq man they wanted was in reality a Ger-
man named Siemens, who went by the
name of Mullins. Murphy was, of course,
discharged, and apparently that ends the
matter. But it certainly shouldn’t. A loud
rebuke ought to be coming to someone.

Of the 30 or 40 samples of the art of L.
Bernard Hall (Director of the National Gal-
lery, Melbourne), at present being exhibited
at Angus and Robertson’s gallery, Sydney,
the really charming are- the still-life can-
vases and the interiors. In these the artist
is at his height, and warms up an admira-
tion that stands with more or less chilled
feet before such nudes as in “Under the
Colosseum” and the now somewhat famous
“Sleep.” In these two, as in others ex-
hibited, the draughtsmanship is the attrac-
tive force ; the common, familiar feelings of
the ordinary passer-by find themselves alien-
ated. In “Sleep” the vulgar eye is held by the
marvellous sheen of a metal shield, its reflec-
tion on the model’s flesh, a brass-moulded
bowl and a handful of figs <m a table. Of
all the exhibits, the writer would first choose
“The Old Kitchen” —a cracked and smoke-
aged fireplace and a few pots and pans, yet
so exquisitely has the feeling of homeliness
been limned that, as Hugh McCrae put it
the other day, “you can sniff the smoke.”
That gem can be purchased for 18 guineas ;
“Sleep” is marked at 300 'guineas; “The
Quest,” ditto. The last-mentioned relates to
O. Khayyam’s lines : —-
I sent my Soul through the Invisible
Some letter of that After-life to spell.
The relationship isn’t clear. As an inter-
preter, the Soul (a shadowy female faintlv
illuminated with colored halos) may con-
sider her drink-selling license cancelled and
her billiard table confiscated. Presumably
the large sphere upon which she is perched
is the Invisible, but it doesn’t look like the
article. Of the interiors, a sun-fingered cor-
ner in the artist’s house is an envious piece
of work; and, of the portraiture, the artist’s
wife. The .“still-life (melon)” and
“Japoneries” are a winning double in their
line, and their line, as has been said before,
is No. 1, topside. There are, on the other
hand, one or two canvases hung which can-
not be observed with equanimity; but for
the sake of being in harmony with the Peace
Society for once in a lifetime The Bulletin
refrains from mentioning them. The ex-
hibition, which ranks as one of the important
of the year, will remain open until July 22.

Police Commissioner Sainsbury says he
did not publish the names of the ladies and
gentlemen who were found in the “swift”
Melbourne restaurants the other Sunday
morning, because he wished to avoid creating
domestic trouble- For this Sainsbury will
be remembered in the prayers of many. It
would have been a distressing complication
had the world been informed on Monday
morning that it was not Smith’s wife Smith
was supping with at the Fish and Chips
Palace, but the wife of Jones, whereas the
lady supping with Jones at the other estab-
lishment was not really Mrs. Jones, hut the
wife of Smith. Families do get so mixed up
in the excitement of a police raid.

—Recent cable.
Lo ! once more the dreadful babel
Of the latest news by cable
Sets the whole world quivering ;
Tells how females, wild, pedantic,
Tried the sacrilegious antic
Of assaulting George the King!
This is crime of wrong begotten ;
Oh ! the state of Hingland’s rotten—
Hingland, mistress of the sea!
Yet, methinks, that such a story
Tends to heighten George’s glory,
Leaves his fame less transitory,
Makes him known to you and me —
Yea, the violence of the sex
Puffs the name of Georgios Rex,
Lifts him from obscuritee !

THE VICTIM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She sat within the waiting car.
A sorrow seemed to rise
Like thick, grey, ocean mist, and mar
The splendor of her eyes.
No rest her stricken heart could find ;
She hated contact with her kind.
Long looked I on her sweet, sad face,
And saw her spirit’s stress
If some rude passer dared to place
A hand upon her dress;
For one was here whose faith in man
Was smothered under sorrow’s ban.
The young conductor lingered there.
Her thoughts were far from him.
He tapped her arm, and murmured “Fare!’’
Her face went hot and grim.
“Stand back,” in horror screamed the lass —
“I’ve just been vaccinated, ass!”

Suffragettism has added one picturesque
expression to the British language—“the
Cat and Mouse Act.” When the hunger-
strike became chronic something had to be
done to, prevent the Law being more of Mr.
Bumble’s donkey than usual. So it was
enacted that if the lady-incendiary who was,
perhaps, on a three months’- —say, 1)1 da vs’—
sentence, reduced herself in, pay, five days to
a state of dangerous weakness through lack
of soup and the like, she would be released—
not definitely, as was the case with the early
hungerers, but until she had put bn suffi-
cient flesh at home to stand another spell of
gaol and lack of victuals. It was reckoned
that if she was arrested and released
19 times, she wbpld have about hunger-
struck hei* way Through her sentence. The
suffragettes Complain that this is the way
the cat treats ,the mouse—hence'the pictur-
esque name given to the Act. Mrs. Rank-
hurst ha§ already been moused, three times,
and isn’t half through her Sentence with the
cat as yet. By-and-b.ye some wOak member
of the sisterhood will rat on the movement
and humbly eat her victuals in gaol. That
is to say, she will turn dog. The hunger-
strike, by the way, is a Russian device, and
used to be much practised in the political
prisons of Siberia. When’'such things existed.
It was generally a protestvagainst scanty
rations or cruelty. And, so far as is known,
the bloated tyrant was defeated every time.
“Tara" : The Battle of the 'Boyne and the
Glorious Twelfth of July are round again,
and, as usual, there are comparisons be-
tween lovely, enlightened Belfast and other
parts of Ireland. , Turn up, then, a book
just published ori" “The Lady Next Door,”
by Begbie, a Protestant, and judge the fic-
tion from the fact,,. In the mean streets
where Belfast’s ■ linen workers dwell the
faces are terrible —either fierce, hard, cruel,
embittered, or sad, wretched, hopeless, de-
spairing. The young people are small, pale,
and flat-chested, bloodless dwarfs. Their
home-life is spent in terrible dog-holes, as
bad as those of the festering purlieus of Can-
ton or Calcutta. Probe, and’ you will find
the most abominably sweated workers of
the world. Before six in the morning, when
work begins, an army of women and girls
hurry to the linen factories, shivering with
cold,,and dull with bodily want. In the dry-
spinning rooms the air is dusty with a chok-
ing fluff. In the wet-spinning rooms the
moist heat is so great that a person unac-
customed to it faints. Most of the females

“F§ng-sliui”: The foreign settlement in
Shanghai is to China what Geneva is to
Europe—a refuge for all the irreconcilables,
reactionaries and disgraced officials in the
republic. Here they can plot in perfect
security, bar accidents, under the protection
of the "settlement police. Occasionally, how-
ever, there are accidents.
The Central Government
sends a bomb and blows up
an inconvenient general or
a superfluous ex-Minister.
General Hsii, the Tiger, an
ardent collector of antiques,
received a vase, a real old
piece of Ming, through the
post the other day, and after
the explosion you could not
distinguish between Hsii, the
and Yang, his ser-
vant, and as for the old piece
of Ming it simply “was
not.” It was just a piece of
old Bang. President Yuan-
Shih K’ai is . alleged to have
a hand in these things. Each
assassination is followed by
an outbreak of decrees - ex-
pressing the most passionate
grief arid astonishment, as

From George Cecil Russell, of Gembrook
(Vic.) :—•
Discussing a recent “record” English picture sale,
“Carmine” (B. 3/7/’l3) has hit the price of Rom-
ney’s portraits wonderfully. There are two female
portraits which have been much sought—“ Miss
Close,” the. property of some people who wouldn’t
quote a price, and the “Lady Russell and Child,”
for which one of the Rothschilds offered some years
ago- £14,000 guineas. To show how values have
moved up, all Romney got for the Lady Russell was
40 guineas. The head of our family still has the
picture with the receipt by Romney and the written
offer of 14,000 guineas pasted on the back.
“Carmine’s” shot ( £15,000) is therefore marvel-
lously near the mark.
Quite a number of quaint old feuds—
some of them local, some imported, some
about religion, some about politics, some
about a girl, some about a cow which a
Scottish subject of James IV. stole on the
Border from an English subject of Henry
Vlll.—still survive in Australia. Tasmania
is rich in them. A murder of a few years
ago was the outcome of a feud in which there
had been many ambushments, threats, mid-
night alarms, bye-drinkings and surprises.
The latest trouble at Mathinna, which led
recently to the slaying and burning of a
farmer, has revealed the fact that many resi-
dents of that region go about armed, and
that the locality is mobilised all the time,
so to speak.
After all the fuss made over the raid on
the fizzy Melbourne cafes—a raid on a Carl-
ton or South Melbourne sly-grog shop would
have been carried out with less advertise-
ment but more effect —the Law is no further
ahead than when it started. After being
unnecessarily arrested the unfortunate em-
ployees,, who merely carried the liqfior to
the customers, were duly charged, and when
the first two cases had been dismissed the
Law perceived that it had dropped into a
cavity. '-Now it is proceeding against the
proprietors, or managers; but whether it
will have any better luck remains to be seen.
If it doesn’t, any cafe or restaurant will
apparently be at liberty to sell liquor at all
hours of the night, and Parliament will be
desired to amend the Licensing Act and
close up the orifice in haste.
John Policeman has been having some
exciting experiences in Sydney lately. The
other day he was guarding an alleged mur-
derer whose bodily weakness had laid him
on his back in the North Shore Hospital.
Observing his charge to be asleep, John
turned round for a drink of water. The
exant length qf time he took in turning
round, irrigating the interior and walking
back round his feet has not been ascertained,
but when he next cast- his sleuthful eye
upon the place whereon he had deposited
the property of Rex, it was minus the
goods. By next day John had nearly worn
his most useful ends off in searching the
hills, caves, gullies, summits, apexes and
other mixed scenery for miles, around. About
this time, no news having been received from
the front, the hospital people decided to
dismantle the bed of the departed State
guest, and lo I likewise ’struth! when the
nurse turned over the sheets .there lay the
identical! The next stirring entertainment
took place aboard one of Johnson’s sub-
urban trains (or whatever they are). A
policeman was escorting a prisoner to Can-
terbury, when the inconsiderate captive
dived overboard into the dusk that was
being left behind at the rate of 150 miles
an hour. When the officer returned some
time later with a search party and a shovel
and broom, the only collection taken up was
an old hat with a dent in it. And, in these
prosaic days, a policeman who reaches the
lockup with nothing more substantial than
an old hat is up against something extra in
the way of explanation.
A case in the Vic. County Court recently
threw light on the brilliant arid copious ways
of the gravediggefi,. It appeared that cer-
tain undertakers, to'.advertise their business,,
notified the public that they proposed to inter-
“the late Mr. Arthur Branston” at the-
Necropolis, Spring, Vale, on January 1, at:
3 p.m., etc., etc. Apparently they hit om
the name of Arthur Bransfon because there-
was no such person in the directory; but itt
happened that there was. a-William Arthur-
John Branston, commonly called Arthur, andl
it also happened that he had been in poor-
health for some time. This Branston suedl
for damages in a general way—and Judge
Box non-suited him, with costs, though he-
was able to prove that his “widow” had beem
the subject of many condoling messages.. So
the coffin firm may go- on its well-advertised
way unrebuked.

“Premo” : We in Melbourne are used to the
deriding of our trams. Still I think the fat
conductor was hardly fair the other cold
morning when he ran alongside his car in
order to stimulate his circulation.
Melbourne’s City Fathers are going to
lock the door of their stable, as it were, now
that the horse is embezzled. Having dis-
covered that the new Auditorium has taken
their best concert clients away from the cold,.
draughty hall, they have decided to brighteni
and warm its neglected interior with 16 elec-
tric radiators. Next winter, perhaps, they
will conceive some means of reducing the-
barn’s draughtiness. Even Melbourne’s city
councillors must live and learn, otherwise
death will overtake them and they will
perish. Inflamed by the enthusiasm of the
present Gaud Mayor they are taking mild
measures to purify the Chow quarters of the
metropolis.
PHILANTHROPY.
RELIEVING GOVERNMENT MEDICAL OFFICERS.
HOW IT’S DID.
A DOCTOR’S COMPLAINT.
“It is scandalous,” said a Sydney medical man
yesterday, “that the medical men cannot get a
supply of lymph for the purpose of vaccinating per-
sons privately. We could attend to hundreds, and
take a lot of work off the shoulders of the Govern-
ment medical officers. TJie supply of lymph, so far
as medical men are concerned, has gone out since last.
Saturday.”

SMOKING A PREVENTIVE.
A correspondent in n Sydney journal states
that, during the small-pox epidemic in Lon-
don, in 187 J, the late Sir Jonathan Hutchin-
son, the eminent physician, who was attend-
ing a patient in the house in which he resided,
advised him to smoke “and keep on smoking."
He did so, and was not in the least affected,
though all the others in the house (three
women and a child) were more or less suf-
ferers.—News item.
