ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
DOOMED.
THE PET PARROT (sorrowfully): “And to
think I've talked all these years and it s
come to this!"
“Lemarj”: Billjim was waltzing with
Matilda through Northern Riverina, and struck a
squattage where the boss isn’t lavish. That evening
he was evidently less lavish than usual when Billjim
passed up his dilly-bag; wherefore, when I drove
through next day, I found this testimonial, written
hi Billjim’s largest letters on the boundary gate:
“Mr. ’ s rations are excellent, if taken just be-
fore or after meals.”
“ M im°s a ” : “Ivory” (B. 12/tt/’l3) enters
ppearance for the defendants, the Queensland
fR a ‘ ISWer the Charge by me
ÜB. 8/5/13) against, them of allowing their cattle
det enorate. I quoted the statement made by
John Cooke the meat exporter (who should know
0,,™ 6 , abo, ' t , the matter), to the effect that
Queensland cattle had deteriorated, as evidenced by
the average weight of the carcases treated. Cooke
was further of opinion that the fault lay with the
.attle-breeders in not importing the right class of
bull, such as the Argentine Estancieros are willing
to pay the price for. I also quoted the statement
of the leading judge and breeders of shorthorns in
bouth Airica, the quality of whose cattle I know.
Writing to me, he frankly stated that he would not
think of importing a shorthorn from here, for the
reason that Australia had never imported an out-
standing shorthorn, as far as records went. “Ivory”
wishes us to believe that Bananaland cattle have not
c etenorated, and says the trouble is merely that
the cattle now being treated have not sufficient age
upon them, on account of the drought of 10 years
ago having thinned out the herds. To all this I
Damrot! Statistics prove the increase
yearly. What age does he want his rump steak to
be? On one station in C.-W. Queensland, muster-
ing for sale has been going on for the past 12
months, and bullocks are run in daily that were
grown cattle when the Australian States federated.
It is not the age that troubles Bananaland cattle
buyers; it is the weight and quality of the beast.
Had Ivory ’ stated that cattle-breeding was being
neglected on account of the high price of wool and
the banks’ preference for sheep’s-wool and mutton
over bull’s-wool and beef, there would have been
something in it. There are certainly a few breeders
of high-class Herefords in Queensland, but the short-
horn, which is the one, is sadly neglected. That
S Africa is a good market for good, cattle is proved
by the fact that recently one Farr, of Adelaide,
Cape Colony, imported from Great Britain and Hol-
land 100 shorthorn and Friesland cattle which, when
sold by public auction, realised an average of over
£l5O per head. This S’African market is, open to
Australia ; but, unfortunately, the sale bulls or cows
will not stand the acid for quality agaihst those of
Great Britain. It would be an eye-opener for
Bananaland’s cattle-men to visit the ranch of the
Liebig Meat Co., in Rhodesia, and see the class of
cattle it breeds; and the foundation of that herd
came from South America. Concluding, I affirm that
the deterioration is due to (1) the dilatory methods
of breeding adopted by the cattle-men of Queens--
land, and (2) sheep being predominant and in the
bankers’ favor, wherefore cattle have been neglected ;
and I add for “Ivory’s” benefit that older and
tougher steak appears upon the breakfast plate than
was the case 10 years ago.
“Button-stick” winds up a controversy
that has outlived its interest, with a few words of
horse-sense:—
I’ve just been reading “W. Kabe,” “8.J.5.” (B.
20/3/’l3) and others, in re our riding and horse-
manship. Without a doubt certain out-back Aus-
tralians can ride anything on four legs, can gallop
through scrub and timber as thick as bristles on a
new hair brush, and so on. But a man may be a
good horseman, and still be unable to do these fancy
tricks. _ The brand of horsemanship that Australia
wants is the sort that works a war-charger day in
day out, with odd nights thrown in for luck, for
week and months on end, on feed that the horse
requires spectacles to see, on dirty and insufficient
water, with a saddle glued on for 24
or 36 hours at a stretch sometimes, doing
without a yard of exercise for some weeks,
and then being asked to do forced marches
for a week and come through, if not with
a Smile, at least without a sore back.
“Wild Colonial Boys” are not necessary
for a job of this sort. On the contrary.
I found in S’Africa that it was the rough-
riders and breakers who were the hardest
on their mounts, and used up more on
the campaign than any other class of rider.
Some of the men who were useful but not
ornamental riders —men who could sit a horse
as long as the horse had no personal objec-
tion only—went through the war without
falling off and breaking anything, with-
out being more than a few yards behind
in a gallop to or from a hot corner, and
generally did quite well what they were
there to do, and did the whole campaign
on one horse, and when they chucked the
job handed over the moke in fair order
and repair and regular running order. I
don’t remember one of the fancy hard-
riding buck-jump breaker riders who
didn’t go through a long string of crocks,
leaving them to recuperate in horse-depSts
or inside the asvogels. The great point to
remember is that when Australia reaches
for its gun and climbs into the saddle it
will want not merely horsemen but horse-
masters. “8.J.5.” is beside the point
when he talks of “chasing brumbies in the
scrubby ranges.” It won’t be brumbies
we’ll haye to chase in the day of trouble,
and as a general thing the “scrubby
ranges” will be avoided as awkward and
uncomfortable places to manoeuvre large
bodies of troops. And the man in khaki
who can be depended on to have his horse
in a condition to do a fair day’s work in
fairly decent riding country is likely to
be more useful than the fellow who
can light a pipe while on board an
animal that is throwing a complicated series of sun-
fish step-dances and volcanic eruptions, but who
has a horse that, after a week’s campaigning, is only
fit for a rest-cure or a neurasthenia sanatorium.
“Lagani Namo” (concerning superstition
in Papua) : I translate what I overheard the native
constable say: “I slept under a tree in the branches
of which a corpse had been placed. The corpse s
spirit leaped on me during the night, biting and
scratching me. I saw it plainly and fired four shots
at it, but these had no effect.” “Bah,” interjected
the corporal, “you were a fool. If you had slept
between two fires and had not snored in your sleep
the spirit would not have harmed you.”

“A.H.” discourses on the local fruit
trade:—-
The two rottenest places in Australia for fruit
are Brisbane and Sydney. And the quaint thing is,
they ought to be the two best. Queensland has an
ideal soil and climate for fruit-growing, together
with rail and shipping facilities. Jack fruit, mango-
steen and pawpaw grow practically wild. Yet hardly
anyone troubles to retail them. As to the Northern
State’s stone and citrus fruits, they mostly face the
public in a ruined state. The fruit fly has absolutely
adopted them, and its ravages take a more wide-
spread and disgusting form there than in any other
place on record. A strong argument for giving the
Federal Parliament complete power over trade and
commerce is to be found in the fact that the Q. fruit
industry, as at present constituted, is a menace to
the whole of Australia.
In the Ma State are to be found vineyards eaten
up with phylloxera. They have borne nothing for
years, and yet are allowed to remain, generating a
plague that is only awaiting an opportunity to de-
stroy the whole gTape industry of the State. Every
badly-infected vine should be destroyed within a
given period, the soil burned and the land either
laid fallow, or covered with a leguminous crop. The
example of France might be safely followed in deal-
ing with this pest, and any grower neglecting to con-
form with the regulations should be treated as a
national enemy and heavily fined. As regards the
other pests, N.S.W. has acted with commendable
harshness, and its fruit gardens are a credit to it, on
the whole. The chronic fruit famine from which it
suffers is due to the King which combines to keep up
prices regardless of the blasphemies of the con-
sumer and the producer.
In Melbourne fruit is about 10 p.c. cheaper than
in Sydney and 50 p.c. cheaper than in Brisbane,
if one takes quality into account. And then
there is Adelaide. How shall I sing the praises of
Adelaide with sufficient vociferousness? There you
can get grapes at threepence per lb., such as are
sold in Sydney at Is. 6d. or 2s. per lb. Also, apples,
pears, peaches and plums at exactly the price per lb.
which is paid in the eastern cities for individual
specimens. The Adelaide fruit is carefully placed
on the market; there is no filling of attractive
windows, and supplying a deluded purchaser with
withered and uneatable stock. Westralia’s record is
equally creditable, or nearly so. It does not import
Tasmanian or Californian apples, and the inhabi-
tants, though handicapped by the soil, the climate
and a deficient rainfall, not only grow a good home
supply, but have gained through the last four seasons
the top prices on the London markets. All of which
goes to show that control of fruit-growing and market-
ing should be vested in a Commonwealth Board com-
posed of experts in each branch. Given such condi-
tions, a wine industry greater than that of France
and Germany combined would grow up, and fruit-
buyers would get value for their money.
“W. J.H.” : Re the “escaped elephant” (B.
12/6/’l3). A circus came to Ohakune, the mud-
diest, wettest, coldest town on the Main Trunk line
(M.L.). When entraining about midnight, after
the performance, an elephant went missing. The
local stationmaster was informed, and he was
prompt with his clue. “It’s been stolen, I’ll bet,”
he said. “You can’t leave a thing lying about here.
Those shiners” (railway-engine cleaners) “will have
it. They got away with two pounds of waste, half
a candle, and a bag of spuds last week.”
“Pick”: Because a Westralian postman
forsook his mailbags and fled at the sight of a few
dingoes, fancied or real (they keep bad- grog in
W.A. occasionally), and because he shot a few of
the animals that were attracted by the smell of his
galley, “Ginger” (13. 19/6/T3) writes up the
warragul as a beast of ravening ferocity and wolfish
propensities. Well, among the Jingera mountains
(N.S.W.), where there are more wild dogs to the
acre than there are to the squattage in any other
part of Australia, I have, when droving with sheep,
hundreds of times pelted them off with firesticks
during night watches. And when I was lost once
on Big Badger, and the aroma of a bear I was
toasting for supper had brought dozens of them
out of the scrub, they knocked holes through the
darkness in their haste to escape from the first
waddy I lobbed among them. That much for their
gameness and savagery. The simple truth is, dingoes
do not hunt in packs. They are solitary animals,
and, except when attending a banquet or a duel
over a lady, are rarely seen in bigger mobs than
one.

THE CALL OF THE COW.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Empire Day was duly celebrated at the school on
Friday. The pupils gathered at the usual time, and
the teacher gave an address on the greatness of the
British Empire, after which recitations were given
by the pupils. The singing of “God Save the
King” brought the indoor proceedings to a close
The “Call of the Cow” made the afternoon a short
one, but one and all expressed themselves as having
spent a very enjoyable day.
: i-lttan from the Cowland press.
Ilark! The Empire calls, and loyalty is throbbing
in our blasts ;
It is throbbing, throbbing, throbbing all the time,
And we’d gladly do it honor till the birds fly t 6
their nests;
But to linger so would really be a crime.
For the cow is calling, calling—don’t you hear her
pleading moo?
God save our gracious King—-but do be quick!
For we cannot wait much longer; we have other
work to do ;
So save our King—but save him mighty slick !
And if You cannot save him, say, by half-past four
o’clock,
Well, You’d better leave the effort go at that :
For old Posie, ruminating in the yard, will get a
shock,
And the other cows will wonder what we’re at.
This loyalty is right enough, provided that it flows
Somewhere between the hours of eight and four;
But if it takes much longer, then it’s overboard it
goes —
For that calling from the cow we can’t ignore!
And, when you come to think of it, a prince is
always near
To step into a monarch’s empty shoes ;
But a milking cow is-hard to get, and always very
dear ;
So you’ll pardon our respect for Posie’s moos.
The kids are disappointed; yes, but what can they
expect
Whenever from the cowyard comes the call?
Aye, I have no doubt this loyalty is perfectly cor-
rect —-
But the cows don’t seem to think so —not at all!
So, Providence, get busy there. In fact, I don't see
why
We couldn’t have a king who wouldn’t need
Such a vast amount of saving, with the bull’s wife
standing by,
What time the CS-lves are fretting for their feed.
Say, why not save him once for all, and let the
matter be ?
Or can it be a king is evil-starr'd ?
Listen! Loud above the tumult of our rural lovalt’
Comes the. calling from our Empress in the yard!

WHAT DYSPEPTICS SHOULD EAT.
A PHYSICIAN’S ADVICE.
“Indigestion and practically all forms of
stomach trouble are, nine times out of ten,
due to acidity; therefore stomach sufferers
should, whenever possible, avoid eating food
that is acid in its nature, or which by chemi-
cal action in the stomach develops acidity,
unfortunately, such a rule eliminates most
foods which are pleasant to the taste, as
well as those which are rich in blood, flesh
and nerve building properties. This is the
reason why dyspeptics and stomach sufferers
are usually so thin, emaciated and lacking
in that vital energy which can only come
from a well-fed body. For the benefit of
those sufferers who have been obliged to ex-
clude / ro , m their diet all starchy, sweet or
tatty food, and are trying to keep up a mis-
erable existence on gluten products, I would
suggest that you should try a meal of any
food or foods which you may like in moder-
ate amount, taking immediately afterwards
ha*! a teaspoonful of bisurated magnesia in
a little hot or cold water. This will neu-
tralise. any acid which may be present, or
which may be formed, and instead of the
as .H a i t j e l 1 I ng uneasiness and fulness, you
nnd that your food agrees with you per-
Ct *. y ; “ lsurat ea magnesia is doubtless the
best food corrective and antacid known. It
s not a medicine, and has no direct action
but by neutralising the
acidity of the food contents, and thus re-
« he sour , ce of . the aci d irritation
. inflames the delicate stomach lining,
it does more than could possibly be done
drug or medicine. As a physician
ieve use medicine whenever
“*' es l ary - hut I must admit that I cannot

“Tarago”: Can’t agree with “Ginger’s”
vague generalities about the courage of the hungry
dingo (B. 19/6/T3). My experience of the Gipps-
land species of the slinking marauder brands him a
coward. For years I lived in dingo-infested country,
and never knew anything more bellicose than a
poddy-calf to suffer from the mong.’s alleged cour-
age. A close acquaintance with him leaves the im-
pression that his chief asset is bluff. I was returning
home with stores and Sunday’s roast one night, and
was followed some distance by a pack. Naturally,
I had some apprehension, and backed up against a
tree with a stout waddy. The mob continued to
serenade me from a safe distance, and appeared to
be content to wait until I dropped dead from starva-
tion. The siege was becoming monotonous, when
three misguided poddy-calves, disturbed by the
racket, galloped across the foreground, and there-
after I was in the moonlight alone. Furthermore,
the female dingo will make no effort to defend her
progeny. I have chased her from a hollow log and
extracted five pups without any resistance. Inciden-
tally, one of the ’foresaid turned out one of the finest
reptile-destroyers in the province. I don’t know if
this habit is chronic with the untamed species.

“Avis”: Australia has all the hawking
falcons. The black-checked corresponds with the
English peregrine; the grey, known as the smoke
hawk, is like the European gerfalcon ; and our little
falcon is like the English merlin, or lady’s hawk.
Here it is called the duck hawk. Less than 12
inches long, bill to tail, it will slay a teal (18in.),
black duck (24in.) and even a wild turkey (4ft.).
The black-check variety frequents Bass Strait, and
has the peculiarity that it rarely constructs a nest
for its red-brown mottled eggs. Those who know
nothing of the falcon will be surprised to hear that
a blow from its wing will slice off a duck’s head.
AND ALL FOR NINEPENCE.
HAIRURESSER (to impatient Backblocks customer): " Orl rightl Orl right! But let us
clean up a bit as we go along!”
Naturalist James S. Bray lately told Bill-
jim what to do when his dog gets nipped. If the
nipper is a snake, locate the fang marks, scarify not
too deeply—a snake’s fang penetrates only about
an eighth of an inch, and the venom doesn’t issue
from the point, but from a small hole on the upper
surface —and rub in salt or tobacco juice. Next,
make the animal swallow salt and water to produce
vomiting. Repeat the dose if the limbs become, use-
less. If salt isn’t handy, tobacco (Billjim mostly
has a bit o’ weed) masticated to pulp or soaked in
water will do. Another useful agent is nicotine
drawn from a pipe stem with a straw and rubbed on
the dog’s tongue. But as an enemy of the friend
of man, the tick beats the curse with the endless
tail because, besides being deadly, it is also fright-
fully painful. First, find the tick. Put a table-
spoonful of methylated spirit, turpentine, strong
vinegar, or any other liquid that has fire in it, in
a small glass bottle. Invert the bottle and press
it tightly against the animal’s body so that the
parasite is immersed in the liquid. A dead tick
should happen after a minute or so of this treat-
ment. Remove the corpse with a pocket knife,
locate the nearest pub, and complete salvage opera-
tions with a personal dose of any remedy preferred
by the operator. Then in nine cases out of 10
Billjim needn’t steal anew dog.

“Gouger”: If the Daintree River blacks
(N.Q.) desire to kill an enemy in secret, they watch
their chance by night, when the foe is sleeping bliss-
fully. The death warrant is a small, flat stone,
which is heated in the doomed man’s own fire while
he slumbers. When warm, the stone is placed gently
on the dreamer’s stomach, a monotonous charm is
whispered over him, while the charmer’s hands are
passed very gently over his victim’s forehead.
While the charm is working the patient is supposed
to be having terrible dreams, wherein his enemy is
surely compassing his death. When the charmer is
satisfied the job is well and truly done, he steals
away, leaving the stone. How deeply-rooted super-
stition is, defying all the pooh! pooh! of the white
man, is proved next morning, when the black man
wakens and sees the stone. He straightway loses
interest in worldly things, and gradually falls into
a state of coma. In about a month he dies. 1 have
had two “boys” killed in this way, and every effort
to shake sense into them has been unavailing. I
have argued with them, I have laughed at them, I
have belted them. “No good, boss; me die!” was
all they’d say. And they carried out their word to
the finish —just when I wanted them most, too, con-
found them!
“W.T.C.”: For cowardice the Sydney
young man is hard to beat. I was at one of the
beaches one night recently, looking for an expected
eclipse of the moon. I was seated in a secluded
spot, waiting for the phenomenon, and enjoying
the beauty of the night, when other young men
began to arrive; but not one of them had had the
pluck to come alone. At length the looked-for
change in the orb of night took place. So impres-
sive—almost terrifying, in fact! Satisfied with the
sight I turned to go, and, shame to him, every young
man on that beach was clinging to his female
companion—l presume in abject terror!
“McNorth”: When Billy, aboriginal, re-
turned to his district he was chock-full of all kinds
of information that he had learnt at the mission
school. So he started in and told the camp nigs
all about the revolution of the earth. One asked
what it turned on. Billy answered that it turned
on an axle. The doubter then wanted to know whac
the axle rested on. Billy said, “A forked stick.”
“What the fork stick he rest on?” “Longa big
stone.” “What the big stone rest on?” “Longa
’nother big stone.” “Yes, yes; I savee that. But
what that big stone rest on?” Billy was getting
annoyed, so he said wratlifully, “Oh, there’s big
stones all the way down.”
The just-deceased widow of Charles Bright
had the distinction of being the only Aus-
tralian woman, so far as this paper knows,
who ever spoke the funeral oration at her
husband’s grave.
Scone (N.S.W.) has entered into posses-
sion of a new hospital, erected to the
memory of H. J. H. Scott, one-time
“Tuppenny” Scott and captain of the Aus-
tralian eleven, and more recently Dr. Scott,
of Scone. The cricketer-medico was a popu-
lar figure wherever he went; and when it
recovered from the shock of his untimely
death, the district decided to build a hospital
to bear his name. One benefactor gave the
site, the public subscribed about £3000 and
the Government gave £2000. with the result
that Scone has a very worthy reminder of a
man who was worth remembering.
“Bookware” : A biography of Henry Var-
ley, the ex-Victorian butcher who turned
evangelist and made a stir in two hemi-
spheres, has been published in London. A
son has written it. Two others of the family
are the Varley Brothers, of Melbourne. In
France you don’t care to admit that you are
a grocer. “Born a man, died a grocer,” is
the gibe. Here the odium seems to attach to
the butcher; hence perhaps the sedulous con-
cealment of Varley’s old beef trade. But he
didn t mind ; and, anyhow, there was always
a vealy look about him. In preaching he
rubbed his hands, washed them in invisible
water, a reminiscence of his old “Buy ! Buy !
Buy !” in the Geelong market. Yet a good,"
honest, sanguinary evangelist he was.
“Naumai”: John Duthie has been called
to the M.L. Leg. Council by his political
brother, Premier Massey. “Honest John’’
was the nightmare of Hansard and of gal-
lery pressmen in general. Like most diffi-
cult men to “take,” he was a wildly irri-
tated person when misreported. In fact, he
was a bitter and maddened bloke. One day
he told Seddon that he (Richard) had been
guilty of “a piece of impertinence.” In the
gallery it sounded like “beastly imperti-
nence,” and so it appeared. When Duthie
read his paper he took the stairs in two
leaps, and for 10 minutes sang a song about
the war. He sat in three Parliaments. By
occupation Duthie is a wealthy ironmongei*.
John Ballance once called him a “tin tack
merchant,” but Ballance was a hard man
sometimes.
“Jean Sibi’s” art note: —
Sculptor Web. Gilbert, who is off to Europe next
year, has been giving some of his chisellings an air-
ing. There are 17 works in the exhibition, and
each one demonstrates Gilbert’s remarkable faculty
for impressing his personality on marble and bronze.
The most charming thing is his “Nautilus,” a slim,
bare girl standing on a shell. “Hedone,” accom-
panied by snakes and less pleasingly feminine, is
very well modelled; the “Reclining Figure” in
marble is honored with a pose that emphasises the
damsel’s nudity ; the “Head of Minerva” is a coldly
dignified contribution; and “Salome” contains all
the fateful expression that one expects in the notori-
ous adorer of John the Dipper. Among the portrait
busts, which include likeable studies of artist McCub-
bin and Mrs. Brough, the most attractive is that of
Barney O’Dowd, whose altitudinous fofehead and
faraway look have been very suggestively done in
bronze. The head, which is now one of the per-
manent institutions of the National Gallery, was
ticketed at 30 guineas, and dirt-cheap at the price.
Geelong woke up the other Sunday morn-
ing to find that workmen were desecrating
the holy day by putting the finishing touches
on anew theatre. Happily somebody re-
membered that proceedings to squash the
horror could be taken under an Act passed
in the days of King Charles the Saunterer,
and the law was accordingly set in motion.
The workmen were charged with pursuing
their worldly calling on the Lord’s Day, for
which the penalty prescribed is ss. or 24
hours in the stocks. Unluckily action had
not been comnjenced against the men until
11 days after the offence, whereas the
mouldy old Act says that not more than 10
must elapse. Thus Geelong missed a beau-
tiful difficulty; for had they been fined the
defendants were going to refuse to pay the
dollar, and throw on Geelong the job of
producing the stocks. They might perhaps
have been mad enough to have Geelong fined
for not providing stocks.
The just-published Letters of Professor
Goldwin Smith (1826-1910), the Oxford
philosopher who settled in Canada, suggest
that he would have been a valued contri-
butor to The Bulletin if he had settled in
Australia instead :
(1879). —Toronto is just now in a paroxysm of
vulgar flunkeyism, called forth by the visit of the
Princess and her husband.... One of the most ludi-
crous parts of the Jingo policy is the attempt to set
up a Court here, with an aristocracy of knights.
One of the knights had been a chemist, another a
miller. The people call them Sir Bolus and Sir
Brom.
(1897 —to G. H. Reid, who, with Barton, had been
been asking for advice.) —It might be well to pro-
vide for a revision of the Constitution, whatever it
might be, after a certain term. Circumstances
change, and institutions must change with them.
As Bacon says, “What man does not alter for the
better, Time, the great innovator, alters for the
worse.” Time has greatly altered for the worse the
American Constitution, the immobility of which,
owing to the excessive difficulty of amendment, is a
very serious evil.
(1899-1900). —The Boers have made a most gal-
lant fight, and they will leave an impression. I
doubt whether England has been put in a worse
moral position since the burning of Joan of Arc
I was thinking of paying a short visit to England;
but the Jingoism there would sicken me.
(1904).—The root of the evil is the Party system,
which we have learned to regard as the necessary
foundation of free government, whereas it is an acci-
dent of English history, the offspring of the struggle
in the 18th century between the Stuarts and their
Constitutional opponents (1909). —The party sys-
tem of government is surely everywhere breaking
down. Here (Toronto) its downfall is complete.
(1900). I begin to fhink that Socialism is the
only force capable of coping with Jingoism. It is
unquestionably gaining ground here.
Name and address of the author of these
remarks: Goldwin Smith, The Cemeterv,
Toronto, Canada—barring his brain, which
he bequeathed to a Professor of Cornell
University, U.S.A., remarking “I would as
soon he should have my brain as my old
hat.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Alliance must trust to the popular education of
liquor, which had been proved a failure as a stimu-
lant, and a poison as a beverage.— -From garbled ac-
count of “Tommy” Walker’s remarks on Derrink
(W.A.)
Yus! orfen I ses to me mate, Mick O’Brine, ’oo’s
apt for to licker too free —
“Youse say yer ’ead’s achin’ —it’s poison youse
takin’ —most deadly, youse take it from me !”
But wot do I get in reply but a snort, or a deep,
’oiler larf, or a sneer?
Mick ’ll say—“ Strike me pink ! One ’ud blankv
well think that I drank! W’y I only ’as beer!
An’ wot’s beer?
Commin beer?
Blokes eats ’undreds of gallons a year!
Youse ’ave tea —
Beer fer me !
There’s no bleedin’ ’idatids in beer! (’Ear! ’Ear!)”
Well, o’ corse, I replies to Mick —blastin’ his eyes fer
a blanky, fanatical fool —
yer know as it’s poison wot lures you weak
boys on to ’Ell, where the Devil keeps school?”
Ses I: “The Alliance is out in defiance of spendin’
six million a year ;
It don’t want to ’urt youse—it wants to convert
youse, an’ wean youse off ’orrible beer.
For wot’s beei —
Narsty beer —
W’ich is orfen as thick as it’s clear?
It goes sour
In a nour —
It’s a norrible poison is Beer!”
But all my advice counts for nix with O’Brine, as,
lately, I’ve ’ad cause to see;
I torks ’obnail liver, in ’opes that ’e’d shiver, nex’
time ’e’ felt wild for a spree:
I ses: “Drink’s a failure, all over Australure; the
doctors won’t ’ave it—no fear!
An’ even Tom Walker” “Shut up, you d—d
talker!” says Mick,
Drink’s quite diff’rent from Beer!
Why, wot’s beer ?
I’ve some ’ere....
Wot, yer won’t ?.... Well (hie) ’s luck, then, ol’
dear!”
Ses I, “Mick!
W’y, youse slack”
Ses ’e —“No. just thic) poisoned by beer!”
(Dear! Dear!)

“N.M.” on a dead Quaker and a deader
ship:—
Gone, Robert Mather, aged 65, head of Hobart’s
oldest drapery business, and connection by birth or
marriage with all the Mathers, Cottons, Walkers
and Mays that dot the Apple Island with Quaker-
ism. (Count in with the last-named family, by the
way, the illustrious Phil May.) “Mather’s,” like
Walch’s bookshop, goes back to the infancy of Tas-
manian trade, when commodities were scarcer than
customers and there was no need to shriek to reach
the public’s ear. All the same, shopkeeping was not
the Quaker equivalent of beer and skittles. Bad
debts loomed large, and the Mathers firm was obliged
to compound with its creditors. The diggings, which
broke out soon after and splashed the trades with
gold, put things right; whereupon the firm honor-
ably notified its former creditors that they would be
credited and supplied with goods to the full amount
that had been owing. A bit of interesting shipping
history connects with the coming of the family to
Tassy. The Australian States were being boomed in
1821, and any old ramshackle vessel that could put
to sea found its complement of passengers. The
Mathers (husband, wife and small children) had the
luck to strike the Hope. After repeated delays in
starting, this coffin-ship went pottering about the
English coastline and ran into a violent storm at
the Downs, which, by bringing the whole infamous
business to light, probably saved the lives of the
passengers. The owners had crammed in as many
as they could, and, after carefully collecting the pas-
sage-money (over £3000), had sent the vessel to
sea, with a 6canty supply of inferior provisions, to
take its chance. To their surprise, the ship turned
up again, and then they played their last card —
they declared themselves bankrupt, and left their
passengers stranded. Eventually, through represen-
tations made to Earl Bathurst, the British Govern-
ment came to the rescue, and provided another vessel
(the Heroine) to carry the passengers to Australia.
The shipping arrangements seem to have been all
very casual. At Rio Janeiro the captain of the
Heroine gave some of the men leave to go on shore;
then, the breeze having freshened, he went off
without them, and would have left them there, re-
gardless of the protests of their wives and families,
if a storm had not driven him back for shelter.
They arrived in Hobart Town on September 10,
1822, after 20 weeks of voyaging, during the last
10 or 12 of which the passengers were shut off from
fresh air and daylight, the hatches being closed and
a tarpaulin nailed over them. Mrs. Mather wrote
memoirs of the voyage. Pleasant it must have been,
travelling in those days!—especially as the water-
supply frequently ran out or went stale, and the
food was salt junk with a supply of vinegar to ward
off scurvy!
JUDGE HEYDON, Arbitration Court,
N.S.W. Here you see his Honor arbitrating
for all he is worth.
You’ve heard of the Duke of Saxe-Coburg. Let’s talk
of the King of Sacks—that is, the “once-used equal-
to-new” sack. It’s the King, because it’s a money-
saver. One of these sacks would prevent Capital
leaving the country if it was, sewn up in it. Our
sacks point the way out of your sack worries ; there’s
no “cul-de-sac” about them as a business proposi-
tion ; there’s only one hole in them, and that’s at
the mouth. They’re tiptop, in short. Write the
“Sack Specialists,” T. SMITH, LTD., Sussex-street,
Sydney. Do it now.*
“0.K.”: Several obituary notices of the
late J. C. Williamson give the date of his
birthday as August 26, 1845, which would
make him out to have been under 68 when
he died. Probably he was three or four years
older. The statement that he was only a
youth of 17 or 18 when he went with a
company to Canada is hardly credible. Be-
sides, the various notices are contradictory.
For instance:—
Mr. Williamson once described to an interviewer
how he came to cross the Rubicon. “We were living
at Milwaukee, and there was an amateur company
with which I had appeared. One of them arranged
to go to Canada, and asked me to come along. I
was then 16. I went to my uncle and told him
about it. He said, ‘Remember, if you go, you go
for good.’ Well, I went, and from that Hay I have
been connected with the stage,” etc., etc.
Another account: —
Mr. Williamson delighted in amateur theatricals,
and got an engagement at the Milwaukee Theatre
where he learned dancing and fencing, helped the
stage manager, played his part in the evening, and
sometimes painted scenery. In 1863 he went to
Canada.
Third account: —
When 16 he joined a theatrical co. at Milwaukee,
and played many characters in that early part of
his stage life, and he, in addition, looked after the
business side of the enterprise. In 1862 he went to
Canada with a company.
These differing notices all give the year of
his birth as 1845, and were seemingly
written in recent years. A more trust-
worthy notice appeared in a Melbourne
theatrical sheet—The Call —of December,
1887, and this said :
Mr. J. C. Williamson was born on 26th August,
18 4 b. At 16 years of age he adopted the profession
of an actor —-a determination which did not meet
with the approval of his friends. His first efforts
were on the boards of the Milwaukee Theatre. Then
for a year or two he travelled through the Western
States and afterwards visited Canada, etc., etc.
But even in 1887, when J.C.W. was said to
have been born in 1844 (not ’45), he was
already at an age when actors and other
people are disposed to lose count of two or
three years in their variegated past. Pro-
bably the veteran was nearer 72 than 68
when his long innings closed. The one thing
certain is that he married Maggie Moore
just 40 years ago, and was a keenly com-
mercial-minded artist in those early days.
No actor of his class—for Williamson was
an excellent character actor —was ever more
thoroughly businesslike. He had his bride-
to-be in his eye, as the ideal Lizzie Stofel,
when he bought “Struck Oil” in the manu-
script and got it pulled into shape for him.
The following year the Williamsons brought
their piece to Australia and started as star
artists. Without the assistance of his lucky
“find,” J.C.W. would have been a successful
man, of course. He had success in him. But
he owed his early rise and his Australian
fortunes to the “Struck Oil” investment and
his choice of a wife, not forgetting the fact
that he himself had become a specialist in
dialect characters, and was the very man to
make a hit as John Stofel. There was no
vanity of the beauty actor about J. C. Wil-
liamson. Off the stage he was known as
“Handsome Jimmy,” but in his handsomest
time of life he disguised himself as elderly
fat men, and one of his first big parts was
that of the Dickens character, Joey Ladle-
bloated, bibulous Joey Ladle —in an adapta-
tion of “No Thoroughfare.” When the pre-
sent writer interviewed J.C.W. some years
ago, he seemed to remember his Joey Ladle,
at Wallack’s Theatre, as the “creation” he
was particularly proud of. An actor who
mostly played dialect characters in America
got little chance of creating anything. Wil-
liamson’s John Stofel was his own glorified
edition of the typical Dutch American. It
couldn’t have been better done, but it ran
on old lines. His Rip Van Winkle differed
only a little from Jefferson’s creation of the
part, and John Stofel, returning to Pitts-
burg in a cranky condition, was much the
same man as Rip after his long sleep. Again,
J.C.W.’s performance of the old servant in
“Kerry” couldn’t help being founded on the
creation of Dion Boucicault, senior, who
wrote the one-act play. Williamson in Aus-
tralia usually played the parts in which
there is least scope for an actor’s individua-
lity. Sometimes, as in “Arrah-Na-Pogue”
and other melodramas, he revealed something
of his identity in a jaunty sort of way. On
the. comic-opera stage he was an admirable
policeman in “Pirates of Penzance,” and a
droll old Judge in “Trial by Jury.” Some
21 years ago, when Billv Elton had just con-
cluded his engagement with the firm,
manager Williamson took “Billy’s” nart as
the Dutch burgomaster in “La Cigale,” and
this was his. last apnearance as an every-
night actor in Melbourne. His subsequent
special appearances happened in Sydney.
Herbert Brookes, the new President of
the Vic. Chamber of Manufactures, has
hitherto mostly been in the shadow of two
relatives—Alfred Deakin, his father-in-law,
and Norman, his tennis-champion brother.
But Herbert is likely to nrove himself as
good iron as either of them. As a youngster
he took his Engineering degree and drifted
into mining, but had the bad luck to strike
the common sort that doesn’t pay; so he
became sec. to an engineering co., and pushed
along till he nowadays is chairman of direc-
tors, with a large, powerful oar also in the
Australian Paper Mills.
Two remarkable deaths were reported last
week. One was that of the oldest inhabi-
tant of Adelong (N.S.W.). aged 305, the
same old unprovable, impossible centenarian.
The other deceased was a boy in California,
who drank 33 bottles of ginger-beer, and
then gave up the ghost. Probably he died
on a Friday.
A number of old Victorian “lags” will
remember Peter Dwyer, their former guar-
dian. Peter’s connection with prisons be-
gan 60 years ago, when he became a warder
at Pentridge, which was always Melbourne’s
“felons’ hotel” for long-sentence boarders.
When Maryborough was honored with a
gaol 50 years ago, Peter Dwyer was ap-
pointed first governor. Then he governed
Portland Gaol, and afterwards took the man-
agement of Geelong Gaol, where the old,
forgotten murderers used to be sent to end
their days. From there he went to Mel-
bourne City Gaol, and finally he returned
to Pentridge as his Excellency the Governor.
In the early 90’s of last century, Peter re-
tired into rural private life at the little town-
ship of Riddell, and there he died last week,
full of years (86) and gruesome reminis-
cences. His knowledge of episodes in the
lives of citizens who hoped he had forgotten
them must have been wide and deep.
Concerning Australia’s political patriarch :
John Dennistoun Wood is last man left who could
write a readable book on early-Victorian politics
from personal experience ; but as he was 83 the other
day the book can hardly be looked for now. Born
in Van Diemen’s Land when William IV. was king,
he was sent to England for his education, and was
called to the English Bar in 1852. Then he re-
turned to these parts, and got a big practice. Next
he went into politics, and was Solicitor-General in
the first O’Shanassy Ministry (1857 —it lasted 49
days), Attorney-General in the Nicholson Ministry
of 1859-60 (it lasted one year and 30 days), and
“Justice” in the third O’Shanassy Cabinet, a dur-
able thing as durability went then, which lasted
from 14th November, ’6l, to 27th June, ’63. In
1867 he made a tour of Greece, Turkey and the
world thereabouts. He had fever at Damascus, and
something else serious—possibly pariah dogs—in
Constantinople. In 1869 he returned to England,
began practice at the Bar there, and during the next
20 years appeared in every case of importance that
went from Victoria to the Privy Council. Then, his
old bones calling for milder weather, he returned to
Melbourne, and resumed practice as a barrister in
the old quarters. He is a most versatile little man.
He has had a taste for the stage among other
things—the theatre was, and is, one of his favorite
recreations. Over half a century ago the members
of the Victorian Parliament played “The Merchant
of Venice” at the Melbourne Theatre Royal, when
George Coppin was to the fore. Rusden, the clerk
of the Council, wrote a prologue, and Wood, who
had a fine voice, delivered it. After the perform-
ance there was a supper at the cafe attached to the
theatre, and Wood was the humorist of the night—
or morning. Like most Tasmanians, he never lost
his love for his native island, and after having seen
the world and the Unspeakable, he returned three
or four years ago to his birthplace, at Dennistoun,
to farm sheep and become a member of the local
Parliament.
Leyton Richards throws up the Collins-
street (Melb.) Independent pulpit. All
through the hairily picturesque Bevan’s
20 years this church went down, down. At
last there would be only 100 or less
people in the gorgeous tabernacle of a Sun-
day. morning, and very few more at night.
On Bevan taking a sort of professorship in
Adelaide, Richards was brought from Brit-
ain, and has been for three years indus-
triously flogging the dead nag. But it won’t
move. A red-hot fanatic might make it
seem to be alive for a while, but Richards

Political spoils going naturally nowadays
to the victors, W. E. Johnson becomes
Speaker of the House of Reps, in succession
to the strikingly successful “Charley”
McDonald, the wire and whipcord member
for the North. Johnson was born at New-
castle-on-Tyne in 1861. He studied tele-
graphy, and as a youngster was employed at
the London Exchange, Mark Lane, the Corn
Exchange, the Smithfield Market, and else-
where. Then he shipped as midshipman, and
went twice round the world. On his last
voyage he was left at Colombo with fever,
and was repotted dead when the ship arrived
in England. After that, not being as dead
as he was alleged to be, he worked in
America and Australia as scenic artist. Then
he came forward in politics, and was
elected for Lang (N.S.W.) in 1903. In
Parliament he has talked unceasingly, and
generally uninterestingly, and in the North-
ern Territory he sat on an alligator, mistak-
ing it for a log. Apart from that, the only
notable thing he has done was when, as
the jackal of the Sydney Congestion party,
he proposed Canberra as the site of Aus-
tralia’s rabbit-burrow capital.
The Commonwealth Government, if it
follows the recommendation of the Historical
Memorials Committee, will buy Lister Lis-
tens painting of the Federal capital site for
£250; and it will also make a bid for Penley
Boyd’s picture on the same lugubrious sub-
ject.
The dropping out of J. T.
Walker and the defeat of Lyne
removes from politics (for the
present, at any rate) the last of
the Ma State’s representatives in
the Convention which framed the
Federal Constitution. Of the
others, O’Connor, Abbott and
Brunker are dead ; Carruthers and
McMillan may be classified as Past
Deeds; Barton is on the High
Court, and Reid and Wise are
otherwise occupied. Walker’s in-
tention to retire was known over
12 months ago; so Oom Bill is
the real last of the 10. And take
him for all in all, he was about
the best and biggest of the bunch.
“J.F.H.” : Not many Australian
swaggies finish up as Governors
and Iv.C.M.G.’s; but the story of
one has just been published in
London. _ Ralph Williams is the
author, 15s. the price, and “How I
Became a Governor” the title.
M illiams went to Australia in an
aimless fashion as a young man,
he says. He took to the bush and
humped his bluey or drum from
station to station, gaining a good
deal of rouseabout rough-and-
tumble experience. Then he joined
an expedition that went treasure-
hunting in Patagonia, where he
lived for a time with the Indians.
Afterwards he made off to South
Afnca, where he got a billet under Sir
Charles \\ arren, in the Civil Intelligence De-
partment. Thus he got his foot on the
ladder, and rose to be British Agent in Pre-
toria, Treasurer at Gibraltar, Colonial Secre-
tary at Barbadoes, Governor of the Wind-
ward Islands, and, finally, Governor of New-
foundland.
PROFIT AND LOSS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
God bless Vm,, darling, and give you the pluck
you need. —Your broken-hearted Bim. — From, the
Marquis of Northampton’s farewell letter to Miss
Daisy Moss, who afterwards got £50,000 out of him
for breach of promise of marriage.
Miss Moss has gained far more than pluck.
For Valor, after all,
Is not a fraction of the luck
That on one’s brow may fall.
Most girls would gladly cringe like hounds
For fifty thousand golden pounds.
Bim, on the other hand, has lost,
Or smashed, the best of hearts —
A process that has doubtless cost
Him divers ghastly smarts ;
And also, which is still more drear,
Two thousand pounds and odd per year.
N.S.W.

As to the figures in the last line, the only question
is whether a small amount included with the £10,003
written off should not have been deducted straight
away from gross profits ; but it isn’t of much prac-
tical consequence whether it is done one way or the
other. The bulk of the £10,003 consists of cost of
buildings, plant, etc., renewed or put out of use. The
practice is to put a substantial sum to credit of a
depreciation and renewals account, and write off
amounts from that account as renewals or replace-
ments are made. This is a satisfactory system so
long as it is clearly understood that the amount put
to reserves is not all true profit. Hitherto, the plant
being new, replacements have been slight, and the
reserves (which include the depreciation fund) have
therefore grown fast. In course of time the renewals
will cost more perhaps in a year than the amount
put by to cover depreciation in that year ; and then
the reserves will start to shrink. It must therefore
be understood that the reserves at present are largely
fictitious, and the profits, so far as they include the
amount required to cover wear and tear, are ficti-
tious also. Instead of anything from £35,000 to
£40,000 of profit being made, last year’s total was
only £17,047, which was more than absorbed by the
sinking fund and the contribution to the city funds.
The sinking fund contribution is 1 p.c., plus, of
course, the interest earnings of the fund. After that
had been handed over, and the amount judged neces-
sary to cover depreciation was provided, there wasn’t
enough left to furnish the grant in relief of city
rates which was made ; wherefore the profit and loss
balance had to be cut into. This shows that and
other points:—

The council never should have started the practice
of burgling this young enterprise’s cash-box in order
to relieve the city ratepayer. The time may come
when the infant may reasonably be asked to do
something; but it shouldn’t be until the business
has provided a much more general and satisfactory
service at a lower cost, and has got more than its
eyebrows out oi debt. To now make a grant which
involves an onslaught on the small unappro-
priated accumulations is very discreditable unless
the council is convinced that the amount (including
a special £7000) appropriated against depreciation
was more than the case called for.

i- that both sinking and depreciation funds are in-

carried the money. In the first table all the earn-

The sinking fund is invested wholly or almost wholly
in Government and municipal securities; the depre-
ciation fund, which may be required for renewals
and replacements at any time, is mostly on fixed de-
posit at 34 p.c. This plan of investing the depre-
ciation fund is an excellent one. In most cases,
even where a sinking fund for the repayment of
capital is invested in more or less liquid securities,
the depreciation reserve is “used in the business,”
as it is called—which means pretty often that it
isn t a real reserve at all; and when money is
wanted for renewals, there is no alternative but to
raise it from either the shareholders or the over-
draft or debenture merchant. The caoital in this
business is £526,000 plus an overdraft of £41,419.
Buildings, plant, installations, etc., stand in the
books at £498,497, with £49.821 on account of
the purchase of private undertakings. The nature of
this last asset is not plain. It may even be a goodwill
or paper item. The total amount put aside against
wear and tear last year was £32,640 (including
special appropriations to cover the actual cost of
such things as renewal of poles), which is equal to
6 p.c. on, say, £540.000. It looks a reasonable
provision. As shown above, the total to the credit
of the Depreciation Fund is now £163,514, but
there will, it is said, be heavy drafts on this reserve
very soon on account of renewals.
Further reductions were made in some of the
charges for lighting last year, which probably
accounts for the comparatively poor spring in the
figures relating to profits. (Also, by the way, it
probably accounts for the further reduction ' just
made by the rival Gas Co.) This table gives a bet-
ter idea of working costs, etc.: —

The term ended December, 1911, was 10 months
only, so revenue for that period was at the rate of
£130,805 a year, or actually more than it was for
the past year. Expenses, however, instead of re-
maining also stationary or thereabouts, have bustled
along from £56,135 (on a 12 months basis) to
£65,130. It is pretty clear from this and other
features that the business will require watching.
There is still a fair margin left after providing for
depreciation and sinking fund, but there plainly
isn't enough left for such contributions to the gen-
eral rates as have been made in the last two or
three years; and last year’s halt of the revenue,
while the expenditure jumped ahead, suggests that
for the current year it may not be advisable to make
any contribution at all. Or, rather, it may be less
advisable than it has ever been.
The Maoriland State Fire Insurance
Office, which began regular business early in Janu-
ary, 1905 (the deficit up to then represents prelim-
inary expenses) :

1906 .. 20,991 10,312 7,215 3,464 6,418
1907 . . 23,302 12,701 8,355 2,246 8,664
1908 . . 26,909 18,904 10,146 6,523
1909 . . 34,038 18,535 10,155 5,348 11,871
1910 .. 41,788 18,914 11,478 11,396 23,267
1911 . . 48,930 27,537 13,125 8,268 31,535
1912 . . 56,315 25,111 14,526 16,678 48,213
This office is now in the extraordinary position of
having no capital in the ordinary meaning of the
word. It was started because there was an outcry
against the over-charges made for insurance by pri-
vate offices, and Parliament authorised the raising
of £lOO,OOO as capital in order to provide some
healthy competition. All this office asked for to
start with, however, was £2OOO, which amount was
raised by the State on debentures. It was on this
small amount able to pull through, and during the
past year it reckoned that it could afford to buy
up those debentures, and do without any outside
help at all. This deal having been made, the office
is in the position of having provided all of its own
funds of nearly £50,000 out of its own earnings
within eight years. Certainly it can’t be claimed
that the whole £48,213 is surplus profit; for
those funds include the money necessary to
cover the risks under policies paid for last year
and running into this year. Making a liberal
allowance on this account the actual aggregate pro-
fits of the office so far have been, say, £20,000,
which is equal to 125 p.c. per annum on the capital
put up by the Government. And that isn’t all the
office has done by a long way. Immediately on the
appearance of this State office, the private cos. pulled
down their premiums, and it has been figured out
that the public has thus saved in the aggregate in
premiums £1,500,000. It has been a wonderful re-
turn from an investment of £2OOO, and the repay-
ment of the principal in full doesn’t diminish the
wonder. No doubt it would be too much to expect
things to go on being so good indefinitely. An out-
size in fires must be expected some day ; and against
that hazard the office must build up larger reserves.
When its funds are equal to 150 p.c. of the year’s
premiums, it may reckon itself in a safe and com-
fortable position. At present they don’t reach quite
95 p.c. ; and no private office would be regarded as
sound enough if it didn’t have much more than that.
Certainly, if something very unpleasant happened,
the State would come to the rescue—the State is
pledged to that—but the office doesn’t want to ever
have to go to the State for tuppence. Hence the
need for the steady growth of funds, not so much
to protect the policy-holders (who are protected as
no other policy-holders are by the State guarantee)
as to protect the State and make the office capable
of standing unsupported under practically any ad-
verse circumstances. And having no hungry share-
holders eager for dividends, it can, of course, accu-
mulate the necessary funds more easily than any of
its competitors doing an equal amount of similar
business.
As for those competitors, some of them are sorry
now they ever cut down the premiums. They hoped
to kill the State office; they have merely
crippled themselves. In their struggles some of them
have adopted the most reprehensible policy of accept-
ing over-insurances, relying on the fact that an in-
surance policy is an indemnity against loss, and
that, though a man pay premiums on £5OO of
stock, he can recover only £lOO if he can show that
he has lost only £lOO worth. The trouble is that
many small insurers don’t know this —the fish-shop
man, for instance—-and some stocks and shops are
far more inflammable when the owner expects to get
£5OO in case they are burned than when he believes
he can only rely on getting £ 100>. This over-
insurance, notoriously prevalent in Maoriland, is
largely responsible for the Dominion’s fire waste,
which has been est’mated by some at over £6,000,000
in 10 years. No doubt the flimsy, inflammable nature
of many of the premises helps; for unfortunately
the fire which breaks out in the little, over-insured
fish-shop has a habit of spreading to other places
that are not over-insured—which makes one wonder
why the decent majority who don’t over-insure don’t
adopt measures to squeeze out the cos. that encour-
age the vicious practice.

This society has been wading in trouble and is not
yet right out of it. Ten months ago, in fact, there
was a scare, whereupon the Victorian Government
appointed a committee of experts to overhaul the
business. This committee has made two or three
progress reports, but its final summary will not be
available for another few months. Judging by the
progress reports there is nothing flagrantly amiss.
“We have not observed,” they say, “any assets or
advances which in our opinion are of a speculative
nature, nor any advances or loans to directors.”
That makes comforting reading. Unfortunately it
doesn’t also make the Colonial Mutual a prosperous
concern.
The society got into the backwash many years
ago, and has stayed there ever since. Nothing suc-
ceeds in insurance like success. Give an office a
good name and policies will come in with little
persuasion ; throw doubt on the future, and every
bit of business has to be fought for. And it costs
money not only to get the business, but also to
keep it. Finding prejudice against it strong in Aus-
tralia, this society struggled to get attractive
figures by going to S’Africa and Europe. It didn’t
get much business to speak of, and what it did get
cost a great deal. The expense rate has run to con-
siderably over 20 p.c. of the total income, and the
insurer with decent sized lines to place —the man
who is looking for insurance as an investment—
knows that an office which consumes from 20 to 30
p.c. of its income in expenses has very little hope
of offering much in the way of bonuses. Bonuses
come from three principal sources—(l) a lower
expense rate than that figured on in fixing the pre-
mium ; (2) better interest earnings than the rate
assumed; and (3) a better mortality experience
than that assumed. With so high an expense rate
bonuses can’t get much help from the first source.
The position is none too good either with regard to
the second source. This list shows the recent interest
earnings on average funds: —
1904 ..£4l 4 p.c. 1909 ..£4 3 3 p.c.
1905 .. 3 17 11 „ 1910 . . 4 3 10 „
1906 .. 318 1 „ 1911 .. 4 5 9 „
1907 .. 316 9 „ 1912 . . 4 511 „
1908 .. 4 1 1 „
There is an improvement there, certainly, but the
rate is still, considering the nature of the securities,
at least 6s. Bd. p.c. less than it ought to be; and
another 6s. Bd. p.c. would mean £ll,OOO or
thereabouts available per annum for bonuses. The
trouble probably still lies in the load of freeholds
and leaseholds carried by the society. The manage-
ment certainly points out that these are bringing in
“over 4 p.c.,” whereas the rate assumed in fixing
premiums was only 8$ p.c. This means that the
society is earning enough to more than pay the
face value of its policies.; provided, of course, its
expenses and claims don’t run away with more than
it was assumed they would. But nowadays the
public wants much more than the face value of a
policy; so, though the figures are quite satisfac-
tory from one point of view, they are not satisfac-
tory from the point of view of new business-getting.

But that £29 14s. sd. p.c. for the past year rather
over-states the case. As a matter of fact, a good
deal of cleaning-up has been done during the 12
months, and various amounts have been dropped in
among the expenses—for instance, furniture depre-
ciation, £2BBl ; written off Fidelity Guarantee
account, £3549, plus a special appropriation to that
account, £4OOO. Strike off these extras, some of
which should, no doubt, have been charged to pre-
vious years, and the position for the past year isn’t
quite so unsatisfactory as it looks, though it is still
unsatisfactory. As for the cost of new business,
approximate figures can be got this way. Assume
that renewal (old) business costs 7 p.c. and annuity
(single premium) business 3 p.c.; total, £27,086.
Take the expenses proper to the year at £185,000 ;
then £157,914, being the balance over and above
the £27,086, was the cost of the new business,
which brought in £115,499 of premiums. This
means that the new business cost 137 p.c. of the
first-year premiums. It is too much, though it is
not as excessive as one would expect from the ex-
pense rate previously mentioned and calculated on
total income.

“Other assets” include loans on policies, outstanding
premiums (a heavy amount, showing that the society
is probably finding some difficulty in bringing
policy-holders up to the scratch), and £42,699
advanced to the Industrial department. To the policy-
holder who hangs back when his premium falls due,
this paper has only one thing to say. On the pre-
sent showing, it certainly couldn’t advise him to
increase the amount of his policy ; but on the evi-
dence available it is utter folly for a man who has
paid several premiums to drop out now. He did
badly to get in ; now that he is in he will do a lot
worse to drop out. If he is a wise man he will stay
and use his influence to get the whole business taken
over by some more progressive concern, just as the
M.L A. and the Widows’ Fund were taken over some
years ago. The Citizens’ office was the nucleus in
that case, and the trustees of the Widows’ Fund sec-
tion lately certified that the bonus now “represents,
on the average, an increase of 100 p.c. over the bonus
distributed prior to the union.” The National
Mutual is in business in S'Africa, so there might be
some advantage in a union in that direction. In any
case, this is a matter of detail. The thing is to
end a costly, unnecessary, independent struggle and
go under somebody else’s wing. It will certainly be
better for present policy-holders ; and, after all, it is
only the interests of present policy-holders that the
society is bound to consider.

Instead of feeding the society this branch has so
far been a severe tax upon it. It got into the busi-
ness too late in the day, for one thing; and, in any
case, it is doubtful whether it is a concern likely to
make a success of this kind of business. Bruce Smith
seems to The Bulletin to typify the Colonial Mutual,
of which he is a director ; and it isn’t easy to pic-
ture Bruce Smith as a successful pursuer of Indus-
trial insurance. In order to give itself a lift along
the road, the society early in the proceedings bought
up the struggl ng Standard office, and paid what
has always seemed to this paper the very excessive
price of £22,000 for the business. Judging by what
it has had to pay since then for organisation, as it
is euphemistically called, it was a poorer bargain
than it looked at the outset. In the accounts the
position is improved by shunting a large part of the
expenses into the balance-sheet, where they appear
as an asset. This purely paper item now amounts
to no less than £108,294. The cold fact is that
though the assurance fund is £72,101, the substantial
assets of all sorts, including outstanding premiums
and office furniture, amount to only £7273. In
other words, while £72,101 or thereabouts should
have been accumulated towards meeting the obliga-
tions under policy contracts, all there is of tangible
assets in this branch is £7273. For the rest, the
branch has for the present to lean on the Ordinarv
branch, and trust to the future to improve matters.

The Brisbane Electric Tramways Investment Co.
doesn’t own the trams ; it merely owns all but half
a dozen or so of the shares in the co. that owns the
trams. Originally, in 1882, an Act was passed giv-
ing a co. the right to use the streets of Brisbane
and suburbs for any old sort of tram for 14 years.
Before that period had quite expired, Malcolm
McEacharn got the concession extended for 25 years
on condition that electric lines were laid. That
gives the co. eight years from now. At the end of
that time the authorities can come in and buy with-
out paying anything for goodwill. That is to sav,
they must pay a fair price for the property neces-
sary for use in the business, but nothing for rights
and such-like. That allows of Brisbane making a
great deal better bargain than has been possible
lately at Perth and .Hobart. Unfortunately, it hangs
things up seriously, for, naturally, unless" the co. is
offered special inducements, it isn’t going to spend
a heap of money on extensions during the last few
years of its life. For one thing, it would be losing
interest on the capital cost while the lines were actu-
ally under construction ; for another, it wouldn’t be
able to recoup itself for the small return which most
lines give in their young days, and while population
is moving out to the newly-opened-up quarters. The
congestion disease isn’t very bad in Brisbane; but
for what there is of it the 25-year agreement with
the Tramways Co. is mainly responsible.
The past year’s profits were cut into badly by the
strike which happened early in the year, and by the
subsequent arbitration proceedings, in the course of
which manager Badger heard things which probably
didn’t improve his sleep for a night or two. You
get a better view of what the strike meant by look-
ing at the expenses of the co. which nominally owns
the trams: —
Ex- Divii Re-
Dec., Receipts, penses. Profits, dends. serves.

That profit of £92,533 made on the actual running
of the trams was cut up as follows: Dividends,
£80,000; renewal fund, £10,000; to unappropri-
ated balance, £2533. Of the dividends, £79,947
went to the B. E. T. Investment Co. That £79,947
and £74 of sundry receipts constituted the B. E. T. I.
Co.’s revenue. The total was £80,021, which in its
turn was cut up this way:—
Gross revenue . . . . . . £80,021
Less insurance and direction . . 4,590
Net revenue .. .. .. .. £75,431
Less interest on debentures . . . . 19,069
Net profit .. .. .. .. £56,362
The B. E. T. Investment Co., it will be seen, has
reserves now of only £13,781, the issue of £75,000
of bonus capital having made a big cut into the
old total; but it will also be noted that it owns
more than 99 p.c. of the Tramways Co.’s reserves,
which run to £105,127. Certainly, of the £105,127
no less than £lOO,OOO is to the credit of a renewals
fund; but it seems very doubtful whether much of
the money will be called for to pay for renewals.
On the other hand it may possibly be wanted to
provide for depreciation ; that is to say, when the
authorities take over the system, the fair value pay-
able for plant, properties, etc., may be considerably
less than the book value, and this reserve may come
in handy then to equalise matters. On this point,
however, there is no information. That is the advan-
tage of having two companies. The Investment Co.’s
Profit and Loss account merely shows dividends on
one side and management exes, on the other. The
Tramways Co., being a “private” concern, doesn’t
publish any accounts, and the public doesn’t know
whether anything, and if anything how much, is
written off to cover depreciation. In order not to
put too attractive a look on things, considerable pro-
fit may be going into new plant, etc., being reck-
oned in with ordinary expenditure. If money is
going that way it won’t be wasted, of course—it will
come back in the shape of extra purchase money
when the sale is made eight years hence. Mean-
time the possible mopping-up of a trifle of the profit
that way may be doing a little to pacify that part
of Brisbane which chafes at having to wait eight
years, and would no doubt chafe more if the profits
were shown to be larger. Certainly something may
be done before the eight years are up—the possi-
bilities of this were referred to at the meeting in
London—but there will have to be special legisla-
tion to deal with the matter. And, of course, a con-
tract having been made, the tramway people will
have to be a party to any variation of the present
terms.

Barring the mine itself, there soon won’t be much
of the old Mount Morgan left. The death of Walter
Russell Hall was directly responsible for the be-
ginning of the changes; and when they started
there seemed no stopping them. General manager,
underground manager, surface manager, secretary —
all are new men. The purchase of the Walter
Russell Hall shares by the Baillieu-Robinson syndi-
cate produced radical changes in the Board also —
and, by the way, in the terms on which the direc-
tors hold their seats. The new brooms are at least
raising a dust. What will happen when the dust settles
has yet to be seen. Profits for the half-year were
certainly nothing to crow over; but this was no
doubt due to the change-over. “Don’t blame us,”
the new men say in effect; “blame our predeces-
sors.” For instance: “The power plant has been the
source of a good deal of trouble during the year,
the type of machines there installed being not suit-
able for the class of work we are now doing. The
breakdown of some of the electric generators last
month and minor breakdowns during previous
months have lost the co, in its production some 800
tons of blister copper, besides which the mainte-
nance , of power plant has proved a verv expensive
item.” A new power plant is now under construc-
tion. Some of it will be ready early next year.
New converters, which were put in a While ago,
will be hung up until then, and three new blast
furnaces will be got ready for blowing in about
March next.
A very important feature of the new men’s activi-
ties is the utilisation of ores hitherto cut out from
reserves as being either too silicious Or too low
grade. General-manager Magnus reckons that, in
order to work out these ores simultaneously with
the smelting ores, at least 500 tons a day must be
drawn from them—probably more, as their limits
are not yet reached. He has had an experimental
concentrating unit going for some months now, and
finds profit-making easy. How much profit there is
in the business he is too shrewd a Yankee to say
mst yet. However, he has apparently satisfied the
reni or a capable at the start of handling
000 tons a day is under order and construction, and
Adam Boyd, the underground man whom Magnus
was wise enough to entice up that way (Magnus
himself being a metallurgist and not a miner), is at
work getting things ready for mining this ore. Also,

building and installing machinery ere looking round
for means of transport of the raw material, half-
manufactured and finished goods. Messrs. Adolf
Bleichert and Co., of London, have issued various
catalogues and leaflets, illustrating and describing a

terested. A specially trained engineer is retained to

From June to September the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yards
of road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. per cub.
yard, and the cost of the spalls per cub. yard aver-
aged 2s. 4d., making a total cost of 4s. 10d. per cub.
yard, broken and spread. Their last contract under
the handbreaking system amounted to 6s. Sd. per
cub. yard stacked on the roadside, and spreading Is. ;
total," 7s. 3d., as against 4s. lOd. — a saving of 2s. sd.
per cub. yard. This result was obtained by using a
Bigelow i6in. x 9in. Patent Stonecrusher, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these breakers.* [2

Messrs. Mephan Ferguson Proprietary, Limited, of
Footscray, Victoria, have secured the contract for
the supply of steel pipes required in connection with
the duplication of the Bombay Water Supply. The
contract amounts to over £226,000, and the pipes
to be used are of the locking bar type, of which Mr.
Mephan Ferguson is the inventor and patentee.
Engineers will be interested to note that this Aus-
tralian invention has been adopted in preference to
other classes of piping, after critical and searching
inquiries as to the merits of the various types of
water pipes made in the world. It is worthy of
note that all machinery required to carry out this
work has been designed and manufactured at the
firm’s Footscray Engineering Works, and will be
shipped to' Bombay, where the pipes will be manu-
factured. *

to help smelt some of the very silicious ore which
is rich in gold, a contract has been made with the
Towers people, who own Iron Island, to drop in
100,000 tons of ironstone. Magnus prefers to put
this’ rich gold stone into the furnaces in spite of its
silicious character. The losses frighten him off
mill-concentration. And he ought to know what he
can do by this time, because during the past year
he has smelted 51,712 tons of ore excluded from" the
old reserves —which means that pretty nearly every
fourth ton sent to the works has been stuff that was
previously reckoned no good. Theoretically, of
course, it is a fine thing to work out all your ore,
the poor with the good, for the time generally comes
when you can’t work the poor by itself. But it’s a
question whether Magnus in this case has achieved
more than a technical triumph. He had an excep-
tional market for copper; and it is probable that,
in order to get the advantage of the market, he
should have used his lame plant on the better stuff,
and waited for the reorganisation to be complete
before bogging into the poor-grade stuff. That is,
assuming that the better stuff was procurable—which,
of course, isn’t certain. There has been many a
time during the last few years when, in spite of its
vast reserves, Mount Morgan has had to scratch hard
to get the ore necessary to keep the furnaces fed.
That part of the business suffered terribly when
Seale died a few years ago. One way and another,
the new plant is going to cost close-up £250,000.
Hence the cut of £lOO,OOO out of last year’s profits
for a rebuilding fund.
But shareholders needn’t be alarmed. There seems
to be no reason whv the directors should make a
habit of these cuts. The balance-sheet as at May, 1913,
shows no liabilities whatever to the public. On the other
side there were liquid and semi-liquid assets of
£511,382, exclusive of shares and debentures in other
cos. ; and out of that there is only £50,000 to come for
a quarter’s dividend. Nothing is more certain than
that the new crowd who have thrust a vigorous finger
in the pie will make as much money out of the
mine as they can. and do it as quickly as they can.
Walter Russell Hall and his friends weren’t built
that way. They wouldn’t force things. The new
people will. They will rush matters along for a
few years —and then Mount Morgan will Drobably see
the end of them. Dividends are too slow for the
modern money-spinner of the Baillieu-Robinson pat-
tern. There is quicker money in getting in at £3,
whooping things up till shares are worth £7 in a
few years, and thereupon dropping out. That will
probably be the policy.

This bank was, born just 25 years ago, and that
wasn’t a good time for any bank’s mother to earn the
baby bonus. The infant fell into trouble promptly,
inevitably and thoroughly; and, looking back now,
it is a marvel that it didn’t put up its shutters for
good. It was probably saved by the fact that it
didn’t attempt to humbug itself into any false ideas
about the position. It just sat down to years of hard,
careful work in order to save up and put by another
lot of capital to replace the lot which it knew was
lost. Queensland’s remarkable capacity for pouring
out money from its wool and cattle and sugar lands
made the task comparatively easy and unexpectedly
rapid. For years, as profits in excess of the very
modest dividends were made, they were written off—-
the management’s hint that the canital wasn t yet
intact. Then came a time, a couple of years ago,
when the surplus profits, instead of being written
off, were allowed to accumulate to the credit of Profit
and Loss. Now, apparently quite sat'sfied that the
capital is represented by assets worth 20s. in the £,
with presumably a trifle laid by inside to cover con-
tingencies, the directors for the first time for very
many years make a substantial addition to the formal
reserve fund—£7soo. Simultaneously, a dividend
at the rate of 7 p.c. is paid—the highest in the bank s
history. For some years past it had been plain that
the business could stand more capital. There was
evident disinclination, however, to ask for any un i
the old capital had been thoroughly restored. that
done, 255. was called on the 50,00!) shares,
making them paid up to 655. That f, ayes
them with a Lability of 755. The other 60s.
of shares originally £lO was written off as lo 't
the bad old days. As this 255. of capital has been
coming in by instalments during the past two h
years, the earnings on total funds (capital and -
serves) can’t be stated with accuracy. For the P •
half-year they were at the rate of about £9 •
p.c. p.a.—a figure which suggests that there isn
much wrong with the assets nowadays.

Of the £l6BO mentioned in the last line the divi-
dend tax takes £BO, and £lOOO will be absorbed in
paying shares up another Is. This will make them
13s. paid ; but as they are liable for another 375.,
there is plenty of room for further bonus distributions
if the profits can stand them. It seems hardly possi-
ble, though, to keep distributions going at the recent
rapid rate. Every addition means that a trifle more
is called for to pay the ordinary 10 p.c.
div. ; and there hasn’t been much left over
even in the past two or three years to
add to reserves. The business certainly keeps
almost astonishingly profitable. On a paid capital
of £12,000 and reserves of £14,450 at the start of the
half-year, net earnings were at the voluptuous rate
of 14$ p.c. ; and it’s a nice business that will do
that when practically the whole of the funds are in
gilt-edged securities. Yet the solid fact remains that,
though its earnings are, as a rule, so very good, the
trustee co. does far more for its money than most
people who work on a commission. It is work, too,
that must be done; and there is no comparison
between getting it done by people who are trained to
the job, have special facilities for doing it, and put
up security that they will do it properly and go on
doing it, and by amateurs who may as likely as not
be either dead or missing when the time comes for
them to take up their trust. This co. can’t complain
that its superior advantages are not being recognised
in its own field. Six years ago the estates in hand
were valued at only £415,999; to-day the total is
£1,662,137.
Work at Lloyd’s, Burraga (N.S.W.), has
been retarded by the unusual wetness of the season.
For the last fortnight 1588 tons of 4.8 p.c. ore
were raised and 1500 tons sent to the mill. The
return was 249 tons of concentrates worth 14.06
p.c. copper, and 91 tons of picked ore worth 11.85
p.c. These products are smelted with slag, making
a total of 339 tons, which returned 50 tons matte
containing 32.2 tons copper and low values for gold
and silver. The matte during the fortnight averaged
64.4 p.c. copper, and is being shipped in that form
to refining works. Slime concentrates will be pro-
duced when the Minerals Separation plant is com-
pleted, and when this is done, the extraction of
copper from crude ore assaying 4.8 p.c. should show
considerable improvement. At present, the loss in
slimes, tailings and slag is very heavy. Firewood is
difficult to obtain in wet weather, and damp wood
isn’t an ideal fuel; but these troubles are small com-
pared with the heavy and continuous loss of copper.
The first set of bores at old Lambing Flat
(N.S.W.) have not justified the water that was poured
into that little Melbourne float. Work was started
at the top of the lead, and little else than blanks
was bored into. Now the camp is pitched 17 chains
farther along the gutter, to see if the hatters left
anything down stream. Meanwhile Frank Powell,
who has charge of the operation, continues as full of
cheer as a pear is full of juice.
Whether the Broken Hill Junction North
office has its roof in Collins House or Sydney doesn’t
count, so long as someone nails down the shing’es
that lie about loose. A few outsiders knew first about
the late fearful balance-sheet, and the market reeked
with the news of the sale of the zinc-lead concen-
trates before the transaction got *into print. *
In 1912 Bullfinch Prop. (W.A.) paid
away £18,252. of which £15,347 went in mine
development. Sundry receipts, mostly transfer fees,
totalled £315, leaving a debit for the year of
£17,937. No ore was treated during this period.
A summary of the results of crushings for the cur-
rent year is given in the report, but nothing is said
about the prospects of the undertaking, except that
reserves are estimated to have a gross value of
£409 736, and to represent something over £200.000
profit ’in sight. On December 31, 1912, the liabili-
ties amounted to £31.771, but against this the co.
had, besides mine, plant and ore reserves, stores
apnroximatelv worth £5600. Profits from ore treat-
ment to the end of June totalled about £36,000, so
the co. is now out of debt, and has a few thousands
in hand. # *
