» * * »
There is strong local objection to the
terms of Chaffers (W.A.) reconstruction on the
ground that Lionel Robinson and Co. are getting too
much of the lolly. The assessment on 500,000 shares
amounts to £37,500, and the commission to be paid
to Robinson and Co. for guaranteeing this number
works out at almost 10 p.c. Recent developments, it
is claimed, make it practically certain that the re-
construction will go through, and for a co. to pay 10
p.c. com. and offer a three years’ option over 86,604
reserve shares at par for guaranteeing what looks like
a certainty is regarded by the objectors as giving
away too much of a good thing. Nevertheless, money
for the resuscitation of Westralian mining ventures
cannot be got in London for nothing, and it is doubt-
full if it could be got there at present without Robin-
son’s help. Therefore he and his party have the
box seat.
* * * *
Edna May (W.A.) has its new mill in
full swing, and intends to clean up at the end of this
month. Plant is stated to be running well, and the
amalgamating plates to be all right. The co. has
taken a three months’ option on the Myrtle East, situ-
ated about 200yds. east of its own show. A little
development has been done in the new show, and
some ounce ore is exposed in its workings. Terms
of option are £3OOO and a tenth interest.
* * * *
Lionel Robinson and party have manned
their Slippery Gimlet option at Ora Banda (W.A.).
This is taken to signify that they mean business,
and are getting ready to place the Gimlet and sur-
rounding leases on the London market. Meantime,
the praises of the neighboring Victorious mine are
being chanted in order to help matters.
A Melbourne co. with a nominal capital
of £2400 in £2O shares has been formed to work
a certain gold-bearing area on Hampton Plains
Estate (W.A.). Portion of the ground has been
tried before by others, but reports recently obtained
from two experts infer that the aforesaid others
missed a lot.
Poseidon Alluvial (Vic.) looks like ger-
minating into a rather feeble wallflower. It has
been superphosphated with £2361 of gold from the
mine and £3485 of calls, but isn’t yet even peeping
into the dividend list. The last trouble has been over
some slum which slushed into the leading drives, to
worry the miners and stick up the wheeler boys. Most
of this drifty ground hangs round the junction of the
Poseidon and the Main gutters, so no exact estimate of
the trouble ahead of the co. can be made. What
they ought to do is to go back to the old order of
things—stop panelling, drain the ground, cut off
another block of the lead, and call up a bit more
capital. All this is heroic, but it pays to be heroic
in deep-lead work, when the wash is green and heavy.
The average of the dirt treated for the past six
months is 2|oz., and it takes Oathcart Central to top
that, so the outlook isn’t dreary.
* * * *
Over 2000 ft. of tunnel has landed the
Homeward Bound, Yackandandah (Vic.), on a big
formation that assays up to an ounce, though there
is more that doesn’t go a quarter of it. The co.
wants cash to test the stone, and shoves out 40,000
shares paid up to 15s. by way of a preliminary excur-
sion. *
“Some Phases of Mining in Victoria” is
the title of a lecture that H. Herman (the local
Director of Geological Survey) will deliver in the
Board room at the Melbourne Chamber of Mines on
Friday Legal managers and secretaries invite atten-
tion to the matter of Herman’s discourse, so it is
presumed by the cynical that he will have nothing
to say about certain phases of legal management.

Lake and Currie do the talk for the
Briseis in London, while Lindesay Clark runs
the show at Derby (Tas.), and keeps silent.
Hence Australians have to get facts about, Briseis
filtered through Lake and Currie. That firm s prac-
tice is to leave a bit over for emergencies and sur-
prises. Therefore, when they put Krushka Flat con-
tents at 750 tons black tin, a bit can be added for
trimmings. On Lake and Currie’s statement of costs
there is £lOO,OOO profit in sight in that bit ot
ground. On the Ringarooma leases water will have
to be pumped for many a long day before the basalt-
crusted drift can be sucked into the sluice box. One
slab of this area Lake and Currie think contains 3288
tons metallic tin. The difference between getting this
stuff and the other stuff lying in the Krushka Flat is
about £4O per ton. But let the total cost be £9O
and tin worth £lBO, and a profit of £9O per ton
remains. Multiply that amount by 3288 tons, and it
will be seen that £295,920 should be earned. This
is the Krushka Flat profit, so that , lt . i ap P® ars as if
the co.’s shares are not over-valued by the dreary
market. Divs. are, however, sure to be spread over
a long period, and a modern man won’t wait. More-
over, S just as tin has slid from £230 per ton to
£lBO, so it may slither down to £l5O. j[ f ®°T7
and the Lord forbid—the foregoing estimated profit
will have to be written down towards nothing.

IT STRENGTHENED MY THROAT.
“1 had a weak throat,” writes Mr. C. V. H. Stal-
ed, 10 Bunbury Street, Fofttscray (Vic.). “The
slightest change in the weather would affect it, and
I would have to go home from work feeling fit for
nothing. My sister swears by Chamberlain’s Cough
Remedy, so I thought I would try it. I did so, and
can honestly say it has worked wonders, for my
throat is now quite strong.”*
Charles

WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, July 14, 1913.
My Dear Arini, —
The woes of the vaccinated are making an
epidemic of groans and high temperatures in
our best suburbs. The Sore Arm Brigade
includes every other person who hasn’t a
sore leg and a limp. The disease itself —-
whatever it may be —is a mere picnic to the
preventive. If the punctured takes his
lymph too seriously he climbs up to 103 deg.
without a stepladder. At the Board of
Health and the Town Hall, very large after-
noon parties surge round the vast policeman
who stays the stampede with the white cot-
ton gloves of the law.
The innumerable Little Mother, aged any-
thing from five to 10 years, dragging in her
wake, by their dyed garments, w r hich didn’t
come from Bozrah, but more probably from
Surry Hills, small and insubordinate rela-
tives, has been a feature of the Board of
Health At Homes. The other afternoon the
welkin —that portion of the welkin which
domes or welks over Macquarie-street—rang
with the yells of a toddler aged about 24
full moons. “Come on now —yer got ter be
done —yer needn’t bawl like that.” The
still Unpunctured, struggling by inches to
the man with the lymph, made way for the
Little Mother. _ The accumulated ages of
the pair couldn’t have been more than nine
years. “’E’s got ter be done,” said the
juvenile chaperon in explanation, “an’ ’e
don’t want ter to be! See wot I’ll give yer
after yer been done —orlright then, I’ll ’ave
the bulls eyes meself!” Kindly womenfolk
made various abortive efforts to dry the
tears of the rebel, who howled so much the
more. She —rather dusty about her lingerie,
but bright-eyed and resolute—lifted Him —a
cataract of kicks —high up and called aloud
(though no one had as yet asked for them)
their full names and address. “An’ we ain’t
ever been done before,” said She, holding
out all their four arms for inspection. Half
a dozen more Little Mothers lurked on the
edge of the crowd, showing a courage that
older folk might well emulate, and fiercely
resentful of the success of the foster-parent
of the yelling two-year-old. “It ain’t ’is
turn to be done—she brought ’im a long
whiles after I come with my lot —no, I
won’t be quiet! Let the copper take me
up But the majesty of the Law pre-
vailed. An infant insurrection to be imme-
djately vaccinated was quelled of policeman
XIZ, who inquired in a voice of thunder,
‘vvhat’s all this row about?”
Sydney has surged with the invasion of
the I 010 sheep fraternity. Mutton matrons
in their prime and frisky young lambs in
their first season tore round the Paddington
Town Hall the other night. The Civil Am-
bulance Ball was the occasion. It was
chiefly a polo party. Players who smacked
the hard white balls at Kensington last week
U P niost of the tickets, and turned the
affair into a spree for themselves and their
friends. Decorations were good and strongly
reminiscent of the defunct Shakspeare Ball,
where once the same paper hollyhocks and
roses bloomed against the cardboard front
of Ann Hathaway’s cottage in the Town
Hall. The Paddington Town Hall is a
simple proposition, and trims up more
easily. “Society” gave a mild exhibition of
its general resemblance to a bear-garden when
it got itself jammed together outside the
female cloak-room. This was early in the
evening. Our best young men at this epoch
were puffing cigarette smoke over the beauti-
fully undressed shoulders of our most ex-
pensive girls, and condescending to ask for
dances in that airy fashion which shows that
the dancing man knows his priceless value
these days.
Han - s , e c. Mrs. Macneill Simpson got
through the throng in a cloud of raven dra-
neries that showed the glitter of small, shiny
white stones over the shoulders. Mr. Jim
Osborne stuck his head through a wreath or
horsecollar of paper herbage, affixpd a piece
“f. lemonade wire to one eye, and gave his
friends an excellent imitation of the Ass in
, A Midsummer Night’s Dream.” One of
the Philip Morton girls floated past in dia-
phanous white draperies. Polo man George
Mack and Mrs. George (who used to be a
and a herd of Willsallens,
Osbornes, Whites and a Nivison or two
bustled upstairs to the ballroom. Frank
Osborne, beaming with good nature, ambled
along in the. wake of his countless relatives.
The municipal Laytons gazed over the
heads of most of the small dancing men who
were proudly guiding Junoesque maidens
round the mazy waltz. The Turkey trot,
which consists of a good d al of farmvard
knee action, was amongst those present. Mrs.
J. J. Rouse, in Quaker grey, watched a
nice-looking young daughter caper round.
Mrs. Sydney Jamieson’s delicate cameo pro-
file looked over a white shoulder draped in
a film of azure blue. The Leonard Dodds
lady, magnificently upholstered in a gown of
velvet and lace that was too intricate to be
hurriedly grasped with the tail of my eye,
was a sartorial event of the party. Mrs.
Willie Anderson, who used to be Lily See,
\yas one of the prettiest young matrons at
the revel. The imposing appearance of Mrs.
Spencer Brunton was swathed in the species
of black gown—hung with diamonds
over the foam of corsage lingerie—that the
deserving poor can only see through tears of
envy. The white blaze of fierce electric light
bit through the thin pink rags that were sup-
posed to shade the ballroom lights. A revela-
tion in cosmetics, laid on with the generous
disregard to quantity that marks Billjim
when he paints the fowlhouse, was the result.
Polo ponies thundered across the quaking
sods at Kensington on Saturday, for : the
finish of the tussle for the Dudley Cup. The
play was good in spots and streaked with
tame places when the ball was tucked up in
a hole and the players smote the atmosphere
with some dust. One or two ponies took it
into their heads to roll over more or less on
their riders, who rose up none the worse for
this, strenuous form of physical culture. The
terrific rush of the Gee-Gee’s polo team was
amongst the missing at last week’s battle
of the ball. Narromine won the Dudley
trophy, and girls with green and white rib-
bons in their coats chortled with joy. A
strong Mack clan barracked for the Narro-
mine boys, and treble yells of applause for
“Boxer” Stevenson, who played a dashing
game, greeted every lucky smack at the ball,
xtiuswellbrook A team got away with the
Knox Cup just as the air was getting a
very cold chill in its evening breeze. Gov-
ernor Strickland and Lady Edeline drove
out in a victoria. The rest of the family
(excepting the baby, who is too young to
anoreciate polo) arrived in a smart little
governess cart with a dark green body and
a pale roan horse. The Admiral tramped
round with his Ex. while Mack senior—
the proud parent of two polo players—sat
in the vice-regal chariot to pass the time of
day with Ladv Edeline. The Maxwell
Allans, some of the Manar Gordon clan, the
George. Fullers, and a few thousand or less
of Whites, Osbornes, Willsallens, Nivisons,
Macks and Macarthurs shivered'amongst the
spectators.
The early-rising habits of Governor
Strickland and his young daughters are
ahead of the loud alarum of the kookaburra
out Rose Bay way. Before that uncertain
timekeeper opens his mouth to yawn at the
morning fog, horses’ hoofs clatter down the
red gravel to the gates, and canter off to
meet the sunrise, or to hold applauding con-
troversy with the morning star. The family
desire to ride out to greet the time o’ day
as it winks over the edge of the sea is only
second to its unswerving devotion to chess.
Two, at least, of the youthful maidens of
the house are as keen about this ancient
game as their vice-regal papa. The Strick-
land infant, a. puny morsel when she arrived
here, is putting on weight with a reckless
indifference to her waist measurement. Baby
S. is improving so fast that another year
or two may see the family instinct to play
chess suddenly break out in her infant
cranium. Colonel Campbell, A.D.C., who
used to be in the Westralian militia, gener-
ally squires the kindergarten when it takes
its official walks abroad. He looks as if
he could be trusted with an entire boarding-
school of curls and giggles.
The Cullen dame opens a bazaar in the
Royal .Art Society’s loft on July 31. The
cause is a Jumble Depbt that hangs out its
sign as a really reliable rag-store in
Surry Hills. The Girls’ Realm Guild made
up its mind that a vast amount of clothes
have been regularly thrown away by extra-
vagant young minxes in easy circum-
stances. They fixed up a depot where
honorary workers sell these garments at
slaughter prices—a shilling for a shirt or
3d. for a hat —to a large and enthusiastic
circle of customers in Hard-up streets. Now
they want to collect a horse of refined man-
ners and not too big an appetite, and a van
to gather more superfluous clothes than the
uncertain habits of the average carriers’
horseflesh can deliver at schedule time.
The late Justice R. E. O’Connor’s por-
trait in oils is at present hanging (on loan)
in the Australian court of our National
Gallery. It is a large decorative canvas,
chiefly remarkable for the subject’s easy pose
and its benign likeness to the original—one
of the best Australians who ever plunged
too deeply into his own pockets to help im-
pecunious friends. Artist Percv Spence has
nainted the benevolent appearance of the
late R. E. O Connor in his judicial uphol-
stery. The kindly eyes peer out under the
legal roof of curled horsehair, and the figure
is draped in the • learned petticoats of the
Law. The Bar or some of its wigs, gave
this portrait to the High Court, so it is
only a visitor here.
mi £ hty Polo Burnham’s engagement
to a Melbourne girl has been a Blow to some
ot our own Society damsels. One short vear
all had hopes. Then came the
affliction or the eviction.” Up to the last
scream of the engine that tore the G-G
Denmans from the Ma State, distracted
young maidens hung about the Gee-Gee’s
Young Gentlemen, and flung last farewells
at their baggage. Now all is over “Mel-
bourne,” said one of the disconsolates rather
bitterly, “gets everything except the small-
pox!” Which was cruelly unjust. Mel-
bourne doesn’t get the Harbor.
Mrs. George Craig, of Wellington (M.L.),
is staying with some relatives at Neutral
Bay for a while. A bout of ill-health drove
her to this blue water-front to recuperate,
and she is doing it with much success.
Hurried word drops in that Lieutenant
Anderson, R.A.G.A., is engaged to the nicest
girl he has ever met. Her name is Ethel.
She is a daughter of the late Captain But-
cher and Mrs. Butcher, of Albany (W.A.).
Mr. Anderson, who is permanently a gunner,
as you see by the shocking length of his offi-
cial tail, camps at the Forts, Albany.
Mrs. Ronald Forbes, wife of the A.D.C.
with the plaid accent in his family escut-
cheon, tripped back from Queenslana in time
for the Ambulance Polo ball. At Saturday’s
meet of polo ponies, out Kensington way,
Mrs. Ronald wore a saucy black beaver hat,
with a purple plume falling off its brim.
The rest of her attire was buried under a
brown fur coat that was snug enough to
warm up a' young iceberg.
His Anglican Legs of Australia has re-
turned to the Sydney fold, after a hurried
scamper through Bible Societies, etc., on the
All Red Route. Mrs. Archbishop Wright,
who scampered with his Gaiters, is now tear-
ing through an orgy of triumphal tea and
tarts. If this welcome-home pastry isn’t
soon eaten to its final crumb, the Welcomed
will have to fast for a few days, to repair
their religious convictions —I mean their
digestion.
The Linschoten dallied here a few days
longer than its original date of departure
from this port. Amongst its passengers is
the indefatigable Beatrice Grimshaw, who
changed her mind about going to the Malay
Archipelago. She returns to her palatial
humpy of two rooms or so on the beetling
brow of a Port Moresby knoll, for another
brilliant attack on local scenery. Miss
Grimshaw slings a graphic pen across that
sun-baked territory, with no rival of any
consequence to steal her thunder.
Maoriland’s once amazingly busy repre-
sentative here, E. H. Montgomery, is now
taking a long, long holiday under six feet
of his native soil, in or near Auckland. His
blithe and buoyant temperament incited him
to rush through a mountainous task, which
included everything from immigration agent
to social ambassador and general rouseabout
of Dominion interests so far as they were
located in this State. He was the beginning
and the end and the middle of the M.L.
Tourist Bureau in Sydney, and his sudden
death has steeped that department in mourn-
ing. The late lamented Montgomery was
one of the best. The light flickered out so
quickly that he was dead before his innu-
merable friends knew that he was ill.
The Man with the Scythe has been work-
ing overtime lately in these parts, and hack-
ing down so many apparently healthy
humans, that the obituary notice boards are
getting overcrowded. Amongst his most re-
cent and loveliest victims was the charming
Mollie Willcox. She was the only daughter
of an adoring widowed parent, souatter Will-
cox, of Beanbaa station, up Coonamble way,
A few minutes before Death gave his im-
perative knock at the homestead gate, the
pretty Mollie was in her usual rosebud bloom
of health. She was dressed to go to the
Bachelors’ Ball. Fianc6 R. Bull was chat-
ting to her when she said she felt very hot,
and fell down in a dead faint. Willcox
pere, who was in Sydney, rushed to Coon-
amble with two of our most famous special-
ists. But the ewe lamb never rallied. A
clot on the brain or something of the kind
was the technical explanation. The beloved
Mollie was buried about 48 hours after the
first symptom of the tragedy. Her wedding
was to have occurred when September tossed
the wattle blossom round Coonamble.
A lively young damsel declares that last
week’s hop in St. Paul’s College was the
most attractive festivity that academy has
roofed for many a day. The party was
given by the warden, Dr. Radford, and the
students, who rested from their labors at
the ’Varsity for the occasion. Mrs. Warden
Radford, Mrs. Busby, Mrs. C. G. Wade (who
has a son at Paul’s), and Mrs. Barff (who
belongs to the serious Uni’ set, which never
wears hair-pads and seldom powders its
nose), chaperoned the girl brigade. Jap-
anese lanterns bobbed in the quadrangle.
The ballroom was elegantly attired in
mixed flowers. Students had turned their
private lairs into sitting-out snuggeries. Two
easy chairs, a fire, the family photos, and
the table at which young Hopeful attacks the
coming exam., with a wet towel round his
head, made a cosy retreat (for two) after
the madding dance. ’Varsity boys had
asked all the best-looking young women of
their acquaintance. Miss Ogilvie, from New
England sheep walks, the two Svdnev Jones
sisters, Mollie David, Miss Colletti, who
counts as a beauty, the tall Brennan girls
(Florence and Adele) and a Merewether
maiden were amongst the most dressy young
persons present.
Hon. Sec. Mrs. Heydon, Mrs. Ted Antill
and Mrs. Seely (hon. treas.) helped to push
a cheque into the yawning coffers of
the Woolloomooloo Day Nursery the other
afternoon. A children’s dance in aid of that
useful charity hummed with youngsters in
fancy dress. The Paddington Town Hall,
where this annual revel always thumps the
floor with the bounding steps of young and
hearty dancers, wore the Civil Ambulance
ball herbage on its walls, and fluttered a few
relics of the previous Sports Union hop, over-
head. The Day Nursery takes care of small
fry whose mammas go out by the day to do
chores for other folks. It feeds, amuses, and
I fancy, gives a snack of kindergarten to in-
fants who are rising five or six years. To
the babes of Woolloomooloo this anchorage,
where they can’t bump their heads, fall into
tubs of boiling water, or set the premises on
fire, is a cheerful haven, flowing with milk
and sponge cake. It will thankfully spend
any contribution you can send along.
America’s star soprano, Lillian Nordica,
is alleged to be loaded to the Plimsoll line
with ropes of pearls, encrustations of dia-
monds and the contents of a large emerald
mine. She doesn’t wear the lot at once, but
mercifully displays these glittering baubles
on consecutive evenings. The U.S. warbler
collects precious stones by the bushel as
easily- as the average female toiler collects
washing, or grey hairs, or a hole in the heel
of her stocking. The haunted Czar and his
sad-eyed Czarina once found a peaceful in-
terval when their bomb-shattered nerves
allowed them to drink in the Nordica trill.
They promptly loaded the singer with a
green serpent bracelet ouilt of emeralds the
size of sparrows’ eggs—or thereabouts—as
a slight token of their esteem. American
audiences have more or less pelted the lady
with diamond tiaras. One of these costly
gauds runs into more carats than I dare to
mention in cold print. Some of these gems
will blaze in our Town Hall in a week or
two. Nordica is nearing this port in the
Sonoma. She only engaged the top deck, or
most of it, for herself and suite, and all the
hold for her baggage. The rest of her bag-
gage is being towed behind, all except her
supremely best hat, which follows in another
ship under escort of a man-of-war. Arrange-
ments have been made with friendly Powers,
so that even if war breaks out while I write,
and the British fleet is fully occupied, the
best Nordica hat will arrive here safely. It
reposes under the protection of an inter-
national guarantee. In fact, it has been
neutralised like Belgium—which is just about
the same size.
The Butt-Rumfords finished here, for the
present, at a matinee that was packed with
enthusiasm and good Federal legal tender.
They’ll look in again during August, to col-
lect any cash they may have missed during
their recent swoop on the emotions of plain
citizens. They turn their vaccination marks
due north before returning to the Cabbage
Patch via Sydney.
Pianist Harold Craxton, of the Kennerley-
Rumford team, strayed into the Civil Ambu-
lance ball the other night He looked mildly
msthetic in the usual swallow-tail that
evaded the ordinary by gaping open over a
soulful waistcoat of lizard grey. Plis hya-
cinthine locks continue to escape the mow-
ing down process that pursues the commer-
cial cranium or the suburban lawn. Craxton’s
small, quaint personality encloses a graceful
turn for composition. His “Come you
Mary” was one of Baritone Rumford’s most
popular encore numbers.
David Bispliam’s good-bye to Sydney on
Saturday evening dragged an enthusiastic
audienpe from the flank of the domestic hob.
His extensive baritone had been laid on to
the Symphony concert in the afternoon. He
shook the Town Hall rafters at his own
recital with “Danny Deever” and “The Two
Grenadiers” nevertheless. Beethoven’s
“Hymn of the Creation,” one of Bispham’s
greatest efforts, starred his farewell warble.
There was no sign of vaccination tremolo in
the American singer’s left arm when he imi-
tated the bagpipes in the lilt of Robbie
Burns’ runaway soldier —the one who fled
from the Battle of Killiecrankie. Pianist
Harry Gilbert is likely to have an inter-
esting limp in a day or two. He was punc-
tured under the knee so as to keep both
hands in active service. Gilbert was re-
called twice for his spirited attack on
Chopin’s Ballade No. 111. This was his
biff farewell at the grand piano in this city.
Bispham, by the way. is under contract for
a 42 weeks’ engagement in a leading role
m light opera. A New York management
has signed and sealed David the singer at a
stiff salary for this event.
Chappell and Co. have forwarded two fat
consignments of melody. There’s just space
enough to squeeze in the “Little Grey Home
in the West,” by Herman Lohr, and a few
other items. If the “Grey Home” had been
a few feet larger it wouldn’t have had a
hope of being mentioned as a melodious
valse, suited to the technique of 12 sum-
mers. Marjorie Hope Lumley contributes
an album of fine songs One is a charming
trifle that flutters round “a thin little wisp
of. new-horn moon.” More melody to a
middle-aged summer moon is built on vaude-
ville lines by Leslie Elliot. Three African
dances, the compositions of Montague Ring,
are for the schoolgirl who likes simplicity
starred by a few octaves.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORAIN.

“ ABORIGINALITIES,” from -The Bulletin.”
Selected by Charles Bertie, Author of “Old Sydney,” etc.
WITH 39 ILLUSTRATIONS BY LIONEL LINDSAY.
Price, 1/-; Post free, 1/1.
Readers of The Bulletin, the most popular paper in Australia, will welcome this book with
enthusiasm, as it will enable them to enjoy afresh many a hearty joke that tickled them years ago.
Mr. Bertie has selected only the best, so that readers are saved the weeding-out process inevitable
in such a collection of contributions as appears in a weekly paper. The supreme advantage of
having an artist like Lindsay to illumine the text will be apparent the moment you open this book,
which should prove a mirth-producer second to none. To those who may not be Bulletin
readers we say, “Buy a copy of ‘Aboriginalities’ at once. You will laugh and grow fat.”
“ BILL’S IDEES,
5 > By
A. G. STEPHENS
(“The Bookfellow ”).
Fully Illustrated. Price, 1/-; Post Free, 1/1.
In “BILL’S IDEES” the N.S.W. Bookstall Co., Ltd., has the pleasure of introducing Mr. A. G.
Stephens as a new Australian humorist. Mr. Stephens’ reputation as the doyen of Australian critics
is widespread, and he has several books of prose and verse to his credit. “Bill’s Idees” is the first
book in which he has given his natural humor free play—as we venture to think, with the happiest
results.
Many years ago the original of “Bill” was a larrikin in Sydney—the leader of a “push.” He
reformed himself, and for a long time bas been a highly-respected citizen; as he says, improving
his mind, cultivating his good instincts, and trying to live up to his ideal. Bill’s ideal is “a quiet
bloke with a quiet wife and a quiet dog with a quiet name.” Bill has had to struggle to reach his
ideal, because he had a lot of bad instincts to get rid of, and because he never had much chance in
the way of education. Still, with the help of his favorite author, Vere Foster, he is getting along
nicely. Bill’s story is told in his own words. He is interesting when he looks back upon his unre-
generate past, and is no less interesting when he contemplates his reformed present. Hard life has
taught him a philosophy of his own, worth considering. His native humor is of the kind called
“pawlcy,” and he enjoys a joke against himself. Bill isn’t a fool, and he isn t a clown , perhaps
his readers will seldom laugh right out. But they will get a few broad grins and a whole heap of
chuckles. Bill’s “missus” is several chapters to herself.

For sheer wonder
what modern invention
can compare with the
Pianola Piano? which
not only instantly re-
moves all the manipu-
lative difficulties of
piano-playing, but also
guides you in expres-
sion and execution, as
if the great composers
themselveswere
prompting you.
At the same time,
your interpretation is
as personal and in-
stinctive as if your
hands were touching
the keys. In this
quality the Pianola
Piano is alone.
Those remarkable devices, the
Metrostyle and Themodist,
which give you as complete a
mastery over the music, are not
and never can be duplicated
by any other piano-playing instrument.
The Pianola Piano is a combination of
the Pianola with the famous Steinway,
Weber, and Steck Pianos, and is the
only piano-playing instrument recognised
by the great musicians of to-day.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, July 14, 1913.
My Dear Yarrie,—
The Federal Parliament opened with the
usual useless flourish on Wednesday. The
big grey house made a fine scenic back-
ground for the matinee, which included six
bay horses, purple and powdered postilions
perched on same, an open carriage contain-
ing one Governor-General, and a cloud or
host of scarlet and gold military supers.
The procession provided some entertainment
for Bourke-street, ere it halted at the
Spring-street frontage of the House of Argu-
ment, where a well-polished band burst into
the. usual music incidental to the occasion,
while the small G.-G., who was really very
anxious to get out to the Flemington races,
hurried up the steps with his sword dangling
and his staff panting at his heels. Within
doors, the galleries were packed with women,
some of whom had been there since 11
o’clock. (Imagine, if you can, a three
hours wait for such poor but distinguished
mummery !) The Barttelot lady, a tall, wil-
lowy figure in nut brown, brought her two
boys, and little Judith Denman and
her brother hung about in charge of a
governess. Judith was amusing herself by
practising Genee toe-steps on the mosaic
floor. Mrs. Guise, in a cute little purple
hat, waited on the steps to see the imitation
pageant. She is a stranger to the country,
and is curious about the tribal customs. As
soon as the G.-G. was through with his
famous Parliamentary turn, he simply flew
home, changed into his grey sporting clothes,
and motored out to Flemington in time to
see the third race or thereabouts of the
mid-week G.N. galloping.
The Forrest dame, wearing a beaming
smile, a large feathered hat and some silken
clothing of great value, entertained some
people at tea. (I am now back at Parlia-
ment House.) Flocks of women, many of
them more or less like birds of paradise,
rustled up and down the carved marble stair-
case, and sat about on the comfortable red
leather chairs —the sort that cunning people
build for the comfort of men. Hugh Pat-
terson, chairman of the Canberra picture
selecting committee, had his fellow-judges
with him. The pictures of the dry place
will be sorted out in the Prime Min-
ister’s office and arranged for a special view.
It is expected that legislators will always
be able to raise a thirst by looking at them.
The mask ball in aid of the Alfred Hos-
pital carried so much social weight that it
was completely . crushed. Harmless frivo-
lity was impossible, and plain “Bohemian-
lsm was completely frozen off. Dominoes
and masks -were marked like packing cases
that go down to the sea in ships, and the
alleged revellers carefully inspected one
another’s credentials before signing any
agreements to dance, cavort, rotate or
bound in the manner hereinafter described,
lou never saw such a cautious show in
your life. It was the most circumspect per-
formance ever given with joyous intentions;
but the John Madden dame cast a coldly ap-
proving eye over the crowd from the chap-
mu dais and seemed perfectly satisfied.
Ihe G.-G. and the State potentate came
along to the (supposed) frivol, after dining
solemnly at the Athenaeum. Aide Burn-
ham, bereft of his fiancee, whispered darkly
to Aide Curwen. Victor Hood, not being
on duty, roamed at large amongst the dis-
creet assemblage. The Barttelot Help, out
ot action through his hunting accident,
stayed at home and spent a frivolous even-
ing reading Gibbon’s “Decline and Fall.”
Ills pretty wife came along in a purple
frock with her white shoulders outlined in
silver. Mrs. Ross-Soden blazed in purple
velvet, and two other Society women flared
together in shot purple silk. Mrs. Willie
Sharp (a committee chaperon), also
mourned for the deceased spirit of gaiety
in the sombre color. Mrs. Alawa Buchanan’s
trock was made out of a lace curtain —at
least that is what it suggested. All these
people did not bother about disguise. No
masks or dominoes could have veiled or
obscured their social brilliance, and you
couldn t have hidden their lights under a
bushel without seriously damaging the
bushel. Governor Fuller sat surrounded by
chaperons and looked amazingly cheerful.
It was probably much better than wander-
ing loose in the mob of distrust, and being
cold-shouldered in mistake for some impos-
sible person called Jonesmith or Brown-
green. There were strict rules about smok-
ing in the lounges or corridors, and if you
were not charitably satisfied with the sup-
per and its claret cup, you could go up-
stairs and get champagne. But you had to
be a committee person, or a Governor, or
an accredited representative of one of the
Great Powers.
For the last day of the Flemington Grand
National, the weather was fine and breezy
and the going good. After a succession of
soggy,_ rain-soaked courses and muddy gal-
lops, it was like coming into a large legacy
a nice day. The jumoing races were
delightful to watch. One light bay soared
like a monoplane. I noticed him only once,
and he was then leaping in the ruck/Afraid
that he liked flying so much that the horses
"■ho just pimped high enough hurried in
ahead of him. Every animal in the Steeple-
chase was heavily fancied; the different
clubs were each “on” a different horse, and
everybody seemed keen on picking up the
money they had dropped or hurled away on
previous gallops. As usual, Mr. Wooloash
buzzed about the Birdcage, while Mrs.
Woolcash fussed with the chauffeur over
lunch baskets and food under the elms. The
winter meeting always draws the country
people, and how thev do compete with one
another. The dressing was lavishly expen-
sive, but of the smartly tailored sort that
“station” people seem to “do” to perfec-
tion. Four cheerful Government House
men, dressed uniformly in grey so that you
couldn’t tell the Governors from the Helps,
and most of the time unaccompanied by
womenfolk, roosted in the vice-regal box
just long enough to watch the various races,
then dashed off to interview the uproarious
persons who wear cash slung round their
necks. After the racing, G.-G. Denman,
shadowed by his faithful Burnham, hurried
off by motor to Sunbury, to put in the week-
end with the Russell Clarkes, at Ruperts-
wood.
There were some new and interesting
effects in the frocking, on the mercifully
hne Steeplechase Day, at Flemington. Mrs.
Currie, in a grey, feathery garment, looked
like the story princess who disguised her-
self as a bird. Gladys Chirnside, tailored
in champagne-color, wore as head-covering a
black velvet belltopper. She paraded
proudly with a Camperdown girl who
looked fearfully unhappv—perhaps because
her hat was flat, and bashed and quite
feminine. Nowadays, height is the hall-
mark of hat smartness. Take a quill or a
straight feather (the longer the better),
stick it in the back of a turban of silk, and
lo! you are all right! Another trick is to
stick an American Beauty rose with a fopt-
long stem in your headpiece. It must be at
the back, though; your hat-trimming fol-
lows respectfully behind you always. Mrs.
Isaacs had three purple streak feathers
dipped in vivid red, while daughter Nancy’s
white feather measured exactly (This
doesn _t sound like his Honor’s associate,
does it?) The High Court Miss did the
lawn with a Hammond-Clegg girl, who
scored a poster effect with large civet cat
furs. Ballarat Ham’s daughter—a bride of
yestermonth—looked blooming, and wore a
lavender feather in grey souirrel mufflings.
Widower Brodie, of Sanderson’s, escorted
three smart damsels.. Nan O’Hara, in white
cloth, m with a Riverina squatting party;
and Mrs. Leslie Stuart was swathed in a
smart green frock. Chirnsides were scat-
tered all over the stand and lawn; Mrs.
Buchanan talked horse in the birdcage; and
the McCullochs, from the Western Dis-
trict, gave the biggest lunch party on re-
cord and thereby achieved a certain amount
ot tame.
Inter-State hockey was the chief topic in
flapper circles during last week. The visit-
ing teams managed to escape vaccination
on their way hither, but were carefully
scratched before being despatched home
Ihe young amazons played with proper
ferocity and have many honorable wounds
to show. The Melbourne girls say that
Sydney plays a ‘lovely” game, but that
Adelaide is too rough. After the Melbourne-
f delaide contest, Fanny Rigby, Melbourne’s
best half-back, was lamed and had wrists
and ankles cut. Mrs. Burston gave a tea
party, the following day, in the Botanical
txaidens, and this indomitable young woman
arrived in a cab and was carried
to the tea house on the shoulders of admir-
ing girl-players. Mattie Fogarty, as some-
one expressed it, “got off with a smack on
the jaw : but Miss Clendidden was told to
qint the held on account of an injured knee.
Ihe ardent little player resented being kept
out of the playing area by an anxious
brother. "Its nothing but a touch!” com-
Pmc nec L * e heroine. Though they take
therr hurts lightly, these hockey maidens
don t; approve of very rough play. Thev
?°. n t ™ ln , d , a slap on the shoulder, or a
frightful blow on. the ankle; but when a
player goes on like a prehistoric squaw
they start to get wild.
Gn Saturdav, after a deliriously exciting
week, the hockey girls took the visiting
plajers to see Within the Law.” Cheerful
cripples, with wrists and ankles carefully
bandaged and complexions ruddier than
ever, limped proudly up the mignonette-
green velvet carpet of the Theatre Royal
stairs, and afterwards limped down
again to a jolly supper party in a Collins-
street cafe.

Wtodow S - Per Son who Esca Ped out of the
Even now we can’t quite sort out the true inward-
ness of that cafC raid, which was so like a scene out
of “The Magistrate.” Was it a misfortune or was
it not ? A heap of people are mad because they
weren’t found unlawfully on the premises—young,
unmarried, gay-doggy persons, who feel that their
professed wickedness has been called in question, and
their alleged iniquity has fallen into disrepute. They
try to explain that they are really Mohawks and
Cheerful Cherokees and all that sort of thing, and
this was the only night in years when they weren’t
painting the town red, and doing the homeless eat
act on the tiles or on the top of the Equitable build-
ing, and throwing the decalogue into the Yarra.
They had gone home early on account of toothache.
Then there were the people who were found
but didn’t want to be discovered, in consequence
whereof they fled in hysterics; and there were the
people who were found but didn’t care, in conse-
quence of which they didn’t flee in anything. But
the people who regret that they were found are few,
while the people who are mad because they weren’t
found are many.
During the scatter one of the Very Best matrons
made a hopeless dash for home, only to be held up
by the relentless Law. “Your name, madam ?”
thundered the Statute at the top of its voice. “I
won’t tell you!” panted the poor, desperate lady.
You must!” insisted the respectful Civil and
Criminal Code. “I won't!” reiterated the trembling
Social Light. “You —you must ask my solicitor!”
A young man who was supping at one of the
raided places on the fatal nigiit speaks with irrev-
erent glee of the scramble. “I was just in the
middle of a yarn when I noticed the line of bulky
men coming in. They didn’t look like anything but
police, so I knew it was all up. One of them went
over to the band, and the music died of fright—the
flute expiring absurdly on a wild, high note. Then
they took our names, slowly and carefully, being very
anxious over the spelling. (You know policemen
always have great trouble about spelling.) Some of
the fellows got nasty, and a few of the women tried
to get away. I don’t know which was the
more foolish thing to do. After the waiters had been
taken away, the band revived and played again.
But the music wasn’t the same. For one thing, that
flute had been nearly scared to death and the violin
tried to be jo.yyus in an undertone. Perhaps the
orchestra thought it saw the end of the job approach-
ing It was an extraordinary transition from gav
to glum. e J

Pianist Edward Goll gave two recitals in
the Auditorium last week, and musical Mel-
bourne closed its eyes in rapture and sighed
something to the effect that it hadn’t heard
Chopin served up so divinely for many long
years. Goll could give complete programme*
of Chopin items and still not weary
his audiences. At the first recital he made
a centre dish of Beethoven, played a boiled
sonata of Haydn, flavored the lot with a
httle Schumann, and topped off with six
Chopin studies. Society sat reverently at
his feet in its softest chiffons, and a heavy-
weight dame who implies that she only be-
gins to breathe when in London, murmured
in a dazed way, ‘‘But whay is he living here
when he is such a Success in London
I robable explanation is that Goll was for-
tunate enough to marry a charming and
home-loving Australian widow. Also per-
haps he does better here than in crowded
London. The O’Haras and the Burstons
took their music on the balcony. Law-
yer Waxman, himself a player, was wildlv
enthusiastic aloft. Stead, of the Review of
Reviews, listened with his wife, and Mrs
Ihompson and her daughter sat side bv
side. The latter’s dazzling complexion and
litian hair contrasted nicely with some
white fox furs. Mrs. Tom Hogan’s place in
the audience was marked by a large black
osprey like a fountain of ink. Mrs. Walshe
of Balwyn, threw precedent to the winds
and actually made a journey to town to
listen to son-in-law Edward, who wore his
hair in beautifully-brushed waves and his
music likewise. He responded to the rap-
turous applause with a far-away smile, and
straightway smote the piano again. After-
wards everybody who is anybody went off
Wl £ h a party to feed in a cafA Only the
nobodies eat in the privacy of their homes.

POLITICAL
POINTS
A cablegram from England :
A British departmental document, just issued,
shows that wheat, barley, oats, bread, beef, mutton,
pork, bacon, milk, cheese and eggs were all dearer
in 1912 than previously.
Butter attained the highest figures reached for nine
years past.
Grapes, greengages and strawberries cost more.
Apparently even the blessings of Freetrade
can’t keen down the price of food, which is
rising so fast that it blows the cork out of
the bottle. The population of the earth
keeps multiplying, and the food-growing sur-
faces of the globe don’t expand, so the food
problem gets worse, as a matter of necessity.
Has anyone noticed the remarkable par-
allel between Elijah, the Prophet of Israel,
and Holman, the Premier of N.S.W.? Each
one struck a drought. In one case the Lord
ceased to pour rain on Palestine, and in the
other case Israel ceased to rain loans on
N.S.W. Each man did a famous run.
Elijah ran in front of the King’s chariot
from Mount Carmel to Jezreel; in other
words, he ran almost across the kingdom,
so apparently he was a thin and leggy kind
of man. Holman ran to England, and if
he didn’t get in front of the King’s chariot
he at least probably saw Geo„ V. in his
vehicle. And each man, sitting amid arid
conditions, saw a friendly little cloud on
the horizon. In Elijah's case it foretold
rain and promised to save his reputation.
In Holman’s case it foretells that if the row
between McMillan and Wade becomes much
worse there may be a split among his
enemies, and it promises to save his job.
Bakhap (Tas.) threatens to be the verbal
high-roller of the Senate. In moving the
Address-in-Reply, he introduced himself to
“the paladins of debate” as “a recruit to the
legislative phalanx” thus :
If at present 1 lack anything in regard to a sense
of the responsibility developing upon me through hav-
ing been deputed to assist in regulating the political
affairs of one of the world’s continents, I hope, never-
theless that 1 shall soon acquire that proper sense
of responsibility in the atmosphere of this Chamber.
... .1 shall on the present occasion content myself by
moving the adoption of the Address in Reply in this
formal manner, and shall reserve for a future occa-
sion the exposition of those stores of political wisdom
which I have accumulated.
After that there were 10 minutes for re-
freshments, and a carpenter did some hasty
repairs to the dictionary.
Recent files of Cold Country papers men-
tion that a strike of 250 men and women for
higher wages is in progress at a Barking fac-
tory, a few miles out of London. The present
pay is from 16s. to 225. for men and from 9s.
to 15s. for women, and the week consists of
55 hours. There is nothing very exciting
about all this. Some Cold Land workers get
more than these rates ; plenty get less. But
the matter concerns Australia because the
strikers happen to be match-makers, and Aus-
tralia’s largest firm of match-makers, in Vic-
toria, pays, or paid until it had to shut up
shop lately, 2Ss. to 525. for women, and 50s
to 60s. for men. and the week ran to only 48
hours. Yet, though this meant something
like 400 per cent- more than the English
rates, Australia’s Protective duty against
English-made matches is 6d. per gross of
boxes, or only about 25 per cent, ad val-
orem! When will the Australian Labor
party realise that, when it continuously
pushes up wages and reduces hours, and yet
sinks the fiscal issue, it is only playing into
the hands of the extraneous sweater, and his
agent, the foreign importer?
Perth, which some years ago allowed the
usual benevolent syndicate to give it the
boon of a street, tramway service, got the
usual sickener when it inspected the syndi-
cate at close quarters; and one or two
strikes, in which the co. adopted a hoggish
attitude, settled all hopes of permanent
friendship. Now, therefore, when the Wes-
tralian Parliament has ratified a contract
which the Scaddan Government lately made
to buy out the hdggish company, even the
West Australian raises its voice in a
gentle bleat that “there can be little doubt
but that the system is in the right hands,”
inasmuch as “the suburban railways and
the trams can be worked harmoniously to
the benefit of the public under the Govern-
ment” ; and the poor old paper, which is in
the habit of picturing nationalisation as a
monster with dreadful claws and smoking
nostrils, implores its readers not to be terri-
fied this time, but to reflect that, “though it
is nationalisation, it is the nationalisation
of a public utility that was a monopoly.”
THE SERIOUS MOMENT.
And although I have taken the helm of State at a
moment when storm and cloud encompass us round,
and I am called upon to play the part of the daring
pilot, storm and cloud have always been our native
favoring elements, through which we have won a hun-
dred times in our tempest-tossed career, (Cheers.)
State Premier Holman (N.S.W.).

POOR OLD GREG.
Mr. L. S. Forsyth moved: “That in the opinion of
this conference the time has arrived when the
Farmers and Settlers’ Association of New South
Wales should declare for a straight-out political
party of its own.”
FARMER (to Wade): “Look ’ere, Pm sick of
supportin’ YOU. Corner on out of that and
let ME do a bit of ctintbin for a change.”
Apparently the question involved in the
alleged lack of qualification which surrounds
Victoria’s new Labor M.L.C., Fielding, is to
go undecided for all time. Attorney-General
Drysdale Brown says that Fielding was told
to get himself naturalised two years ago,
when he w T as appointed a J.P., or honor-
arium magistrate. It isn’t certain whether
he did or didn’t the deed. But as Fielding’s
father and grandfather were British sub-
jects, Dry Brown holds that Fielding was
one himself, though born in the United
States, and there was no need for him to
take out naturalisation papers. Brown
adds, in a cold voice, that any common in-
former can take up the question if he likes,
and begin by proving that Fielding isn’t a
British subject. So Dry Brown retires
with the dignity of a gentleman [which he
is, whatever else he isn’t), and leaves the
common informer (who is a very common
creature) with a large contract on his hands.
A few nights ago Joe
Cook delivered a speech in
.Melbourne that was full of
the milk of human kindness.
His party, he said, was full
to the neck with goodwill
towmrds everybody; and he
slopped over into generalisa-
tions about the good time that
was in store for everybody
when the “Liberal” party
got' thoroughly going. But,
on the very same evening,
one of his alleged followers,
Sampson, spoke a piece at
Horsham, and Sampson’s
piece fitted in with Joseph’s
declaration in much the same
way as boiling lead matches
butter. This Sampson, who
was returned by the Wim-
mera cockies with a huge
majority, declared that one
of the first acts of his party
would be to so amend the
Arbitration Act that the
rural workers would be cut
out from its benefits, and the
farmers would be able to
pay what wages they liked,
whereat there were loud
cheers and much hooraying.
Fortunately, there is no
chance of the disastrous pro-
posal of Sampson being car-
ried into effect. Even on his own side there
are men —Wynne, for instance —who would
never vote for a free hand for the cocky.
The incident therefore merely shows the
heterogeneous nature of Joe’s majority, and
its precarious quality.
A “Greater Adelaide” scheme is being
talked of by the suburbs, which run pretty
well from hills to sea. Unley, Nor-
wood, Hindmarsh, Thebarton and St. Peters
have had a preliminary conference, and
the various district councils which come
right up to the City boundaries on some of
its spreading sides are expected to join. The
City itself is for the present sitting back to
watch. Already there is joint action of Citv
and suburbs in the matter of electric trams,
abattoirs, milk-supply, and (practically)
public health ; and from that to combination
in refuse destruction, lighting, and quarry-
purchase should be an easy step.
N.S.W. State Minister Trefle appears
anxious to be the supreme lOU. He wants
to spend “£30,000,000 or £40,000,000 or
£50,000,000” of loan money “to-morrow.”
Which is a record for heroism. Also, on
the part of a member of an allegedly anti-
loan Cabinet, it is a record for cheek.
The N.S.W. Country party is a new
political institution which is much wanted.
Its great desire is to do battle with the
Congestion Policy, under which all the ex-
portable produce of an area of 310.000 square
miles must go to Sydney, or go nowhere. If
Sydney is so far off that it doesn’t pay to
send the goods there; or if the bottle-neck
at Redfern or the wharves at Darling liar
bor are so crowded that the goods can’t get
there—well, the goods must just rot. Said
Donaldson M.L.A. on this subject:—
1 have seen bags of wheat lying [at country rail-
way stations] absolutely unprotected. I have seen
men loading trucks at stations ankle-deep in wheat
from the bags that had rotted. I have seen them
rescuing sound bags from that criminal destruction
of the foodstuff of the world. And yet there are
people in other countries who are absolutely craving
for food—for the bread that is made from that wheat.
Also, what is of much more importance to
N.S.W., there are people who are absolutely
craving for the money they ought to get for
that rotted wheat. When millions of bags
of grain are stranded at country railway
stations, and the State’s stock of cattle and
sheep and pigs and cows, and its butter and
cheese exports, are all decreasing by reason
of the Congestion insanity, it is time for a
new party to develop.
Even Hobart, the place where the awful
municipal franchise obtains —a landlord may
exercise seven votes, an occupier only one—
now owns its tramways. Last week the
Mayor formally took over the lines and the
quaint two-decker cars and the old manager
and staff —after handing out a cheque for
£210,000 for the privilege. The outside
value of the show is £lOO,OOO. according to
experts; but the other £llO,OOO represents
the monopoly-right in the streets which the
old Private Enterprisers got for nothing
some 16 years ago. With rail-less trams
and up-to-date motor-’bus services supersed-
ing street-railways in many cities, including
Leeds, London and Edinburgh, the Hobart
Tram Co. probably considers itself the
luckiest tram co. in the wide, wide world to
have got out when it did. and on such ex-
cellent terms. However, the property-
elected aldermen who made the purchase all
reckon the bargain is a good one. The Mayor
passed his word to the same effect, and said
that he spoke as “one of the oldest share-
holders in the private company,” and there-
fore competent to judge. And the vendors
wear the smile of sweet content. There are
others, of course, but these are the mere
general public who have to foot the bill by
paying twopence a time to ride the shortest
distance measurable at the slowest pace
imaginable on any tramways conceivable.
Melbourne friends of the late lawyer Sam
Gillott suspect him of having died a million-
aire. H e was childless, and it is understood
that his wealth will pass to public charities
after his widow’s decease.
THE GREAT PROGRESSIVE PARTY.
Although the Government may be prevented from
giving due effect to the policy that the people have
endorsed, it still retains the power of preventing anv
further excursions into the domain of Socialism by
the Labor party.— Syd. D.T. July 9. 1913.

BALLADE POLITIQUE a DOUBLE REFRAIN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Scene: A Photographer’s Shop.
Parliamentary (or £l2-a-week) Liberal, and Or-
dinary (or non-stipendiary) Liberal, discovered
viewing full-length photograph of the new Prime
Minister.
Parliamentary Liberal (with sudden enthusiasm):
Hail Liberalism’s brightest star,
That, after lurking dim, afar,
For years, has now contrived to swim
O’er Office’s horizon-rim!
From allegory to desist:
Hail Cook, the Anti-Socialist !
Ordinary Liberal (impatiently):
Silence ! O foolish rhapsodist:
Cook is no Anti-Socialist.
Parl. Lib. (stupefied):
You talk like a Disloyalist:
Cook is an Anti-Socialist.
Ord. Lib. (firmly):
No more than the misguided man
That him, these three years past fore-ran.
What of the Fisher Land Tax ? Say
Will Cook sweep that offence away i
The infamy which confiscates
The choicest of our big estates;
And one of whose provisions drives
Rich absentees to spend their lives
Out here (a place they cannot stand)
Instead of in the Mother-Land.
Not he! None but a satirist
Would call him Anti-Socialist.
Paul. Lib. (furiously):
Scoundrel and secret Laborist!
He is an Anti-Socialist.
Ord. Lib. (with relentless calm):
In that event it would be meet
For him to scrap our “futile fleet,”
And, in its place, hire British craft
To guard Australia fore and aft.
“Conscription” would be swept aside
As hateful to the nation’s pride.
Judge Higgins’s loathly Court would go.
All Anti-Socialists well know
That efforts to control supply.
Demand and such, are hopeless. Why!
Cook has himself exposed the flaws
That lurk in these insensate laws.
And yet he no more means to try
To cause one such reform than I.
No ; Cook may be a Loyalist;
Also a fervent Methodist;
Likewise a pharmacologist;
Or journalist, or novelist;
But that man is a Sciolist
Who terms him Anti-Socialist.
Parl. Lib. (maddened):
Liar! Pro-Boer!! and Atheist!!!
Take that! You never will be missed.
(Throws camera at Ord. Lib., who falls dead.)
So perish all base fabulists.
(Turning once more to photograph, his eyes shining
with Party loyalty and unquestioning, hound-
like trust.)
Hail! Prince of Anti-Socialists !
( Curtain.)
N.S.W.
J. B. DALLEY.

THE SUPERB
RONISCH PIANO.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The first request made by Waldemar
Holberg, the Dane, when he landed in Sydney, was
that he might be matched with Hughie Mehegan.
Naturally, Reginald Baker was dubious. He had
already matched the Australian with Matt Wells, who
was on his way to Australia under a big guarantee,
and it would prejudice that scrap as a drawing pro-
position should the Dane overthrow Mehegan a fort-
night before the great battle. But the Viking got
his way, and on Saturday night he climbed through
the ropes and fixed his eye on the man he was so
anxious to meet. The crowd was astonished to learn
that the apparently little Dane weighed 9st. 1211 b.
—half a pound more than the Australian, who
seemed to tower over him. But it was obvious that
the visitor was heavily handicapped by the shortness
of his reach. This brevity proved disastrous. Many
and many a time when he strove to counter, it was
lack of range that prevented him reaching the mark.
He started with a terrific burst. His speed was
dazzling, his ducking bewildering, and his blows ter-
r.fic. Mehegan looked at him, with a casual ex-
pression, and decided that he was dangerous. He was
partly bull-ant and partly dynamite. Mehegan always
starts slowly, and for at least half the first round
gives the idea of bordering upon the helpless. Wal-
demar is evidently accustomed to get off the mark
at top speed. The shortness of his reach has made
a clincher of him. His short-range blow makes it
necessary to get in close through the other man’s
guard, deliver the wanop or goods or proposition, and
then hang on. It is the right game for his build,
and he worried Mehegan with it at various times.
But it soon became apparent that he was outclassed.
He was of the wrong build to fight an opponent like
the Australian. Mehegan did not adopt his crouch,
neither did he use his old-time smother. But he drove
terrific blows to Holberg’s body, and the splendid
condition of the visitor was evidenced by the way he
stood them. A feature of the fight was the absence
of wasted effort. It was clean, hard, deliberate hit-
ting in which every blow was carefully aimed. Both
boxers were using their heads as well as their hands.
After the fifth round it became apparent that Hol-
berg’s star was sinking. He fought magnificently.
He did all that a man might do, but he was slowly
getting the worst of it. In the ninth a.left that was
like a cannon-ball dazed Holberg, and before he could
recover a right cross caught him on the cheekbone
and knocked him half-way through the ropes. There
lie clun«', all but out, and was counted to nine when
he same back and tried to clinch. The gong salvaged
him. He returned a badly rattled man, and everyone
expected to see it end there and then. But he made
a marvellous recovery, and hit back steadily, wh le
taking enough annoyance to have felled a bullock.
The crowd went mad over his pluck and endurance,
and right there he became a popular favorite. In the
12th round Mehegan, with a left hook, closed Hol-
berg’s right eye. The Dane continued to clinch, and by
clever ducking managed to evade some withering
smites. But in the 13th Mehegan suddenly smothered
and, boring in, cut loose in a terrific style. In vain
Holberg tried to clinch. He was bashed off. He
bent almost to the floor, and Mehegan bent after and
assaulted and battered him. Again the gong saved
Canute’s countryman. Thence onwards he was merely
trying to last the full time. His power of offence
had gone, but he would see it but somehow. So he
clinched and clinched, and whenever he could he
landed a blow that sometimes made the crowd think
he was going to recover and come again. In the 17th
Nature went on strike. The plucky fellow had fought
himself out, and he suddenly collapsed to his knees,
fell forward on his chin, unable to move, and was
counted out. No sooner had the fatal 10 sounded
than Mehegan lifted him up and placed him on his
chair. It was a splendid defeat. The Dane had fought
himself to a standstill.
This was Mehegan’s first appearance in the ring
since his return to Australia, and he appears to
have improved a lot, good fighter as he was when
he went away. His hitting power seems to >»ve
increased ; and he has got himself under perfect con-
trol. He wastes no blows. Every time he strikes he
hits something, and it is seldom the atmosphere.
Not a wallop he sends along but is a possible knock-
out. His fighting is more versatile than it was, and
he no longer depends on his smother for protection,
but has developed a very pretty fend with his right.
The only contra-account is that he does not strike
the writer as being as fast as he was before he went
to Britain. But that may be a fancy due to having
seen the leaping Frenchmen during his absence.
Mehegan has got himself down to within three
pounds of the weight at which he is to meet Wells,
and he looks capable of getting off the other three
without having to unduly weaken himself.
East Saturday the fastest 25 furlongs
steeplechase that ever was resulted in the victory of
the quickest and safest jumper that ever topped the
23 obstacles of the Flemington Grand National course.
A year ago Guncap clipped 2 jsec. from the previous
record. On Saturday the time for Leah Kleshna’s
journey was 4sec. better than Guncap’s, and 4sec. is
a consideration to a rather small mare weighted with
list. 51b. Her performance was worth going a long
way to see. On the flat, Leah Kleshna is merely a
good, honest goer—something better than a plodder,
but in no way brilliant. The mare seems ,to main-
tain one solid pace from end to end, and if tackled
at the finish by anything that can make a sprinting
effort, she is bound to be beaten. But in this race
of her life she had a lead of half a dozen lengths
when she cleared the 23rd fence, and as her nearest
pursuers were no faster than herself, she won com-
fortably. Of the 16 starters only six came to grief.
Big Bob shifted Fred Burn from the saddle at the
first fence. Guncap fell at the second fence. Two
possible winners were outed in the last mile. United
Kingdom (last year’s runner-up) had taken a strong
lead, and looked unbeatable when he blundered five
furlongs from home; and, just previously, Myall
•lack was going well in the first flight when he fell.
With these out of the way, Leah Kleshna, always in
the front four, had no dangerous opponent left. The
top-weight, speedy Bullawarra, was wearing himself
out at the jumps whilst Leah was flying them, and
he could get no nearer than fourth. For the last
half-mile the G. N. Steeple was a procession, in which
the order of the five leaders never varied. The
winner—a 10 to 1 chance —got a great reception.
Apart from the two chief jiimnlrtg races, the most
notable incident of the Grand National meeting at
Flemington was the revelation of the imported Sea
Prince in the Braybrook Handicap (12 furlongs) on
the Wednesday. Sea Prince, who was apparently not
half wound-up, won in brilliant stylK He has been
let into the Caulfield and Melbourne Cups at 6st.
131 b. and 7st. 51b. respectively, and his owner has
already backed him for both races to win many
thousands. If the imported horse thrives these next
three or four months, he is pretty certain to start a
hot favorite for one or both of the Spring handicaps
in Melbourne. Other dead-birds may come into the
market, but it is hardly conceivable that anything
can look better than Sea Prince as long as he con-
tinues to do satisfactory work. He is a 5-year-old by
Persimmon, the sire of Comedy King.
“Snaffle”: Desmond, whose death is told
by cable, once had an offer of 40,000 guineas made
for him. Of his progeny, Cragunour fetched 1200
guineas as a yearling, Stornaway 5000 guineas (also
as a yearling), and 10 others, sold in 1912, averaged
2484 guineas. His noted descendants include Craga-
nour, Aboyeur, White Knight, Declare, Knockl'eerna,
Land League, Hall Cross, Sir Archibald, Lomond and
Charles O’Malley. Last season his stock won 37
races, value £20,008, of which Craganour, with five
wins, contributed £B7OO. His fee was 250 guineas,
which ranks somewhere near the top.

Dead, at 50 odd, H. H. Young, a promi-
nent and fairly successful owner of horses in S. Aus.
Years ago, his colt Vagary provided the only in-
stance the writer has ever seen of a double fatality in
a flat race. It fell on the far side of the course, at
Morphettville, and neither horse nor rider stirred
again.
* * « *
“S.T.S.” : The late Alfred Lyttelton kept
wicket for England in the great “seven-runs match’’
—the word “test match” was then not yet invented —
of 1882. He was a soynd batsman, yet Maurice Read
told me long after that he considered the game was
lost by Lyttelton and A. P. Lucas, who merely kept
their wickets up for some overs, with the pitch
steadily getting worse, Spofforth and Boyle getting
more difficult all the time, and C. T. Studd, the best
batsman, to go in, being plied with brandy by Hornby
in the pavilion. A little recklessness would have paid
better. Some history was made that day. It was the
first time Australia ever won in England, and the
Sporting Times disgustedly said that English cricket
was dead, and should be cremated and the ashes
taken to Australia —thus originating the famous
phrase. Personally, after travelling all Monday to
see the match, I as nearly as possible missed the finish
on Tuesday evening, being almost persuaded to leave
the ground when England, with seven wickets to fall,
wanted 30, or thereabouts, to win. In 1884, when
Murdoch and c 6. made an immense score, Lyttelton
actually went on to bowl, and succeeded where the
regular bowlers had failed. W. G. Grace took the
gloves, having already fielded in every other position
in the XI during that innings. Meanwhile, one of
London’s clever thieves went off to the Lyttelton man-
sion, applied for the Hon. Alfred’s overcoat and even-
ing clothes, and got away with the whole lot.
* * * *
So far there is no bicycle record between
Sydney and Brisbane, chiefly owing to the awful
roads which prevail on the N.S.W. side of the border.
The distance, by land, is something over 700 miles,
and it ought to be a fine, interesting run. But it
isn’t; it is only a precipice, a bog and a misery.
However, a Queensland rider, F. Riddell, will shortly
make an attempt to achieve a through ride southward
from one city to the other, If he succeeds on such
roads, his name should be indelibly graven on slush,
and a large hole should be dug in the worst bog, and
there a statue of wind should be embedded in his
memory.
* * * *
League Rugby in Sydney on Saturday
was far from inspiring. Only one match, that be-
tween North Sydney and Eastern Suburbs, showed
any semblance of a struggle, and even that had its
depressing aspects. North Sydney had the champion
team beaten, but its intense respect for the top dog
of the competition caused it to throw the match
away. Its respect got it down, got on its nerves and
caused it to miss excellent chances. It was sorrowful.
Eastern Suburbs has apparently gone stale ; even the
leg of Messenger has lost its cunning, and he missed
goals that quite recently would have been dead easy.
But North Sydney was too nervous to take advantage
of its opportunities. At half-time East led by 16 to
13, and at the end of the game it was 24 to 18 in
favor of East. The smashing of Balmain by New-
town was what might have been expected, consider-
ing the way the same team tore through the cham-
pions the previous week. Balmain played clumsily,
a thing which no team can afford to do when
matched with Newtown, and the Tattered Suburb
was lucky to escape with a mere 11-point defeat.
Annandale played excellently against South Sydney
right up to half-time, when it led by five to three ;
but in the second half the team went to pieces in an
amazing manner. The damage was done in almost a
single burst of scoring. Western Suburbs never had
a chance against Glebe. The red-clad crowd was on
top from the lump, and kicked like blazes all the
time. At the interval ft led by 13 to nil, and at full
time by 27 to eight.
In the Competition, Eastern Suburbs is
still in the van with 16 points. But it has no sine-
cure, for Newtown is on its heels with 15, the earn-
ings of seven wins and a draw. South Sydney has
14 and Glebe 12 ; so in this particular riot anything
is possible. Balmain, North Sydney, Western
Suburbs and Annandale are practically out of the
hunt. They are all single figures, and North Sydney,
the best of them, has only six. The Annandale team,
though at the bottom of the list, is deserving of
encouragement. It is the youngest member of the
crowd, and it is an improving member. It has a long
list of disasters, but in every case the team has played
a plucky game, and given an interesting disnlay;
and, judging by its work, is doing its best to bring
itself up to the mark. For one reason and
another the writer expects to see Annandale hold a
much better position in next season’s competition.
On Saturday tlie Yic. League sent one
team to Adelaide and another to Ballarat, and gave
the metropolis a chance to rest itself. The met.,
however, wasn’t taking any rest, and conveyed itself
to the Ass. matches, to the repletion of the Ass.’s
coffers. On the North Melb., ground ’Scray made an
unseemly exhibition of the locals by playing a con-
certed as opposed to an individualists game. At
half-time ’Scray’s tally was 49 points to 14 ; at
three-quarters, 74 to 26 ; at the end, 89 to 46. A
keener, closer game happened out at Brunswick, where
P’ran was up agkinst the Brickies. The latter, play-
ing with n’ce method, got a small lead and kept it
till the middle of the last quarter. Then the shop-
keeping suburb threw its winter fairs, its bargain
counter and the rest of its stock-in-trade into : the
controversy, so to speak, and flattened out its oppo-
nents, the result being P’ran, 73 points ; ’W : ck,’ 57.
That defeat pushes ’Wick down from third place to
fifth. . ’Town, which thrashed Melb. City by 97 to
49, is only half a game behind. ’Town’s committee,
by the way, put in the early part of this week study-
ing how to save its face. When it played ’Cote on
■Tune 28 the umpire reported a player, whose name
he didn’t know, but who wore No. 17 on his rear, for
using the elbow and much indelicate language. At
the inquiry ’Town’s committee produced one Bus :
bridge as the wearer of “17,” but all five umpires
declared he wasn’t the man. The committee said
they must be mistaken, but the investigating trio
coldly remarked that the official record showed No.
17 to be one Adams, and if Adams wasn’t produced
within one week he would be dealt with as though
guilty, whether he was playing or not; also, some-
thing heavy would simultaneously drop on the club
itself. Saturday’s other two games didn’t matter,
though the victorious teams’ barrackers were soothed
when ’Don beat Port by 73 to 41, and Brighton
thrashed ’Cote by 79 to 63.
