Taking advantage of the absence of League
games, Soccer enthusiasts of Yarraside indulged in their
annual riot —England v. the 'lioy
ground, in the presence of several immigrants and
about 3000 or’nery bloques. The episode was pleas-
ing in spots and disappointing in patches, the lack
of effective combination and an undue tendency to
dribble being noticeable, especially on the part of
Scawtlan'. “Tonal - ’ had the weight, a lot of it, but
his opponent had the pace, and, in the long run,
speed won by three goals to one.
The S. M. Herald football scribe was the
first in Sydney to note that, although there are two
matches and the final yet to be played, the N.S.W.
Rugby Union competition is practically ended, and
the premiership lost and won, irrespective of what
may happen. Eastern Suburbs, having won the whole
10 games played this season, has 20 points. New-
town, having lost three and drawn one game, has
only 13, so if it won the three remaining matches
and defeated Eastern Suburbs in the final, it would
have only 19 points. University, which is next on
the list with 11 points, has four matches to play,
owing to the irruption of the Maoriland University
teams; but if it won them all it would also have
only 19. So Eastern Suburbs is too far ahead to be
overtaken, and practically has the premiership in
hand. Only two matches were played on Saturday.
The premier team walloped Newtown by 31 points to
eight, a truly awful event in Union Rugby, and
North Sydney scrunched South Sydney by 11 points
to four. In the first half Newtown managed to hold
its own fairly well, and was three to the champion’s
eight. But in the second half the Eastern team got
properly going and jumped on its fainting opponents.
North Sydney won its game in the first half, and
neither side scored in the second. So the game was
more even than the scores would lead one to suppose.
* * * *
The star match of the Rugby Union’s menu
was, of course, that between the Maoriland Univer-
sity team and the Svdney Metropolitan XV at the
University Oval, where 4000 spectators howled them-
selves hoarse. The unbeaten University team con-
tinued on its victorious course, and won very deci-
sively by 27 points to 10. That is the sort of score
there is no disputing. It has been a most successful
tour, the visiting team winning its three matches
with 62 points to 40. On Saturday the Maoriland
pack outclassed the home forwards, and the backs
excelled in line-kicking and passing charges. The
home tackling was weak, and the marvel is that the
downfall was not worse. The Metropolitan team had
sudden bursts of brilliance, which, however, quickly
faded out. Altogether, the Maorilanders were far
superior, and one cannot help wondering if the N.S.W.
standard of Union Rugby is not a declining one.
Tbe Soccer crowd started tbe second round
of the Rawson Cup competition in and around Syd-
ney on Saturday, and played off four matches.
Annandale smashed up Granville by three goals to
two after a very dashing game, in which anything
was possible right up to time. Newtown had an
easy time with Glebe, but did not make much use
of its obvious superiority, and won out by only two
to one. Sydney made a much better showing with
North Sydney, and in a far more evenly contested
game danced on a more formidable opposition by
four goals to one. Balmain, the champion team,
drifted over Canterbury in a dead calm sort of way,
and served it up with four goals to one.
• * • *
After being held up with adverse weather
for many weeks, N.S.W. Bowling Association took
advantage ( of a fine dav and wiped the much-post-
poned John Young shield off the slate on Saturday.
It is a strange, sudden-death, one-day affa r —the
team which demolishes an opponent by the greatest
margin having tbe right to the furniture for 12
months. The result of the hurricane among the clubs
was a win for City over Gladstone Park by 204 to
65—a difference of 139 points, which created a new
record for the competition. The marginal effort of
other clubs was a long way to rearward of those
who thrive within the quarantined area.
Sydney lacrosse was full of surprises on
Saturday, but the two upheavals that astonished the
natives mostly were those of University and Birch-
grove. The University smashing of Burwood was like
the fall of the Tower of Babel, w'th Burwood under-
neath, the final figures being eight to one. Birch-
grove danced on Sydney by four to one. Eastern
Suburbs got the scare of its life when, at half-time,
Mosman led by two goals to one; but the team from
the home of champions pulled itself together and
soused Mosman in the soup with eight goals to four.
Which was good going. Petersham and Manly met bv
the sea, and Petersham won by nine goals to three.
Play all round was strenuous—too strenuous very
often, with a tendency to use an opponent’s head
instead of the ball. This is a phase which requires
more attention than it is getting. The general result
on the competition was to knock things more askew.
Eastern Suburbs has now a more decided lead than
ever with 19 points to Sydney’s 15, Petersham’s 13
and Birchgrove’s 11. There are others, of course, but
they do not really count.
* * • *
Queensland athlete Charles Swain now
monopolises the mile championship of his State, hav-
ing just won it for the third time. The 880yds. title
also belongs to the legs and feet of the flying Too-
wong harrier; and Swain is probably the most bril-
liant performer Bananaland has yet produced.
Saturday’s round of Sydney baseball was
a joyous time for batsmen, who treated pitchers’
efforts as just plain tripe. Mosman-N. Sydney v.
University supplied the closest tussle of the day, and
likewise the heaviest tally, out of which the players
from over the ferry scrambled with 18 runs to the 17
of Uni. Petersham and Paddington were also in
merry mood, and the brethren of Petersham scored a
victory by 28 to 11. Lane Gove made a chopping-
block of BurwOod by 10 runs to five; while Leich-
hardt recorded the best marginal win of the day
over Waverley by 12 runs to two. Lane Cove and
Mosman, therefore, still remain at the top-rung with
one defeat each, while Leichhardt (last year’s pre-
miers) is close behind with two losses. There is
small possibility of premiership success for any of
the other stragglers in the competition.
» * * *
Is Matt Wells afraid of Mehegan? It
looks like it. They are to fight for the lightweight
championship, and as the fight takes place in Aus-
tralia, Mehegan very reasonably thought that Aus-
tralian conditions would apply, which would make
the weight limit lOst. Wells then produced his
agreement, and it was discovered that Mr. Baker
had contracted to give him three fights at 9st. 91b. ;
and on that specified weight Wells and his manager
were obdurate. Mehegan offered to split the differ-
ence, but the visitors would not concede an ounce.
The trouble was, of course, that to get down to that
weight Mehegan might have to waste, and be
thereby weakened, while Wells is apparently under
the impression that he can make it in the ordinary
course of training. It may be good business, but it
leaves a nasty taste in the mouth, and will not con-
duce to popularity. However, Wells probably con-
siders a win, or anything that will assist him to
accomplish a win, worth stacks of popularity. Which
is a good, worldly way of looking at the matter.
Bill Lang having contracted influenza will
be unable to meet Curran, at Sydney Stadium on the
26th, and it is just possible that the gap will be
filled by Pat Bradley and Jerry Jerome, the aborigi-
nal. It will be an interesting scrap, and ought to
provide some fun. Jerry is beautifully unconven-
tional, and Bradley has a style that is peculiarly his
own, and also peculiarly effective. It is very un-
likely that the black will be able to settle Bradley.
In fact, most people are beginning to think that in
a little while Patrick will have climbed over the pile
of victims to the top of the game in Australia, and
a fight that is much talked of and devoutly wished
for, in spite of the disparity of weights, is one
between Bradley and Dave Smith. It is said that
Bradley is quite willing to tackle Smith, or anyone
else. In fact, there is a suspicion that Patrick would
cheerfully tackle an elephant if the purse were
worth it, or do battle with a whale, or even a
pyramid.
Last week Jack Johnson, accompanied by
his wife, servants, hangers-on, motor-cars and other
varlets, arrived in London, and received a greeting
from the Cockneys that must have shaken him up.
He had difficulty in securing quarters, and, as he
hunted through the streets for somewhere to
stow his outfit, the people made unpleasant remarks
and occasionally applied epithets. In spite of this
irritating experience, he stated, for the benefit of the
public, that, as soon as he had finished his music-hall
engagements, he would return to America to answer
the White Slave charges against him. Chances are
that the music-hall crowds will give him a hostile
reception; and it is more than possible that the
British authorities may exercise the power they have
under their own law to deport him. The fact that
he was convicted in the United States gives the
Government that power; and it will be a brave music-
hall proprietary that will dare to stage before an
English audience a convicted White Slaver who is
a fugitive from iustice. To make money Johnson
should get converted, take a hall under Wowser
patronage, and deliver his interesting lectures, “How
1 Became First a Boxer and then a White Slaver” ;
“What It Was Like to Abduct Belle Schreiber” ; and
“The Awful Secrets of the Game Exposed.” There
might not be millions in it, but there are decent pick-
ings if Johnson can get the right crowd of Wowsers
to take him up.
Last Sabbath morn, walking in
meditation in my garden-paths before my cold-meat
dinner, I observed a concourse of young men coming
over the green hill, and making west by south-west
for the rivulet. They were all well-dressed, not
loudly, but in sober, seemly Sabbath greys and blues
and browns, wearing overcoats and carrying gamps,
as befitted the day. Looking not to the right nor
to the left, the brethren proceeded earnestly up the
hill and down the hill, along the scrubby creek-bank,
across the water, and so up another hillside into the
unknown. A godly-looking, 'gentle-mannered bunch
of Sabbath wayfarers, by the appearance of them.
Yet, alas ! that such things should not be what they
seemed, for it came to my troubled ears, later in
the day, that the gathering was what the sinful call
“a two-up school” shifting camp, owing to a sud-
den hint of police activity in the rural north-east
from which they came. Truly, as of yore, man
regardeth the outward appearance, and looketh not
into the heart, and is deceived grievously. In :fact,
he mucketh up the whole business, and muddleth the
circumstance.

Letter from a man who has just seen a
tram-car decorated with dangling leather
nooses: —
Just at the time when Sydney is clamoring for
vaccination and avoiding all places where fresh air
is scarce and germs are numerous, some inspired
traffic-manager seems to be calmly and callously pre-
paring to overload Johnson’s scarce Cremorne trams
by fixing four hang-on straps in the villainously nar-
row compartments where 10 crushed passengers sit
knee-to-knee and tread on one another’s feet. By way
of balancing the congestion, eight hanging straps are
now provided in the central compartment where there
is sufficient room between the seats to enable the
passenger to sit naturally. I have only seen one car
fitted in this outrageous manner, and it may repre-
sent the last effort of a demented fitter before he was
taken away, kicking, in a cab. On the other hand,
it may be the first of a swarm of similarly-fitted pub-
lic vehicles. If it does represent a carefully-planned
experiment in traffic handling, then Johnson’s tram-
way department has thrown honesty to the winds.
DOUBLE THE PLEASURE OFORIVINO
By
Fitting -
The whole outrage seems to be the result of the Syd-
ney Ferries’ reduced ferry service. The Tramway
Department, apparently, does not see why it should
run trams that merely connect with an empty wharf ;
so the tram “service” has been cut down to match
the so-called ferry “service,” and the same old num-
ber of passengers can either cram themselves into less
trains, or walk. Some of them must necessarily go
without the ventilation and seating space they pay
for ; and in order that they will not enrage the other
passengers by falling on them, the straps have been
provided. The systematic overcrowding of the trams
is unpleasant enough when the city has a clean bill
of health. But in a time of pestiience, nobody—not
even a Government department—-should be allowed to
cram people up like sheep in airtight bins. At the
present time, no sane individual wants to inhale
other people’s breath; but, judging by that well-
strapped tramcar, that is exactly what Johnson’s tram-
way department seems to be preparing to make him
do. The Public Health people should hurry after
Johnson and ask him what he is thinking about, or if
he is thinking about anything.

The Inky Way.
‘‘Wandau” : My par in Bulletin 3/7/’l3 was writ-
ten before the recent conference between the A.J.A.
and proprietors of dailies and weeklies thereto at-
tached. In it I referred to the A.J.A. agreement as
having “expired.” But the conference proved abor-
tive, and, pending the compulsory conference before
Justice Higgins, which was promptly applied for
(per midnight wire, in fact) by the A.J.A., the old
agreement continues. The date and place of the
"ompulsory conference will be fixed by the Judge,
rnd if the result be again nil, Federal Arbitration
Court proceedings will follow.
“Well Inked”: The recently-formed Journalists’
Associations in Australia and Maoriland are gradu-
ally making acquaintance with the Industrial Courts,
and great is the disturbance in the minds of some
of the ancients, who shake their heads sadly and
mourn for the departed dignity of the profession.
Some other mourners are representative journals of the
wealthy party. A few days ago at Christchurch(M.L.)
the Arbitration Court granted, amongst other things,
preference to unionists (as was done at Auckland and
Dunedin), and the official mouthpiece of Riches in
M.L., the Dominion, had the assurance to bray
thus: “It is a sound principle that the community
has a right to obtain its news from an unpolluted
source —that no artificial restriction confining selec-
tion to the members of any one class or organisation
should be placed on the employment of the men who
provide the facts and truths upon which the public
bases its judgment on current events—for, once the
degeneracy of the press is permitted, its reputation
as the guardians of the rights of the people is gone
and its influence for good has reached vanishing
point,” That chunk will do to cheer the M.L. boys
up in the dog days. The Dunedin Star took another
point: “The disclosures made on the witness stand
at Christchurch,” it said, “showed, beyond doubt,
that under the new order of things, the esprit de
corps, the camaraderie and the innate love of the
profession, which were the outstanding features of a
noble calling in days gone by, are slipping away,
and are being replaced, more’s the pity, by what is
very much akin to selfishness.... If pressmen have
any dirty linen to wash, let the process be under-
taken in the editor’s sanctum, and nowhere else.” As
a matter of fact, it has been the long, despairing
failures to obtain a fair thing from editors and
managers in M.L. and Australia that have turned
pressmen to the Court. And the reluctance with
which they turned in that direction needs no adver-
tisement. “The innate love of the profession” isn’t
going to be undermined by an extra 10s. to buy the
child a frock or sufficient spare time in the 24 hours
to see the child out of its sleeping hours. On the
other hand, those things will tend to give the pro-
fession a flavor that it at present lacks. “The innate
love of the profession” gag might be saved with
advantage for the business sheet of the Press Associa-
tion. The pleader for the employers, before the

Court in Christchurch, wept into his brief and asked
if it were “for the public good that the large major-
ity of the people who did not believe in unionism
should be compelled to take their news, truths and
facts from a polluted source.” I do not propose to
answer him. But if ever I do hurl a rock at the
glasshouse in which the M.L. affluent press lives,

“H.C.C.”: “Jack” Blackham, who steps down-
stairs in Melbourne Herald office from leadership of
the reporting staff to captaincy of the advts., first
became connected with the paper in the early ’9o’s,
when it was shifted by certain land boomsters from
Little Collins-street to' the Collins-street building in
which the snuffle-busting Daily Telegraph had
petered out. Blackham was one of the Telegraph
staff who were mixed with a! few Herald identities
in the “reorganisation.” In those days (’Bo’s and
early ’9o’s) some of Melbourne’s pressmen walked
abroad under belltoppers (with coat-tails to match),
and it was frequent subject for discussion whether
Blackham or “Charlie” Norman, of the Argus, wore
the shinier stovepipe. To the keen observer it seemed
that “Jack,” by assiduous polishing, beat the other
man’s larger aggregate of new tiles by a short head.
Ultimately, the Blackhamites, hungry for the ver-
diet, demanded that the jewellery of each competitor
be thrown into the scale. Then their man won easily.
For “Jack” sported the most auriferous vest in all
Melbourne—gold watch, gold chain, gold lockets,
gold medals—all presentation stuff, too. Assayed any
day of the week, he must have yielded a Bullfinch
to the ton. It was a glorious victory. As a vdcalist
Blackham had a soothing quality, and looked with
kindly tolerance upon the Kelynophone, an instru-
ment in great demand at smoke concerts. It con-
sisted of a huge paper funnel, or bell, through which
its inventor, Tom Kelynack (Herald), a born mimic,
spoke, while concealed from view, giving imitations
of local celebrities and notorieties, lawyers, singers,
Governors, etc. As war correspondent for the Daily
Telegraph, “Jack” was quietly impressive in his
black-braided black uniform, with peaked cap a la
tramguard. We were a mixed lot, we war correspon-
dents on the battlefield of Langwarrin Easter En-
campment. From the Argus, Donald Macdonald,
took his life in his hands in a tweed sac suit. So did
Frank Whitcombe, from the Standard, and I, from
the Herald. The late W. J. Lambie, from the Age,
who had survived the Soudan contingent, kept Black-
ham company in uniform. Only Lambie wore the
complete khaki outfit and inhabited, moreover, a
special, private, double-roofed tent of his own,
whereas each of the rest of us, even Blackham,
scribbled and dossed in one of the common or garden
bell-shaped affairs, for all the world like an ordinary
Gippsland unmounted rifleman. “Jack,” by the
way, was too good a soldier to wear those trinkets on
his uniform—he knew the enemy were fair marks-
men. Nor did he ever sing in the firing-line. His
long experience as Railway Department paragraphist
should serve him well now that he is Advertisement
Adjutant-General. Also, his hat and jewellery would
justify an increase in the rate per inch.
THE POET MAKES HIS FORTUNE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I admit that acting for the cinematograph is
rather a dull business. We artists who are so en-
gaged day by day would give much to have the
oppressive silence broken with an occasional hand-
clap. No doubt our work is less spirited for the
lack of some show of appreciation.- —From an inter-
view with an actor.
Dear friends, I mean to write no more
Exquisite rhymes to tickle you.
My sounding lines will never roar
Along the column as of yore.
I'll sell my Pegasus. He’ll do
To feed the lions at the Zoo.
The weary graft of making jokes
And writing pars, of Hughes and Cook
No more my subtle power invokes.
1 do not care which paper croaks,
Or if the shilling buyers look
No more at any kind of book.
The fact is, I’m all right at last;
My fortune’s made. I’ll go my ways
Of pleasantness. I’m holding fast
To bales of lovely quids, so blast
The blooming laurels and the bays!
I've made a great machine that brays
Applause at pros, who all their days
Act stuff for living picture plays.
VICTORIA.
SILAS SNELL.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.
Yet: Your paragraph was cast out on a
charge of being too vast, too learned, too technical,
too brilliant and complicated and elaborate and full
of knowledge and crowded with bottomless research.
Also, its profundity killed t. And it was addi-
tionally slain by its scientific aspects, its wealth of
erudition, its intense medical and surgical and physi-
ological investigation, its boldly speculative charac-
ter, its magnificent disregard of old conventions and
superstitions, and its esoteric ratiocination and phil-
osophic outlook. Besides, it was all about how to
treat a tuberculous cow. The editor of this paper
has sold his cow. The associate editor never had a
cow. The chief sub-editor doesn’t know a cow when
he sees it. The business manager prefers a motor-
car, and the secretary prefers cigars. On top of all,
the narrative you supplied was long and dreary and
hard to read, and big enough to fill a wheelbarrow.
Incidentally, we wouldn’t be surprised if we heard
that you were a vegetarian. You may even be dead
or a Congregational st for all the evidence we have
to the contrary. These remarks are made in a per-
fectly friendly spirit. You can have the loan of
18d. if you require it. The sporting editor will
gladly take you out for a drink in the hotel next
door where the thin barmaid is. But we can’t stand
all that intense and passionate cow-learning. It
makes us feel as if we had eaten two dead languages.
This is our busy day, and space, as you observe, is
scarce. Please go and throw yourself over a pair
of precipices. .. .Tas: Yes, thousands of people have
heard of “parrots pecking lambs to death.” The
Maoriland kea, or mountain parrot, is charged with
the habit. A publication issued by the Government
says of the bird: “On the Alpine slopes, where the
fjirthest-out flock-owners pasture their sheep, the
kea is under a ban, because of its carnivorous tastes.
It has developed a taste for living mutton, boldly
attacking sheep and lambs and digging into their
vitals with its great savage beak.” Friends of the
kea, on the other hand, say that this is a cold-
blooded slander. .. .Subiaco: It bears all the out-
ward marks of veracity, but we are aweary of the
Australian horsemanship controversy. .. .Through the
Fence: It isn’t what we want, but it should be sell-
able elsewhere. Copy returned... .L.0.P.: Divin ng
rod beaten to sawdust long ago. . . .H. E. Wedde: (1)
The last British monarch who appeared in person on
a battlefield was George 11., at Dettingen (Bavaria),
in 1743. (2) There was no law passed to prohibit
British sovereigns going into action. George IV.
always alleged that he fought at Waterloo, but
nobody believed him. British kings have simply got
out of the habit of raging in the front of the battle.
(3) There was no such incident. (4) George 11.
died in his bed. At least there is no record that
he borrowed anybody else’s bed to die in. He was
an economical potentate, and had probably saved up
enough to buy beds....W. Fenton: Very much
obliged for your testimony, though we can’t print
it. .. . Koolywurtie: In the days of our grandfather
the yarn was told of Isaac Newton. .. .Jimmo: You
rhyme all right; but it takes more than rhyming
all right to make reading all right. .. .Haremai:
Hardly suitable for publication, but worth remember-
ing for incidental reference some day....Al: Don’t
think any “defence” of the late Mrs. Annie Bright
is called for; certainly “0.K.” didn’t cast any
reflection on the lady’s “charitable and loving
nature” —-just about the most certain and conspicu-
ous thing about the lady. .. .C.E.M.: Won’t swear
to it; but 325. per 1000 has been quoted....A.
Humphrey: Don’t care for parables, and “Islandia”
is a poor one.... Grit: Copy of Grit (26/6/’l3)
received, with blue-pencilled statement to the effect
that “Liquor is to blame for one out of every
adult male deaths.” Well, wouldn’t these people
have died anyhow? In other words, would they
have lived for ever but for liquor ? It seems a rash
theory. Nobody has lived for ever as yet. Every-
body who dies must die of something. And the
person who dies of something at the age of (say)
54 might, if he had avoided that something, have
died of something else at the age of 45. That is
to say, the whisky that killed him at last might
have averted some serious infection previously.
Doubtless many a man has caught the germ that
killed him through not being in a clean, impervious
cell “sleeping it 0ff.”... .James K.: So far as we
know he is dead, but otherwise quite well. At least
deadness is the only bad symptom we have heard of
in his case. Better get some one in Adelaide to
look him up, or to get a photograph of his tomb-
stone for you. . . .M.D.(Q.) : “Shadow too strained. ...
M.HC.(M.L-): Story unsuitable for B. Always glad to
consider pars... .Lela M.: A feeble poem. .. .Dorothy
M. H.: Verses too young, but the beautifullv-written
manuscript was a pleasure to read... .P. Bade: Hope-
less verse... .Pathfinder: Your friends must be easily
amused... .P.P.Q.: Returning sketch. Try and boil
it down to half present length. . . .W.T.C.: Try it on
another paper... .E.E.C.: Yarn too wild, though the
detail is well done. Verses promising, but hardly
worth print.

F. C. Burgis: The best one is (alas!) an old
Scotch jest. The Bailie printed it in this writer’s
infancy. And the Bailie wasn’t much of a paper
either. .. .L.M.P.: So “a friend” heard that joke,
did he? Well, your friend heard somebody reading

badly and spoiled the joke.... H S'.: Picture dealing

that we can’t stand more than 55 pictures dealing
with Joe Cook in any one week. Yours happened
to be the 56th Joe Cook picture. (N.B. —We are
thinking of having one issue wholly devoid of Cook,
if you don’t m nd. N.B. some more —You can’t
draw Cook.) .... Dan: You have a bigger front than

off that jest as new... .Buck D.: No earthly g00d...

name of Heaven, do all you artists send in pictures
of J. Cook ? He has met Parliament with a
majority of one, which was in the chair, and he has
done nothing yet save adjourn. Anybody can ad-
journ. The Bible adjourned for about 480 years,
which caused a great historical gap between Arta-
xerxes of the Old Testament and Herod of the New
Testament. .. .W.J.N.: There is no great demand
here for jokes about carbuncles, boils and the like.. .
C.F.P.: Have made 10 different attempts to make it
a habitable joke. Unfortunately, the joke is auite
roofless and unfurnished, and we have given it up...

Ernest D.: Small masterpiece, representing meat with
flies on it, rejected... .E.A.: The art isn’t much,
and some of the gigs are bad, and some of them
are dashed bad, but the worst of them is the
“celery-salary” foke. How did that verbal ghost
happen to again revisit the shadows of the moon ?...
Redluom: That vaccination joke was told of Mrs.
Langtry in her youth. It went round from paper
to paper, as a crisp little yarn about how the doctor
told the lady she would have to swallow the mark
if she didn’t want the public to see it. Then an
elaborate American spread the three-line joke into
half-a-column, and killed it. . . .F.W.B. (Asylum Re-
serve) : Drawing about the size of a postage stamp
and impossibly bad. Also, when you write the
dialogue all over the picture you spoil the picture.
Incidentally, the idea appeared in The Bulletin
years ago. .. . J.A.S.: Outer darkness swallowed your
suffragette picture. Incidentally, outer darkness has
had a pain in the stomach ever since. .. .C.C.: Two
men, a fallen tree, an axe, a dog, a fence, a horse,
two humpies, a dry landscape, and no explanation
nor name nor address. Why didn’t you add a dead
tree and a crow ?.... Invicta: You’re more honest
than a few others, anyhow—you at least, when send-

We have been making saddles for close on half a century—and
all we know about the game has gone into the making of this
famous Stock Saddle of ours. It is hand-made throughout—no
cheap and nasty machine work anywhere. It is built on the
staunchest tree of specially tempered steel, fitted with an
unbreakable gulletplate, to which are riveted (not screwed, mind
you) solid steel hind crupper plates. This makes the tree
absolutely unbreakable, yet gives just the elasticity and “give”
needed. The seat is made of specially tanned bag leather, with
very long, thick harness leather flaps, that do away with the
need of wearing leggings. There are leather points to the panel, that will
not wear out for the whole life of the saddle. The knee pads are 54 inches
high, and made of solid leather, double sewn inside so that they won’t sag
or give over. The thigh pads are made in the same way and lj inches
high. We have the seats with as high a dish as 6 inches, but anv lower
dish you want we can give you. The mountings are brass and nickel, and
the Dees are fastened so securely that no tug will strain them. The panel
is serge lined, and with extended facings so as not to frav or wear out by
the rubbing of the swag or tucker gear. The saddle is sold fitted with
told or split leather girths, 4 bar irons, stirrup leathers, ring or ordinary
crupper and surcingle. -. • ■

For Everything that Is Good and New for the We
ar and Use of Man.
IN
BANANA LAND
The prickly pear commission, which is
meandering through India, hasn’t yet found
anything of much interest. The one item that
is worth further investigation is that, in
certain parts of the land of Chunder Loo,
the curse is used as a fertiliser. Apart from
that, the commission seems to be discovering
the things it was well acquainted with yes-
terday.
It seems likely that the Queensland
Government will have to mourn over the
£5O per bag it paid for the imported coch-
ineal bugs. So far the little beasts refuse
absolutely to eat the Dulacca pear, and have
had to be kept alive on special cactus sent
from Emerald, in the middle innards of the
country. To make matters worse, every
bird, it seems, and most large beetles, insist
upon including cochineal bugs in their diet,
and the betting at present is on the birds
and the beetles. The cochineal bug isn’t a
fighter, and when the local beetle comes
along and tells him to put up his props he
simply wilts and calls for the police.
Go to the bush, and the settlements that
are being bitten out of the bush, to find the
ready, helping hand. The Maranoa Advo-
cate tells how a Yingerbag settler recently
died, leaving a wife and several children in
poor circumstances. The neighbors brought
along their machines, with 32 horses, and
grafted till they had put the clearing under
wheat. And then Providence lent its hand,
and sent the rain that was wanted to give
the widow’s crop a start. Providence is
right on to its job.
The other day a deputation from the
Lockyer shires waited on Treasurer Barnes.
It politely asked for a loan for 40 years to
build a road. Whereupon Barnes was moved
to talk sense:—
Ever since he had been Treasurer he had dis-
couraged loans over long periods. If they had a
loan of £ 500 over 40 years it would probably cost
£llOO. That was not good policy. He would be
very pleased, provided their position was a good
one, to find the money for the usual period, which
he believed was 10 years. The life of the road could
not possibly be 40 years.
Which is quite an admirable sentiment. But
when the State Government borrows money
for permanently unproductive works it
makes no provision for repayment in 40, or
even 4000 years, or any other old time this
side of eternity. In fact, it is in such mortal
teryor of reducing a loan by a few pounds
that, since surpluses have to be employed in
paying off debt, it almost habitually fakes
its accounts in good years so as to avoid
showing any surplus. J
Under-Secretary Ryder, of the Home De-
partment, is to retire this year, as he has
just passed his 70th milestone. Ryder was
born on Prince Edward’s Island, a cold,
bleak region in North America. He came
to Queensland as a very young boy, and
dashed into journalism, but shortly aban-
doned the Inky Way for the Civil Service.
He has always been known as an able and
courteous officer, with no prejudices and no
partialities. The Denham crowd is allowing
him to buy up his lapsed pension rights by
paying down a sum in hard cash —a prac-
tice admirable or otherwise just as you look
at it from the standpoint ’of the retiring
official or of the general taxpayer. As the
latter doesn’t grumble, nobody worries about
him.
Bananaland Assembly discussed higher
wages for members recently; £5OO a year
was mentioned. The chief feature of the
debate was the “friendly” hostility which
some of the Denhamites assumed. The
speeches were remarkably short, in the hope
that the vote might be reached before other
business was called on. Digby Denham
tried to block this by proposing a referen-
dum. A vote, overwhelmingly against this,
was, however, squeezed through just on the
stroke of six o’clock. But all was not yet
lost. As the Speaker proceeded to put the
original motion, a Ministerialist got the
floor. So the motion is still on the business
paper, and members will have to wait a few
more weeks for the extra money.
THE BRAND HE WANTED.
PARSON (on the subject of strong drink): “‘It biteth like a serpent and siingeth like an
adder.’ ”
THE OTHER ONE (deeply interested): “No! Tell us where yer got it, mate. I haven't
had a drink with a nip in it for a week.”
“Basalt Bill”: Somebody handed to Too-
woomba Town Council recently tne "lavish
generosity” championship following upon its
gift of a month’s holiday on full pay, after
32 years’ service, to a retiring employee.
But what about the Einasleigh (N.Q.) dis-
trict hospital committee? Recently the in-
stitution was minus a matron and plus a
typhoid outbreak. Thereupon the medico’s
wife rolled up her sleeves to the shoulder,
and starred in the “ministering angel” role
for eight days of from 14' to 20 hours each.
At the same time the wardsman (a lad of
63 summers) toiled 16 hours per diem, and
moreover the young scoundrel kept going for
over a month, wages being 255. per week.
The committee thought carefully over the
matter, and finally awarded the heroine and
hero a whole vote of thanks. The reward
would probably have been increased, but the
hospital has a surplus of only. £lBOO and
sorr“ odd pence.
“Norbell” on some casual habits:—
They’ve had another big fire in Charleville. A
whole block, including the leading hotel, the news-
paper office and several shops, has ended in smoke,
and sundry people barely escaped incineration. The
hotel was a favorite watering place for squatters,
commercial travellers, tick inspectors and other
potentates, and was a tinder building which simply
invited a bonfire. One reads that the fire brigade
was helpless. It’s a wonder there was a fire brigade.
They take a lot of chances with the wooden walls
of 5 -'•Western Queensland. At Goondiwindi, where
about half the town was burned a few years ago, and
where a fine river—the Macintyre—-gives a perma--
nent water supply in the very back yards of the!
main street, they have no fire brigade," and not so-
much as a bucket ready for the inevitable blaze.
In Dalby and Roma, important, flourishing and
largely wooden towns, both with a good pressure!
of artesian water laid on, they are casual to the
same degree. Given nice dry weather and a good
wind, there’ll be a hot time in the devil-may-care
West one of these nights.
Ballade of Ghouls.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
While some do crave a daily crust,
And others pine for rich array;
While some in saintly helpers trust
To aid them win their earthly way;
Not ours the wish for high display,
For regal board and sumptuous bed
One simple boon we nightly pray—
Give us this day our Daily Dead!
Alike on evil man and just
The Scythe it falldth ev’ry day,
And soon the Worm within your dust
Shall hold an undisputed sway;
But ev’ry clod is teeming clay
Where myriad lives are born and bred,
So start not when ye hear us say —
“Give us this day our Daily Dead !”
For years that tame the Heart of Lust,
For creaking back and hair of grey,
For Age that rots in senile rust
We find a calm and sheltered bay ;
To none who knock we answer Nay,
And when the Greedy Pit Is fed
We beg the Reaper none may stay—
“ Give us this day our Daily Dead !”
Envoi.
Not ours to lift the gladsome lay
When babes are born or maids are wed ;
We chaunt the dirge of all decay—
“ Give us this day our Daily Dead!”
QUEENSLAND.
F. B.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, July 12. 1913.
My Dear Thargominoah,—
The Wattle Day League is making enthu-
siastic arrangements to hold a celebration
here, sometime after the best of the
blossoms are done and the trees near town
have been torn down by the picnicking pest.
In order to spur the lagging interest of the
State school kid, a public meeting is to be
held in the School of Arts Hall on July 21.
Ministers Tolmie and Blair and Botanical
Gardens director Bailey will make compli-
mentary remarks about the national vege-
table, and several well-known amateurs will
uplift the voice of song in Australian ditties.
The other day a mild sensation was
created in the Government offices. A depu-
tation of the unemployed arrived, fiercely
demanding a little conversation with Premier
Digby Denham or one of his satellites.
Digby not being forthcoming, Bishop Barnes
dashed into the breach, and, after a slight
wrangle with one of the deputation as to the
right of a free citizen to wear his cady in
a Minister’s room, promised to do something.
Said something took the shape of work,
which was found for 13 of the clamorous
ones. Whether the ill-omened number put
them off, I know not; but only eight turned
up. Next day more wild yells for toil
shocked the ears of our politicians while the
House was in session. A vague hint that
tbe f° r sucb bad-mannered proceedings
was £ooo or thereabouts calmed the tumult,
and Premier Denham poured some more but-
ter on the trouble by half promising to dis-
cover or concoct work for the workless, or
those of the workless that want work.
This week has been conspicuous by the
return of several trippers, suffering from
sore arms, and a grievance against your
namiet. It was your village or clachan, you
th , at be &an all the trouble. Mrs. B
W. Macdonald and daughter Vera arrived
on Thursday. The trip from Vancouver
was made in the Niagara, and was continued
from Sydney aboard the Wyandra. Both
travellers had vicious scratches on their
arm«. Amy Page, who went with them,
““ t 0„ fix up additional frills and

Mrs. E. B. Wareham and her pretty
d° lighter reached home by the Canberra last
Monday, after a four months’ holiday in
Singapore, Java, and the North-west coast.
Both have vivacious accounts to give of the
“good time” they had, and the brasses and
embroideries included in their baggage are
causing the stay-at-homes to treat the tenth
commandment with contumely. Daughter
Elsie wore a frock, w r hich was the work of a
discolored garment maker. Actually, it cost
less than half what it would have done here.
Cheapness is mighty and will prevail. The
two ladies went straight from the Canberra
to the Wandilla, where Wareham pere, with
a small but select few, were waiting to cele-
brate the occasion.
The Very Private Assemblies gavie another
of their cavorts on Thursday, and thereto
Lady Macgregor and the Y’ision, with the
Foxton aide as an after guard, blew in about
10 o’clock. In similar formation they blew
out about 11. The hall decorations were
of black silk and trails of bignonia venusta,
and the supper was all that could be ex-
pected for the money. If anybody was dis-
satisfied it was kept a profound secret.
Mr. and Mrs. H. M. Hicks, back from a
holiday jaunt in pleasant places, gave a
little luncheon party at Finney’s Cafe on
Monday last.
Miss Deighton, a quiet, kindly woman
whose charities were always done* with the
least possible amount of publicity, passed
over list Sunday. She had been ailing for
some time. and was resting at Warwick
when the light of this world went out.
The Liberal Young Women met on Tues-
day evening, and discussed “Organisation
from a Woman’s Point of View.” Mrs.
Wilkinson’s was the chief p. of v., and
Mrs. Halkier added a few remarks, which
left the aged subject much where it
was before or thereabouts. Thereabouts is
probably nearer the longitude.
The Wimmin’s Temperance Union is again
on the warpath. This time its good works
have been entrusted to Miss George, who is
a general organiser and plenty big chief in
the tribe. . Mrs. W. H. Carvosso will house
the blue ribboness while she is in Brisbane.
The Women’s Residential College is
slowly becoming a possibility. The Old-
Girls’ Associations of the various schools
have decided to give it a friendly push, and
a proud secretary announced that £43 odd!
had already been precipitated into the ex-
chequer.
R. J. Spence, of Dillalah, has announced!
his matrimonial attentions towards Mary
Petersen, who is how a Brisbane girl, but
who used to live up Longreach way.
With kind regards to Jenner,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
. Some men get rewards for strange ser-
vices. Thus Brisbane Methodist Conference
the other day presented a Fijian missionary,
-tvatu limothy Suavoli, with a large um-
brella in token of admiration of a man who
told Conference that he could easily talk till
morning on the subject under debate —and
didn’t.
A notable citizen of Moreton Bay, Mary
Ann, a black sister of Stradbroke, died re-
cently at the age, it is guessed, of 76. The
guess was probably wrong; nevertheless
Mary may have been the oldest living native
of the district, and she certainly could nar-
rate stories of the happenings of nearly 70
years ago. Local Rechabites claim her as a
hne example of the virtues of total absti-
nence. Mary herself attributed her length of
days merely to ’bacca.
Tobacco-expert Nevill has ceased to be-
long to the sleepy Department of Pumpkins
and Butter in Bananaland. He is to re-
patriate himself soon, settling somewhere in
Georgia, close to the scenes of his child-
hood. It will be a rather pathetic return,
as the old man is bent now on a friendly
Tiru- -NeviU has been a good friend to a:
y hRe Australia, and never failed to show
that the. Chow was no good to the industry
that it was Ah Sin, indeed, who was
mainly responsible for that careless drying
of the leaf which is the chief curse of the
business in Australia.
Treasurer Barnes, on issuing his annual
financial statement, was pleased to take credit
rnu- • e .ff° r t s “to conserve the loan fund."
JLhis is just the one thing Barnes and his
Government have neglected to do, unless the
piling up of indebtedness by two-and-a-half
and three millions per annum can be called
conserving.” Ividston, before he became a
hoomster did conserve the loan fund, paying
±0J0,125 of surplus thereinto in one year;
and Airey, when Treasurer, also supple-
le same account by paying
±115,601 to loan fund in 1908. But since
that year the accounts have been manipu-
lated to obviate _ all but infinitesimal sur-
pluses—a proceeding condemned even by the
Liberal press of Brisbane, but yet persisted
m by a crowd whose idea about the one un-
pardonable sin seems to be that it consists
in paying off a debt.

W. G. Higgs, member for Capricornia
(Q.), jarred Prime Minister Cook in the
Representatives during the joyous proceed-
ings of Joseph’s first squattage of the Prime
Minister’s seat. He asked the head of the
Government if he was prepared to repu-
diate a report in Hansard of his remarks
in New South Wales while he was a member
there. The momentous remarks were: —
I should like to say one or two words about the
question of Federation under the Crown. I do not
know that I am* a red-hot Republican—at any rate,
not one of those Republicans who say they \ will do
nothing until they get a Republic. 1 believe the
destiny of these colonies will be that of a Republic,
and whenever it comes 1 shall certainly hail it, if it
does come in a way which will meet the aspirations
of the great bulk of the people.
But Joseph looked coldly on Higgs and
clamored in silence. In fact, his only
answer was a hostile expression.
S. A. White, of Adelaide, who has the
very unusual combination of a taste for
natural history with private means to back
it up, is going to run an expedition of his
own to the Macdonnell Ranges next month.
The Horn expedition, he says, went through
there in cold and dry weather and in a
hurry. The Commonwealth lot, under Bar-
clay, saw it in drought. Captain White pro-
poses to get there for nesting time, to study
the bird-life, in which he specialises.
Gregory Mathews, of London, is preparing
the absolute up-to-date limit of a book on
Australian Birds, and rather than have a
professional “collector” turned loose, White
has taken up the task of helping Mathews
for the pure love of it. He and his wife
(with an adequate transport equipment)
make up the sporting little expedition—
which is not their first, by a long way. but
is to be their biggest.
The new Australian House of Reps, differs
from its predecessors in that Gentleman One
isn’t the most numerously represented trades-
man. There are but nine of him, and just as
many farmers ; in fact, there are a dozen of
the latter if you include the two orchardists,
and Page, who calls himself a selector.
After these come the journalists and alleged
journalists, who are eight; there are five
miners, four graziers, three agents and as
many storekeepers, two gentlemen, two mer-
chants, two secretaries, two organisers and
two laborers (Archibald, of South Aus.,
and the O’Malley, no less), and one
each of these —traveller, medico, tailor,
landowner, hatter, plumber, pattern-
maker, vigneron, quarryman, artist, jew-
eller, coachbuilder, plasterer, auctioneer,
butcher, engine-driver, watchmaker, man-
ager, tobacconist, and person of independent
means. That accounts for 74. The other
one deserves special mention for stating the
exact truth about his occupation on the day
of nomination. He was then an individual
of no occupation —just Frank Anstey.
Government Notices.
Department of Defence,
Melbourne, 30th June, 1913.
Vacancies (2) for Clerks, Royal Military
College of Australia, Duntroon,
Federal Territory.
APPLICATIONS are invited from per-
sons qualified for appointment as clerk
at the Royal Military College of Australia
(2 vacancies).
Proposed rate of pay for each position,
£195 per annum with single quarters (or
married quarters when available) provided
free of charge.
Further particulars and forms of applica-
tion may be obtained from the Secretary, De-
partment of Defence, Melbourne; the Com-
mandant, Royal Military College of Aus-
tralia, Duntroon, Federal Territory ; or the
Commandant, Military District Headquar-
ters, Sydney, Brisbane, Adelaide, Perth and
Hobart.
Applications endorsed “Anplication for ap-
pointment as clerk, Royal Military Col-
lege,” must be forwarded, addressed to the
Commandant, Royal Military College. Dun-
troon, Federal Territory, New r South Wales,
to reach him not later than 31st July, 1913.
S. A. PETIIEBRIDGE,
Secretary.
Department of Defence,
Melbourne, June 30. 1913.
Tenders for Sand-Blast Plant required
at the Commonwealth Naval Dock-
yard, Cockatoo Island, Sydney.
TENDERS are required for the supply of
Sand-Blast Plant complete, delivered
ready for erection at Cockatoo Island, New
South Wales.
Full particulars may be obtained upon ap-
olication to the General Manager, Common-
wealth Naval Dockyard, Cockatoo Island,
Sydney.
Tenders to be received addressed to the
Director of Navy Contracts, Nayv Office,
Melbourne, or to the Director of Navy Con-
tracts, care General Manager, Commonwealth
Naval Dockyard, Cockatoo Island, Sydney,
and marked “Tender for Sand-Blast Plant,”
not later than 16th July, 1913.
The lowest or any tender will not neces-
sarily be accepted.
E. D. MILLEN,
Minister of State for Defence.

ARE YOU
DEAF?
If you are deaf, or going deaf, you will be glad to
hear of a simple and scientific device which restores
hearing, no matter how old you are. No mysterv or
secrecy about it; we describe it fullv, and explain
just how it acts. You use spectacles as sight magni-
fiers, and in the same wav you use this sound magni-
fier to hear better. Unlike spectacles, the Wilson
Common-sense Ear-Drums are invisible and comfort-
able. No one can tell you are wearing them Thev
rest the ear nerves by taking the strain off them—the
strain of trying to hear indistinct sounds. What a
relief to have done with that tiring strain. Being
made of the softest Para rubber they cannot be felt”,
even when the head rests on a pillow. For the same
reason, a blow on the side of the head will not hurt
the ear. They protect the tender inner parts of the
ear, that are so sensitive, from cold winds, dust, sud-
den and piercing sounds.
So that you may clearly understand how the Sound
Magnifiers.restore hearing, we give an illustration of
one as it lies in the ear. You will notice that the
Sound Magnifier is shaped like a trumpet, and so con-
veys even the faintest sounds right to the natural drum-.
Being of the softest rubber, it cannot hurt the car-.
It fits the orifice of the ear
with precision and comfort.
The division that runs
across the mouth is to al-
low the removal and inser-
tion of the Ear-Drum, and
to brace it.
The shape of the Wilson
Ear-Drum has been chosen
after years of experience as
the best adapt-
ed to carry the

THE
WORKS
OF
De MAUPASSANT.
ELBERT HUBBARD writes:—
“Men of Genius, who have left
the beaten track to discover a
new country, have ever called
down carping criticism. A prac-
tice centuries old, this, When one
cannot understand nor appreciate;
’tis so satisfying and virtuous to
stand aside and sneer. Or bet*
ter, take the Presumptuous One
and nail him to a gibbet. For
aeons men have fouled their own
tongues by slander; they have
dipped their own hands in the
heart’s blood of martyrs; they
have besmirched their own mem-
ory and polluted their times; yet,
all their destructive madness has
plucked not a single leaf from
the laurel crown of Genius. YOu
may kill a great and good man.
Oh, yes ! —you may reproach and
revile him; you may call down
imprecations, but when you are
dead and forgotten, his soul, his
*- memory will go marching on and
on. De Maupassant, of all
writers, has suffered most from
misunderstanding. He has been
crucified on the brazen cross of
ignorance. This man never wrote
to please the Rabble. He wrote
because he had a message for the
world, and because Divine Energy
forced it through his pen. De
Maupassant wrote with the con-
viction that in life there could be
no phase so noble or so mean, so
honorable or so contemptible, so
lofty or so low as to be unworthy
of chronicling—no groove of
human virtue or fault, success or
failure, wisdom or folly that did
not possess its own peculiar
psychological aspect and there-
fore demanded analysis. If oc-
casionally some intellectual pig-
my, after reading, screams in fal-
setto, please remember spon-
taneity and mediocrity are never
kin.
“A WARNING.—Those of you
who find enjoyment in blue-milk
brain-wanderings should never
seek De Maupassant. You will
be disappointed. You will not be
able to digest nor assimilate the
strong elixir of his mind, so it
will curdle in your gullet—then
it's you for mental constipation.”

Stadium treated Referee Sc tjii dfsjfMbefuliy on Satur-
day night wegk, on the ground that he had given a
wrong decision in the Thofn-lhpmas case. Well, he gave
a perfectly fair and proper decision, and I will now
give reasons for saying so. Frank Thorn side-stepped
a lot, which was legitimate,, Also, he back-stepped
more, and frequently he absolutely ran from the too-
pressing attentions of Englishman Thomas. lor a
great part of tjje rest , of the .time, he hung on to
Thomas ;as though Thomas were,a lifebelt, and as if
they were, struggling ~in the water .after Sydney s
phenomenal iferry-smash. , AIJ the scribes .admit that
Thomas scored best in the body fighting; so there
remains only the faee-stoush to consider. I counted
every blow to the dial scored by each man, dotting
the wallops down on paper as they were delivered,
and adding the totals round by round. I had no
money on either boxer. I was totally disinterested,
as I know neither of them from a crow, and wouldn t
be able to recognise them with their clothes on. And
what I found was this: Thomas scored 164 lefts and
44 rights on Thorn’s physiog., a total of 208. Thorn s
response was 166 lefts and 20 rights, total 185.
So, on a matter of ooints, where are the doubting
TnorhfiseS ? Whfefe is the Frank Thorn phalanx
gone ? „ 4

to be allowed powers in regard to bdxirtg similar to
those held by the English Jbcßey Club Officials 6Vet

Arid there is no appeal from their decisions. They
may summarily wipe out an owner, a trainer or a
jockey, or all three, for the best of reasons or for
pone. In many notorious cases they have done
things, which , a court of law, or any ordinary man of

much the man-about-town, and too little the humble,
forelock-dragging stable-boy. So he was blotted out,
as a lesson to others, or “for the good of racing,” as
the Jockey Club might put it. Powers which are
highly liable to lead to acts of tyranny and injustice
when wielded by the reputable citizens who control
the English Jockey Club would be absolutely certain
to be abused, by the far from impeccable crowd that
runs the National Sporting Club. Go there on one
of the “fight nights,” and you will be prone to think
it is a resort of the highest and most respected in
the land. But 90 p.c. of these men only use it as
a club in the sense that they were accustomed to use

There’s a crumb: pf satisfaction in the
news that the Australian, Government has selected
300 acres of land as a site for an aviation ground
and training school. It is situated at Altona Bay
(Victoria!, and it is hoped that within four months
the Aviation Corps will be properly established and
going strong. Senator Millen is taking a keen in-
terest in the business, and made one very sensible
suggestion in connection with the opening of the
ground. He proposes that the first experimental
ascent should be made by Prime,. Minister Cook in
person; and this writer hastily rises to second the
motion and applaud the idea. Joe, seen about it,
said, “Not if I know it” ; but there are four months
in which to persuade him. He never dreamed he
would see himself on horseback, but when Kitchener
came along Joe did get on top of a noble steed a
few times. What more noble sight than the real
ruler of the Commonwealth winging his way towards
the empyrean and sailing triumphantly betwixt
fleecy cloud and empty blue! #
English cricket has opened humorously
this season. An idea was afloat to put down into a
second class all counties which had not the time and
money to play home-and-home matches with every
other. Wealthy Lancashire was the mover. Little
Northampton Was the most obvious victim. There-
fore, when Northampton met Lancs, and won by
three Wickets, there Was an all-round smggef. Mean-
while, Derbyshire, Which really is weak, and has for
some years been liable to get thrown out of the first
class at any moment, had won its first three games,
and was at latest advices poised dizzily on the very
summit of the premiership list.
The Itala Co., which intended making a
big hid for the forthcoming French Grand Prix
motor race with a splendid new racer built specially
for the job, has suffered a severe set-back. The
driver, Arbizzon, and the chief engineer of the co.,
Bigio, have both been killed, and the car has been
smashed. As the racing cars were not allowed to
go over the circuit till the actual contest, the two
men went round it on touring cars, got the hang
of the landscape or the rising of the court, or some-
thing, and then rushed off to Brooklands, in Eng-
land, to see what their flier was really capable of.
They returned by way of Dieppe, and on the beauti-
ful roads around that town started out to do road
practice. To avoid trouble with the traffic, they
went out in the early morning. One morning they
left at 3 a.m., and some time afterwards a laborer
on his way .to work found the car overturned, Arbiz-
zon underneath it dead, and Bigio beside it dying.
How, why and exactly when are unknown, as Bigio
died before he could give much
The Royal Automobile Club of England
has offered a prize of £lOOO for the best internal-
combustion turbine engine that can be produced.
The curious thing is that although the small in-
ternal combustion turbine is regarded as impossible,
the big one is not, and there is at least one in
active service which develops 1000 h.p. The trouble
seems to be to make the little fellow go slow enough.
All investigation goes to show that as the size de-
creases, the speed increases. And just fancy instal-
ling in a motor chassis an engine with a range of
from 10,000 to 20,000 revolutions per minute. What
a time the driver would have!

“0.R.E.H.”: M. A. Noble’s prediction at
the annual meeting of the N. S. Wales Baseball
Association, that “the game will be played in the
summer months in Australia before long,” looks
likely. At present there are only two really good,
hustling, meaty sports of an exciting kind—Rugby
football and boxing. Cut out boxing, as it does not
affect any game played in the afternoon in Sydney,
and that leaves you only Rugby. Football is played
in winter. So summer has no sport with a sting in
it. Cricket? Cricket survives merely because it
happens to be long-established, and it draws big
crowds only when played by international teams. On
such occasions the game, never anything to rave
about, is at its worst, because the players are all
out to win, and adopt safety tactics as much as pos-
sible. Club matches, though very much brighter,
attract very few lookers-on, whereas club Rugby
football is a powerful magnet every Saturday. There-
fore, I say, any better, faster summer game than
cricket will kili that ceremonious old pastime, just
as any swifter winter frolic would slay Rugby foot-
ball. But, whereas there is nothing snappier than
Rugby, or at least the League game so far as N.S.W.
is concerned, there is something infinitely more at-
tractive than cricket —and that is baseball. Yet this
Yankee, live-wire game does not require much, if
any, more exertion on the part of the players. As
it happens, baseball is played in the winter at pre-
sent, When all the best grounds, barring University
Oval, are collared by the powerful Rugby Leagug.
If it were shifted to the summer, I believe many
first-class cricketers would prefer it to the old game
' in’ which a tired man throws something at three
sticks. Indeed. I can already see the ancient monarch
reclining on his pink ear, with his crown hashed.

A Ballad of Freedom.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
i%.TO\V Mr. Jeremiah Bane
\ lie owned a warehouse in The Lane,
|_l An edifice of goodly size,
Where, with keen private enterprise,
He sold imported napery
And drapery—and drapery.
Hi . singlets and his socks were sent
n. t over half the continent;
In clothing for the nursery
\nd mercery—and mercery
plied a most extensive trade,
Vnrt auite enormous profits made,
barracked, with much fervency,
Pnr foreign-trade—described as ‘Free.”
1 He said.
Indeed,
It was
His creed,
Xbe trade described as Free.
. j this good man was known to fame
For charity; indeed, his name
Shone often in the daily press.
Whpn needy folk were in distress
He aided—(with publicity)
Mendicity—mendicity.
\nd though much cash he thuswise spared
There still were people who declared
His act of private charity
A rarity—a rarity.
Donations, duly advertised, .
From business point of view he prized ;
But “good by stealth” he ne’er could see
Was any use to such as he.
But still,
The press,
With much
Success,
Declared his hand was free.
Now Mr. Bane’s employees were
W o nt to address the boss as “Sir,”
To show him most intense respect;
\nd there were few who would neglect
To couple with civility
Humility—humility.
They dressed in cheap but pretty clothes,
Vnd ev’ry man turned up his nose
And - scorned familiarity
Or parity—or parity
With ill-dressed toilers who “combined.”
They thought proceedings of that kind
Were of a very “low” degree,
For they were “cultured,” don’t you see.
’Tis true
Their pay
Was mean,
But they
Felt proud to be so free.
•
Though they were vilely underpaid
They were too proud—or else afraid
To advertise the fact abroad
Or seek to get a Wages Board.
Beside their meek servility,
Gentility—gentility
Forbade so rash an act; but still
One man there was —(his name was Bill)
Who vowed their fool propensity
Was density—was densitv —
An unenlightened state of mind,
A lack of wit that made them blind.
“You’re but a lot of worms,” said he.
“If you were men you’d clearly see
Until
You band
And make
A stand
You never can be free.”
And ev’ry day this person, Bill,
Conversed with them of unions till
They owned his arguments were true,
And one by one w r axed eager to
Embrace an opportunity
For unity—for unity.
They talked about a Wages Board
Which, formerly, they had abhorred.
And girded at their slavery
With bravery—with bravery.
Each man began to feel “The Firm”
No longer owned him for its worm;
Their independence they could see
Achieved by simple unity ;
Forgot
Their clothes
And mixed
With those
Who battle to be free.
When Mr. Bane one morning heard
About this thing he cried, “Absurd!
They’ll never get my clerks to horde
With those who seek the Wages Board,
And lose respectability!
Futility!—Futility !
ny clerks are gentlemen who’d scorn
To mingle with the lowly born I
Such bosh I’ve never heard !” said he.
“Absurd!” said he — "Absurd!" said
he.
As for their pay, they’re quite content,
rhey’ve never asked an extra cent !
And in
The morn
They’ll mark
. Their scorn,
And show you they are free.”
And on the morrow Mr. Bane _
Called them together to “explain” :
“I have a small petition here-
out first, I wish to make it clear,”
Said he, with simple gravity
And suavity—and suavity,
“That no man here is asked to sign.”
(His voice was gentle and benign)
"I trust to your humanity
And sanity— and sanity
lo guide you ; but I feel quite sure
that Wages Boards you can’t endure.
1 leave it all to you,” said he.
It makes no difference to me.
My views
Are known,
But still,
v I’ve shown
*our choice in this is free.”
The staff it looked at Mr. Bane, _
And in his eye it read, quite plain,
Neath that expression so benign,
ihe fate of him who did not sign—
A vision of futurity—
' Obscurity—obscurity—
dearth of work—in short, the sack.
Abe y knew that he who answered back
Would earn, by his temerity,
<5 Severity— severity.
‘ o one and all, with shaky pen,
this refusal to be men....
But surely, as you must agree,
lheir choice was free as it could be.
They said
The Board
They all
Abhorred,
1 referring to be free.
Still Mr. Bane grows fat and sleek.
And still, at thirty bob a week,
His clerks slave on from morn till night,
No hope of better things in sight.
Lut Bane, with much benignity
And dignity—and dignity,
A hen talk of Wages Boards is heard,
Declares the notion is absurd :
“My clerks with prompt celerity
And verity—and verity
Refused the thing with one accord.
Ihe clerks themselves don't want the
Board!
Itis Preposterous,” says he,
To force it on who don’t agree!”
And still
His men
With brain
And pen
To fatten him are free.
VICTOKIA.
C. J. DENNIS.
Household Help Limited
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
AS we walked together towards the
dusty wharf, Bill Sunset explained
briefly how the unique idea had oc-
curred to him. “I always hated the job of
washing up,” said Bill. “I don’t much mind
any other sort of housework; but washing
up always beat me. I used to say to my
people that it was Chows’ work. Since the
Immigration Restriction Act, Chows have
become scarce; so, the servant girl being
practically extinct, I reckoned it would be
a paying investment if I could find a sub-
stitute for the Chow. A year or so back,
when I got home from that Java trip and
heard how scarce household help was, I
started thinking about an old German I
had chummed up with on the boat. I went
round and saw a few men who always have
some spare money, and the result was that
we formed the Household Help, Ltd. Yes-
terday our first consignment of house as-
sistants arrived by the Torres —that big.
yellow-funnelled boat at the end of the
pier. We are prepared to supply first-class
generals”
“What are they, Bill?” I inquired sus-
piciously.
“Apes,” replied Bill simply. “Wonder-
fully intelligent apes trained by old
Schmidt.”
“You’re joking, aren’t you?”
“Come along and see,” said Bill, loading
the way towards the steep gangway. A
number of well-dressed women were hover-
ing about in a lost-butterfly fashion. These
Bill pounced on and waved up the gangway
with a bushman’s bow. To me he whis-
pered, “Darling Pointers every one of them!
I had a big advertisement in this morning’s
paper. Come aboard. I’ll have to help
show them round.”
A fluttering calico sign at the top of the
gangway flaunted a red and white welcome
to inspect in the faces of all passers and
standers-by. Amongst the latter were a
number of staring immigrants from the big
ship on the other side of the pier.
Bill led the way to the deck and pointed
out several large cages, each of which con-
tained a vast, mild-eyed ape. “These chaps
are getting a breath of fresh air. Most of
them are down below. I don’t see old
Schmidt about. I guess he must be putting
one of them through his paces in the saloon.
Let’s go down.”
We descended the stairs and found the
big saloon thronged with Sydney’s Best
People. An ape, dressed in white drill coat
and pants, was coming and going with
glasses and trays of food at the bidding of
a stout, good-humored Teuton, who spoke
to it in a curious, guttural language.
“Gurr-rump!” Schmidt would growl, and
the silent, obedient ape would go hopping
away and return with a whisk broom and
crumb tray, with which it would tidy the
table.
“Luc-goo!” A plate would be taken from
the pile standing ready, carefully rinsed in
the dish of warm water, dried with a rubber
and placed in a rack.
While we waited, Schmidt, by merely
grunting, made his animal scrub, place
chairs for visitors, turn the electric light
on and off again, and perform other house-
hold duties. The well-dressed audience mur-
mured appreciatively at each fresh turn, and
awarded the trainer a round of applause at
the close of the demonstration, which he
indicated by commanding the animal to sit
down on a chair and fold its arms. Schmidt
beamed. “Now, ladies undt gentlemen, vot
is der use of paying for impudence undt
nights oudt? I take me dot monkey undt
I drain him to vork until vot you call dc
cows come home, undt all for nodings ! ot
can’t he do dot de servant you aind’t got
can, eh?”
“Can he answer the telephone?” squeaked
a thin woman with a monstrous hat.
Schmidt reddened. “Gott in Himmel! I
gannot drain him to all de tings ! He vill
answer allridt if your friendts speak to him
in his own languages !”
There was a general giggle, and the thin
woman subsided.
“Fifty poundts each.” announced Schmidt.
“Ven you haf paid dot, you don’t pay no
more! * Der average life of der apes is 15

PILES
A Man’s Terrible Martyrdom—Suffering Always—
Life was a Misery—Other Remedies Prove Useless.
One of the greatest living
physicians, referring to piles,
says The pain is often so
intense that the patient feels
he can neither walk nor lie,
while in some cases the loss
of blood is so great as to con-
stitute a grave danger
The way in which piles yield to Zam-Buk
is nothing short of remarkable. The con-
centrated and carefully combined ingredi-
ents of which Zam-Buk is composed take
away the inflammation; and the pain and irrita-
tion, which at times almost amounts to agony,
is relieved the moment the healing balm is
applied.
Confirmation of this statement is furnished by Mr. J.
Abood, of Albion Street, Warwick, Q., who says:—
“Three years ago I suffered all the agony incidental to
piles. At times I would pass nearly half a cupful of blood,
and became so distressed that life was perfect misery. I
suffered all the time when either standing or lying down. I
tried all sorts of ways and means in my endeavor to rid
myself of this miserable complaint, but the many so-called
remedies I used proved fruitless. At last I thought I would
give Zam-Buk a trial, and from the very first application of
the balm I derived relief. The burning inflammation
gradually subsided and the terrible pains were subdued,
and ten days after the first applications of Zam-Buk I was
completely cured of the piles. I feel convinced I had as bad
an attack of piles as any of my acquaintances, some of whom were
troubled for years, and they were astonished at the speedy cure Zam-
Buk had effected in my case.
“ I cannot speak too highly in favor of this wonderful balm, and
will never be without a pot. It is now three years since Zam-Buk
brought me ease and comfort, in place of torture and distress, and as I
have not had the slightest symptoms of piles since, I feel quite satis-
fied the cure is permanent.”
