year; mit goot food and kindness dey vill
last you 20.”
The audience sat spellbound for a mo-
ment. Then a hushed woman’s voice re-
marked, “What —-what about insects?”
“Dere ain’t none!” said Schmidt proudly.
“I haf de secret brocess. I treat all der
apes before dey goes out. I haf scared ail
der lleas undt oder vild animals oudt of
dem long ago. I gannot gif de trade secret;
but I vill gif half a crown for any troubles
you can find on him. Now you haf seen
vot he does. You haf mit him his book of
languages, so he knows vot you desire of lnm
to do. It takes you not so long to learn.
Mit some little patience you teach him vot
else you like. He is goot tempered and vill
not blay up unless you kick him too many.
If he do not hurry, you crack a toy whip—
so! Dat is enough for him! He is sen-
sible, and vill hustle himselves a move on.
I don’t know no servants what vill do so
much ! You feed him on vot you like ; but
nuts is for him de best. Now, ladies undt
gentlemens, you haf seen for yourselves vot
the ape he does when he is properly drained.
If you are all satisfied, I am brepared
to take orders.”
The audience struck up a chorus of ad-
miration, and a stately Society leader rustled
from her seat. “Please take my address, Mr.
Schmidt. lam just dying to give a> monkey
afternoon!” She slipped into one of the
revolving chairs and started to dredge her
card case from the gold-tasselled bag she car-
ried.
Another woman and her belltoppered hus-
band followed the lead, and the rush set in
without further delay. I left Bill Sunset
busily assisting Schmidt to book the names
and addresses of purchasers. At the shore
end of the gangway, I ran into a group of
sullen-looking immigrants from the big ship
opposite. One of them was declaiming pas-
sionately to his fellow-travellers: “I tell you
all that there steamer is full of flamin’ mon-
keys wot they’ve trained to work for ’em!
Yes, it’s th’ Gospel truth I’m tellin’ yer! I
seen a flamin’ German showin’ off one of
them in th’ cabin ! Wot sort o’ country is
this ’ere Australyer any’ow? I didn’t leave
Lunnon to compete with no flamin’ monkeys!
I ain’t goin’ to work with ’em and charnce
it! Wot do they take us for? D’ye think
I’m goin’ to ’ave me meals opposite a flamin’
nut-crackin’ gorilla? No flamin’ fear! ’
His stolid audience murmured sympatheti-
cally. I realised that the experiment which
Household Help, Ltd., represented was going
to cause a few complications.
One evening, about three weeks later, I
went to dine with the Bellevues. Old Belle-
vue, the great K.C., seemed to be especially
irritable about something ; but I didn’t sus-
pect what it was until Mrs. Bellevue hur-
ried in with a thin, leather-covered volume
in one hand, and a child’s toy whip in the
other. “Oh, how do you do?” she gushed.
“I’m so sorry dinner’s late ; but it’s all on
account of poor Thomas —our ape servant,
you know. Either he is stupid, or else I
haven’t learnt his language properly; some-
how he seems to be all at sixes and sevens
to-night. Just look here!” She opened the
thin volume, on the cover of which I noticed
the great name of Browning had been artis-
tically burnt, and pointed at a particular
line. “The command ‘Ugga-woosh’ is here
given as ‘Bring in the first course.’ Yet
every time I say it the wretched animal
takes the coal-scuttle and fills it! It’s dread-
fully annoying. I suppose you don’t happen
to know any monkey talk, do you?”
“Of course, he doesn’t!” growled the
master of the house. “He’s a sensible
man.”
“Now, Howard,” said his wife, “you know
very well that I have done my best to get
servants; and please remember that you said
last week you would not do any more wash-
ing up, and that I was to get anything at
all that would work. You are solely to
blame for our present helpless state, for you
discharged that poor Wagglin yourself; and
he promised to develop into quite a good
butler.”
Bellevue snorted. “That dirty scoundrel!
Why, I caught him handing my cigars and
whisky over the fence to his Domain
friends!”
“He only did it because they were black-
mailers, Howard ! They had threatened him
that they would tell you he had stolen ducks
from Government House. That was’ a cruel
lie, and you know it!”
“I don’t know it at all! My long experi-
ence of criminals inclines me to believe that
it was perfectly true.”
Mrs. Bellevue sighed hopelessly. “Well,
poor man, he is out of the house now
whether he was guilty or not.” She peered
closely at the book. “I wonder what it is I
should say to Thomas!” Then, with a little
cry of vexation. “Oh, how stupid of me! I
have been telling him the wrong thing all
the time! I should have said ‘Kulla-hoo’
to the poor thing! Excuse me for a moment,
won’t you !” She tripped out the door and
went down the hall cooing encouragingly.
“Kulla-hoo, Thomas! Kulla-hoo, there’s a
good boy!”
“Isn’t that damned nonsense?” demanded
Bellevue wildly. “Just like a woman to
spend fifty pounds on a walking flea-bag!
Why the devil do women do things like that,
eh?” Then he added gruffly, “Come out on
the verandah while the cursed thing brings
the food. I can hardly keep from kicking
the brute whenever it comes near me with
its hairy paws.”
We went through the open French win-
dows on to the shaded verandah, and from
our deck chairs watched the white-clad ape
hopping about, closely attended all the time
by Mrs. Bellevue, who read to it from the
Browning volume at intervals.
“All we want now,” remarked the old
K.C. in a loud voice, “is a barrel-organ. At
the present moment I feel degraded enough
to turn the handle myself.”
Mrs. Bellevue countered promptly. “Don’t
be absurd, Howard! The poor monkey is
doing beautifully. (Now set the chairs,
Thomas. Yes, the chairs, Boy. Wait a
minute !. .. . Aggle-uk, Thomas ! Yes,
Aggle-uk, there’s a dear!) Now, Howard,
see how promptly he obeys! Why, he’s
almost as good as poor Wagglin !”
At these words a ragged object upended
itself at the verandah steps and hooted
angrily. “Got a monkey in me place!” it
shouted. “ I’ve seen yer rotten monkey!”
Mrs. Bellevue paled and dropped her re-
vised edition of Browning. The ape fumbled
foolishly with the furniture. Old Bellevue
leaped clean out of his chair. “Who’s
that?” he demanded, peering short-sightedly
into the gloom.
“It’s me!” shouted the shadow defiantly.
“It’s me —Wagglin! I ’eard you ’ad a rot-
ten monkey in me old place; but I wouldn’t
bleeve it, so I come along to see for meself.
I’ll '’ave th’ lor of you, Mr. Bellevue, for
puttin’ a dirty monkey in my plaqe! That
ain’t British justice, Mr. Bellevue, to go an’
employ dirty monkeys! I’ll ’ave th’ lor of
you an’ soo you for ’eavy damages!”
Bellevue pointed a shaking forefinger at
the maddened intruder. “Didn’t I warn you
never to come here again?”
“Yes, yer did ! But I’m goin’ to stick up
for me rights! I’m a pore man, Mr. Belle-
vue, an’ certain persons ’ave done their best
to rooin me character; but I ain’t goin’ to be
put down be enyone. I’m goin’ to get some
British justice, I am! Nobody won’t go
stickin’ no monkeys in my place while I’m
about! I’m a ’uman bein’, 1 am, an’ I de-
mand to be treated as one! I arsk you now,
wot right ’ave you got to go an’ put a mon-
key in me place?”
“I only got another monkey!” shouted
Bellevue furiously.
“Oh, Howard ! How cruel you are !” Mrs.
Bellevue rustled out to the verandah and
leant over the railing. “Now Wagglin, you
mustn’t be angry with Mr. Bellevue, or he
will be cross with you ! Calm yourself like
a sensible man. We meant no harm, you
know !”
“Well, I’m damned!” breathed the K.C.
“I’m sorry, Mrs. Bellevue, mum ! I didn’t
mean to be ’arsh.” Wagglin, the shadowy,
face to face with his patroness, grudgingly
took up a penitent attitude and commenced
to whine. “But you know, Mrs. Bellevue,
it don’t seem right for a dirty monkey to be
put in the place of a ’uman bein’—specially
wen that ’uman bein’ ’as done ’is best to
please. W’y did you go an’ put a monkey
in me place, Mrs. Bellevue?”
The lady of the house fluttered unhappily.
“Oh, because we couldn’t get anyone to do
the work, Wagglin.”
“But I was about, mum! You could ’ave
got me! Send th’ monkey away, Mrs.
Bellevue, an’ take me back!”
The master of the house grunted wrath -
fully in the shadow. The mistress wrung
her white hands at the suppliant outcast.
“Oh, Wagglin, we really couldn’t, you
know! Then we’ve spent a whole fifty
pounds on Thomas —and he’s such a faith-
ful old thing!”
At this Wagglin dashed his ragged hat on
the ground. “Bought a monkey!” he
screamed. “You’ve bought a stinkin’ mon-
key and put it in me place! This ain’t
British justice! It’s just slavery faked up
to save me wages!”
“Clear out!” commanded Bellevue explo-
sively.
“I won’t clear out! I’m goin’ to get a
bit of me own back. You just watch and
see me fix your stinkin’ monkey!” Before
anyone could move, he had run up the steps,
bounded into the lighted room and grappled
with the astonished ape.
Mrs. Bellevue screamed shrilly, and the
ape evidently mistook the noise for the com-
mand to sweep up the leavings. Probably
she had hit on the correct scream by acci-
dent. Disengaging himself with a swift
wriggle, he whipped up the massive wooden
crumb tray and broom, and made frantic
efforts to sweep Wagglin on to it. There
was a terrific scramble, in the midst of
which Wagglin fell with a dull thud. Be-
fore he could rise, one hairy arm had rolled
him on to the platter and he was hoisted
into the air and rushed kicking from the
room. His yells diminished as he was borne
swiftly and irresistibly down the long, dimly-
lit hall. Followed faint sounds of a desper-
ate scuffle in the yard, a metallic rattle and
a final cymbal-like crash. Almost immedi-
ately we heard the rush of returning foot-
steps, and Wagglin, white-faced and decor-
ated with scraps of rubbish, ran howling out
the hall entrance. “’E chucked me in th’
dustbox! I’ll ’ave th’ lor of you for this!
’E took an’ chucked me in th’ !”
A white-clad figure dashed out like a gfeat
spider and whipped him off again.
“Come back!” screamed Mrs. Bellevue.
But the command was in good English, and
the ape did not stop. Within five seconds
we heard the lid of the dustbox clash again.
“I hope to Heaven he isn’t injured !” mut-
tered Bellevue anxiously. As he spoke, the
scurry of approaching feet became louder.
“They’re coming back again! Get the
book QUICK !”
I dashed for the book. At the same in-
stant Wagglin reached the verandah for the
second time. “ ’E’s murderin’ me! ’E ’it
me on the ’ead with th’ lid! If yer don’t
stop ’im I’ll go straight”——
“LOOK OUT. YOU FOOL!” screamed
the K.C. “DON’T TALK ! RUN !”
But Wagglin was jerked back into dark-
ness as he screamed.
By this time I had retrieved the Brown-
ing book, and was fumbling with the pages.
At the warning crash of the dustbox lid I
had discovered the monkey word for “Stop-
sit down.” As they began the home rush
for the third time I cried. “Here it is! Say
it all altogether—‘GUK-WOOF.’”
When Waerglin shot out the front door
for the third time, we all yelled together,
“GUK-WOO-OF!” The white cyclone
stopped dead in its tracks, hobbled meekly
into the dining-room, and, sitting down on
a chair, folded its arms.
Wagglin who was crouched abjectly by
the railings, looked up in response to our
earnest assurances that he was safe, and
saw his tormentor sitting silent and erect
like a wooden footman. “W’y did ’e do it?”
he whispered brokenly. “ ’Ow was It’ know
th’ monkey gent understood wot I was say-
in’? I wouldn’t ’ave spoke disrespectful if
I’d known ’e was listenin’! I thought ’e
was a —l thought ’e was just a monkey!....
Tell ’im. if yer please, that I didn’t know!
Reely, I didn’t!” His voice trailed miser-
ably away, and he sat holding his bruised
head.
Bellevue dived into his pocket and pulled
out a coin. “Here! Here’s something for —
something for your trouble! Now go away
like a good fellow and clean yourself up a
bit.”
The wretched Wagglin, crowned disgrace-
fully with rubbish, accepted the sovereign
gratefully. Pic-kina: himself up by degrees,
he bashfully twiddled his ragged hat and
glanced nervously at the proud monkey in
charge. Then, jerking his grimy thumb in
the brute’s direction, he mumbled humbly:
“Mr. Bellevue, sir. would yer mind if I was
ter arsk ’im—th’ monkey gent that is—if I
was ter arsk ’im ter ’ave a drink w ;fV > mp?”
